Refuting an Objection to the Proof of Prophethood via Miracle
Sanusi refutes a contemporary objection against the proof of prophethood, addressing circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a circularity objection against the proof of prophethood is raised, the two proposed answers (rational indication and concurrent circularity), and why the first answer fails because the miracle's divine origin depends on prior knowledge of God's oneness.

Core Idea
The proof of prophethood through miracles cannot be separated from the proof of God's oneness.

This lesson presents a specific objection to the classic proof of prophethood—that it involves a circular dependency between knowing God as sender and knowing the messenger. Sanusi reports a contemporary's two attempted defenses and then refutes the first. You will see why the miracle's role as a divine act makes prior knowledge of tawhid essential.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the literal translation. The objection is introduced at the beginning: if the messenger is known only through the sender, and the sender only through the messenger, a circularity (دور) results.
Notice the dramatic tone of the objection: 'So how is His messenger known from Him?!'
2
Identify the two proposed answers
The contemporary scholar offers two answers. The first claims the miracle's indication is purely rational, so the implied conclusion cannot be separated. The second argues that the miracle can prove both truthfulness and oneness together, yielding a permissible 'circularity of concomitance' (دور معية).
The second answer concedes dependence on oneness but tries to make the circle acceptable.
3
Understand the refutation of the first answer
Sanusi refutes by pointing out that the miracle being an act of God is a necessary pillar of the proof. Since knowing that requires prior knowledge of God's oneness (no partner), the first answer fails. The miracle's rational indication is not free-standing; it depends on tawhid.
The refutation of the second answer is cut off in the source; we only have the refutation of the first here.
4
Reflect on the lesson's incompleteness
Be aware that the passage ends mid-sentence, so the full refutation of the second answer is not available in this lesson. The author likely continued to refute the 'circularity of concomitance' response.
This is a known limitation due to page cut-off, noted in the source review.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكيف يُعرَفُ منهُ رسولُهُ ؟! وقد عرفت أنَّ الرسولَ لم يُعرَفْ إِلا مِنْ قِبَلِ مرسله المعلوم بخلْقِ أفعال على صفةٍ مخصوصة تدلُّ على ذلك ، فإذا كانَ المرسل مجهولاً إِنَّما يُعرَفُ مِنْ قِبَلِ الرسول . . لزم الدور ضرورةً . وقد اعترض بعضُ المعاصرين في شرح له على العقيدة المنسوبة لابن الحاجب هذهِ الحُجَّةَ التي اعتمدها شرفُ الدين بن التلمساني ، وأشرنا إليها في عقيدتنا . . بما نصه بعد حكاية إيرادِ شرف الدين : ( قد يُقالُ في جوابِهِ : إِنَّ دلالة الخارقِ على صِدْقِ مَنْ تَحدَّى بِهِ عقليَّةٌ على أحد القولين ، وإذا كانَتْ عقليَّةً فلا يصح تخلُّفُ المدلول عنها ، وإلا انقلب الدليل شبهةً . أو نقولُ : سلَّمْنا توقُفَهُ على ثبوتِ الوحدانية ، للكنْ لِمَ لا يكونُ ظهورُ الخارقِ دليلاً على الصدْقِ وعلى ثبوتِ الوحدانية معاً ؟! فالدورُ اللازم غيرُ ممتنع لأنَّه دورُ معيَّة ، والبرهانُ إنَّما قام على استحالة دور التقدم ) انتهى

قلتُ : ولا يخفى ضَعْفُ جوابيه معاً في غاية :
أَمَّا الأَوَّلُ - وهو التمسُّكُ بقولِ الأستاذ في أنَّ دلالة المعجزة عقلية - : فلا يتم له ذلك إلا لو لم يكن كون الخارق فعلاً لله تعالى ركناً مِنَ الدليل ، أما إذا كانَ ركناً فيهِ ، وهو لا يتحقَّقُ إلا بمعرفة الوحدانية . . لم يصح ما ذكر ، وظاهر أنَّه ركن على كل قول في وجهِ دلالة المعجزة ؛ إذ معنى كون دلالتِها عقليةً عندَ مَنْ قالَ : أنَّ به خلْقَ اللهِ تعالى الخارقَ على وَفْقِ دعواه وتحديَهُ معَ العجز عن معارضته ، وتخصيصه بذلك .. يدلُّ على إرادة الله تعالى لتصديقه كما يدلُّ اختصاص الفعلِ بالوقتِ المعيَّنِ والمحل على إرادتِهِ تعالى لذلكَ بالضرورة ، هكذا قرَّرَهُ الأئمَّةُ رضي الله عنهم على ما يأتي فيهِ مِنَ البحث في موضعِهِ إِنْ شاءَ الله تعالى

فأنت ترى كيف جعلوا خلْقَ اللهِ تعالى للفعل جزءاً مِنَ الدليل وذلك لا يتم إلا بمعرفة الوحدانية له جلَّ وعلا ، ونفي أنْ يكونَ مَعَهُ شريك في ملكه ، وهو ظاهرُ ؛ لأنَّهُ لو جُوِّزَ أَنَّ ثَمَّ مَنْ يَشَارِكُهُ تعالى في اختراع الكائناتِ .. لم يتحقق حينئذ كونُ الخارقِ فعلهُ تعالى حتى يدلنا أنَّهُ تعالى أراد بإيجاده تصديق نبيه ، ولهذا قال الإمامُ الفخرُ في المعالم : ( إنَّ المنكرينَ للنبوَّةِ طعنوا في المعجزة مِنْ ثلاثة أوجه : أحدها : قالوا : لِمَ قلتُم : إِنَّ هذهِ المعجزاتِ فعلُ الله تعالى وخلقه ؟! ، فانظر إلى الطعن بهذا الوجه كيف هو صريح في أنَّ )
So how is His messenger known from Him?! You have known that the messenger is not known except by way of his sender, who is known by creating acts upon a specific characteristic that indicate that. So if the sender is unknown, only to be known from the messenger, then circularity necessarily follows. And a contemporary objected in his commentary on the creed attributed to Ibn al-Hajib against this argument which Sharaf al-Din ibn al-Tilmisani relied upon, and we alluded to it in our creed, with his text after narrating Sharaf al-Din's objection: (It may be said in answer to him: The indication of the miraculous (al-khariq) for the truthfulness of the one who challenges with it is rational on one of the two opinions, and if it is rational, then the indicated cannot be separated from it, otherwise the proof would turn into an equivocation. Or we say: We concede its dependence on establishing oneness, but why should the appearance of the miraculous not be a proof for truthfulness and for establishing oneness together?! So the necessary circularity is not impossible because it is a circularity of concomitance, and the proof is only based on the impossibility of circularity of priority.) End.

I say: The weakness of both of his answers is not hidden at all:
As for the first—which is clinging to the master's statement that the indication of the miracle is rational—: it is not valid for him except if the miraculous being an act of God Most High were not a pillar of the proof. But if it is a pillar in it, and it is not realized except by knowledge of oneness, then what he mentioned is not correct, and it is apparent that it is a pillar on every opinion regarding the aspect of the indication of the miracle; for the meaning of its indication being rational according to those who said: that by it is God's creation of the miraculous in accordance with his claim and his challenge along with inability to oppose it, and its specification with that, indicates the will of God to verify him, just as the specification of an action to a particular time and place indicates His will for that by necessity. Thus the imams, may God be pleased with them, decided it, as the discussion about it will come in its place, God willing.

So you see how they made God's creation of the action a part of the proof, and that is not completed except by knowledge of oneness for Him, may He be glorified and exalted, and the negation that there is a partner with Him in His kingdom, and it is apparent; because if it were allowed that there is someone who shares with Him in the invention of beings, then it would not be realized at that time that the miraculous is His act, until it indicates to us that He intended by its existence the verification of His prophet. For this reason, Imam al-Fakhr said in al-Ma'alim: (Indeed the deniers of prophethood attacked the miracle from three aspects: one of them: they said: Why do you say that these miracles are the act of God and His creation?! So look at the attack in this aspect, how it is explicit in that...)
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer and refutation. It begins by stating the problem: how can a messenger be known if knowing the messenger requires knowing the sender (God), but knowing God as sender depends on the miracle which is performed by the messenger? This creates a circularity (دور). The author reports that a contemporary scholar objected by proposing two answers. First answer: the miracle's indication is rational, and when it is rational, the implied conclusion cannot be separated from it, so no circularity. Second answer: concede that the miracle's proof depends on oneness, but the miracle itself can prove both the truthfulness of the prophet and the oneness of God simultaneously, resulting in a 'circularity of concomitance' (دور معية) which is not impossible. The author then refutes the first answer: the miracle's being an act of God is a necessary pillar of the proof. Without knowing that God is one and has no partner, we cannot be sure the miraculous act is from God. Therefore, knowledge of oneness is prior. The second answer is not directly refuted here; instead, the author further explains that the miracle's proof inherently includes the creation of the action by God, which requires prior knowledge of oneness. The passage ends by citing Imam al-Razi to show that even the deniers of prophethood attacked the miracle by questioning whether it is indeed God's act, confirming that the divine origin is a crucial part of the proof.

Key Terms
دلالة المعجزة
دور
وحدانية
عقلية
خارق
دور معية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The miracle's indication is purely rational and does not depend on knowing God's oneness beforehand.
Correction: The miracle's proof inherently requires that the act be from God, which presupposes God's oneness, as Sanusi explains.

Checkpoint
According to the author's refutation, what is the main flaw in the first answer that claims the miracle's indication is purely rational?
A) Rational indications cannot prove prophethood.
B) The miraculous act's being from God is a necessary pillar of the proof, and that requires prior knowledge of oneness.
C) The miracle only proves prophethood, not oneness.
D) The circularity is unavoidable.

Key Takeaway
The first answer fails because the miracle's divine origin—a necessary pillar of the proof—cannot be known without prior affirmation of God's oneness.

The Dependence of Prophethood on Divine Unity and the Definition of Miracle
Sanusi argues the necessity of tawhid for prophethood and defines miracle as an act of Allah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why proving prophethood requires prior knowledge of tawhid (divine unity) and how the miracle's definition includes the condition that it is an act of Allah alone.

Core Idea
The miracle's proof depends on tawhid; without tawhid, prophethood cannot be established.

Sanusi presents a logical argument that the validity of prophethood hinges on the prior acceptance of tawhid. He shows that the miracle, which is the primary evidence for prophethood, only serves as proof if we recognize that the extraordinary event is an act of Allah—a truth that requires belief in divine unity. He then quotes al-Muqtarīh to clarify the definition of a miracle and points out a common error among those who omit the divine agency condition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument.
Pay attention to the logical connectors like 'so it is necessary'.
2
Identify the two pillars of a miracle
Notice that the passage states a miracle requires both being extraordinary and being an act of Allah. The second pillar is emphasized as the source of error.
The quotation from Sharh al-Irshad elaborates on these pillars.
3
Trace the logical dependency
Follow the chain: knowledge of prophethood depends on miracle's indication → miracle's indication depends on knowing it is an act of Allah → knowing that depends on tawhid.
This is the core argument for the necessity of tawhid.
4
Understand the common error
The passage ends by pointing out that defining a miracle only as extraordinary without specifying it is from Allah is a mistake that undermines the proof.
The error is making part of the evidence (the extraordinary nature) an independent proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كون الخارق فعلا لله جل وعز ركن في دلالة المعجزة ، وإثباته متوقف على معرفة الوحدانية ، فوجب توقف معرفة النبوة على الوحدانية ضرورة توقفها على دلالة المعجزة المتوقفة عليها
وقد صرح المقترح في ( شرح الإرشاد ) بأن كون الفعل مخلوقا الله تعالى ركن في المعجزة ؛ لأنه قال في فصل النبوات : ( وما أوتي أحد من منكري النبوات في جحد دلالة المعجزات إلا من وجه الجهل بأركانها ، فقد يجهل أن الخارق للعادة فعل الله تعالى ((1) .
ثم قال : ( وقد يعتقد أنه ليس خارقا للعادة ، وأنه مما يجوز التوصل إليه بالحيل والغوص في العلوم ، فأما من هدي لمسلك الحق ، وعرف أن الذي وقع به التحدي فعل الله تعالى ، وهو عالم بدعوى المتحدي ، وأنه لا يتوصل إليه بالحيل ، وأنه خارق للعادة ، فعله الله تعالى على وفق دعوى النبي إجابة له . . لم يسترب في حصول العلم ، ولا يختص بصورة ، ولا يفتقر في دلالته إلى مثال يضرب في الشاهد ( انتهى وبالجملة : فمثار الغلط في جواب صاحبنا عن إيراد شرف الدين . . جعل بعض الدليل على الانفراد دليلا مستقلا (3)
(3) فجعل المعجزة : الأمر الخارق للعادة المقارن لدعوى التحدي ، ولم يضم لذلك كون ذلك فعلا الله دون غيره ، وهذا غلط . « حامدي » ( ص328 )
The fact that the extraordinary [event] is an act of Allah, may He be glorified and exalted, is a pillar in the indication of the miracle, and proving it depends on knowledge of divine unity. So it is necessary that knowledge of prophethood depends on divine unity, necessarily because it depends on the indication of the miracle, which depends on it. And the author (al-Muqtarīh) explicitly stated in (Sharh al-Irshad) that the act being created by Allah Almighty is a pillar in the miracle; because he said in the chapter on prophethoods: 'None of the deniers of prophethoods came to reject the indication of miracles except from the aspect of ignorance of its pillars. For one may be ignorant that the extraordinary is an act of Allah. Then he said: And one may believe that it is not extraordinary, and that it is possible to achieve it through tricks and deep research in sciences. But as for one who is guided to the path of truth, and knows that what occurred as a challenge is an act of Allah, and he knows the claim of the challenger, and that it cannot be achieved through tricks, and that it is extraordinary, done by Allah in accordance with the claim of the prophet in response to him... he does not doubt in obtaining knowledge, nor is it specific to any form, nor does its indication need an example given in the witnessed world. End. In summary: the source of error in our colleague's response to Sharaf al-Din's objection... is making a part of the evidence an independent evidence. (3) He defined the miracle as: the extraordinary event accompanied by a claim of challenge, and he did not include in that that it is an act of Allah exclusively, and this is an error. — Hamidi (p. 328)
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage is a two-part argument: first, a logical proof that knowledge of tawhid must precede knowledge of prophethood; second, a quotation from Sharh al-Irshad defining the miracle and identifying a common error. The author begins by stating that the extraordinary event (al-khāriq) being an act of Allah is essential for the miracle's evidential value. Proving that it is an act of Allah requires knowledge of tawhid, so proving prophethood depends on tawhid. Then he quotes al-Muqtarīh from Sharh al-Irshad, who explains that rejecting miracles stems from ignorance of their two pillars: that the event is an act of Allah and that it is extraordinary. The quote clarifies that when a person understands these, the miracle's proof becomes certain. After the quote, the author summarizes the error in his colleague's response: he took only part of the evidence (the extraordinary event without requiring it to be an act of Allah) and treated it as the whole evidence, making it an independent proof. The footnote (3) by Hamidi restates that the miracle must include the act being from Allah alone, otherwise it is an error.

Key Terms
الخارق للعادة
المعجزة
الوحدانية
النبوة
دلالة
أركان المعجزة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that a miracle is simply any extraordinary event, regardless of its source.
Correction: The passage teaches that a miracle must be an act of Allah alone; without that, it cannot serve as proof for prophethood.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does knowledge of prophethood depend on knowledge of divine unity?
A) Because prophethood is a necessary attribute of God
B) Because the miracle's indication depends on knowing it is an act of Allah, which requires tawhid
C) Because all prophets taught tawhid
D) Because the Qur'an commands belief in both

Key Takeaway
Proving prophethood requires first establishing tawhid, because the miracle's validity as proof depends on knowing it is an act of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever attributed an extraordinary event in your life to luck, skill, or natural causes without pausing to see the hand of Allah? This lesson teaches that recognizing miracles requires first acknowledging that Allah alone is the doer of all things. Do you truly live with that awareness, or do you still allow the world to veil His agency from your heart?
?
The error Sanusi warns against is reducing a miracle to mere extraordinariness. In your own faith, have you ever accepted a sign from Allah only because it seemed impressive, without grounding it in tawhid? What would change if you conditioned every supernatural claim on the foundation of divine unity?
?
If you cannot prove that Allah is the exclusive actor behind an extraordinary event, then prophethood itself remains unproven. Does your conviction in the Prophet’s ﷺ miracles rest on this solid foundation, or have you never thought about how tawhid underpins your belief? Take a moment to examine: do you truly know that the Qur’an, the greatest miracle, is from Allah alone—or could your certainty be shaken by a doubt about His exclusive power?

Moses' Request as Proof for Ru'ya
Sanusi argues from Moses asking to see Allah, proving ru'ya is possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Moses' request to see Allah serves as a transmitted proof that ru'ya is possible.

Core Idea
Prophets don't ask for the impossible, so ru'ya must be possible.

Sanusi now presents the second transmitted proof: the story of Moses asking Allah to show Himself. The reasoning is direct: Moses, as a prophet, knew what is impossible for Allah; he would not ask for it. Therefore, ru'ya must be a possible occurrence. This argument defends the Sunni position against those who deny vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage slowly. Notice the structure: it states the proof, explains the premises, and concludes with prophetic infallibility.
The word 'حُثالة' refers to the least intelligent of the Mu'tazila.
2
Identify the key premises
The argument rests on two premises: (1) Moses knew what is impossible for Allah, and (2) prophets are infallible (ma'sum). Trace how each is used.
Premise (1) is stated directly; premise (2) is invoked at the end.
3
Understand the inference
Since Moses is infallible and knows the impossible, he would not ask for the impossible. His request was for something possible. Therefore, ru'ya is possible.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: if ru'ya were impossible, Moses would have asked for the impossible, contradicting his infallibility.
4
Reflect on the refutation of the Mu'tazila
Sanusi even says that the dregs of the Mu'tazila recognize the impossibility; so if Moses had asked for the impossible, he would be worse than them. This strengthens the claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومِنَ الأدلَّةِ السمعيّةِ : سؤال موسى عليهِ السلامُ للرؤيةِ ؛ إِذْ معلوم أنَّهُ لا يجهلُ ما يستحيلُ في حقّهِ تعالى ، وإلا كان جاهلاً بما أدركَتِ استحالته حُثالة المعتزلة ، فتعيَّنَ أَنَّهُ ما سألَ إلا ما هو جائز ؛ إِذْ سؤال ما يستحيلُ ممنوع، والأنبياء معصومونَ مِنْ كُلِّ ذلل على ما يأتي تحقيقه إن شاء الله تعالى .
And among the transmitted proofs: the request of Moses, peace be upon him, for ru'ya (vision); for it is known that he does not ignore what is impossible with respect to Him, Exalted. Otherwise he would be ignorant of what even the dregs of the Mu'tazila have realized to be impossible. So it is determined that he only asked for what is possible; for asking for what is impossible is prohibited, and the prophets are protected from every defect, as its verification will come, God willing.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage presents the second transmitted proof: the story of Moses asking Allah for the vision (ru'ya) as recorded in the Quran. The reasoning is that Moses, being a prophet, knew what is impossible for Allah. Therefore he would not ask for something impossible. If he did, he would be ignorant—even worse than the least intelligent Mu'tazilites who recognize the impossibility. Since prophets are infallible (ma'sum), his request must have been for something possible. Hence ru'ya is possible for Allah.

Key Terms
موسى
عصمة
سؤال
رؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moses asked for ru'ya even though it is impossible because he was overwhelmed by desire or ignorance.
Correction: Prophets are infallible and fully aware of Allah's transcendence; they do not ask for the impossible. Moses' request proves ru'ya is possible.

Checkpoint
Why does Moses' request prove ru'ya is possible?
A) Because Moses was a great prophet and his request was granted.
B) Because prophets, being sinless and knowledgeable, would not ask for the impossible.
C) Because the Mu'tazila also asked for it.
D) Because the request is recorded in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Moses' request for ru'ya demonstrates that seeing Allah is possible, because prophets would not ask for what is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Moses dared to ask Allah for the vision of His face. Do you long to see Allah with the same intensity, or have you become content with knowing Him only through descriptions? What would change in your daily worship if you truly believed you will see Him?
?
If the dregs of the Mu'tazila recognize what is impossible for Allah, how much more should you? Examine your du'a: have you ever asked Allah for something that implies an impossibility about Him (like seeing Him in this world)? Repent and refine your understanding.
?
The proof hinges on Moses' infallibility. You are not a prophet, but you claim to follow them. In what area of your life are you making excuses for ignorance about Allah's attributes, rather than striving to know Him as the prophets did? Commit to studying one attribute this week until it transforms your heart.

Proof from Moses Asking for Vision
Sanusi presents a scriptural proof from Moses' request for vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Moses' request for vision serves as a scriptural proof for the possibility of seeing God, and why this argument relies on prophetic infallibility.

Core Idea
Moses' request proves vision is possible because a prophet would never ask for the impossible.

Sanusi presents a key scriptural argument from the story of Moses (Quran 7:143). The argument hinges on the prophet's knowledge and infallibility. Since Moses knew what is impossible for God, he would only ask for something possible, thereby establishing that seeing God is possible. This lesson unpacks the logic and addresses a common misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the concise argumentative style.
Pay attention to the reference to the Mu'tazila.
2
Identify the logical steps
Break down the argument: (1) Moses is a prophet and knows what is impossible for God. (2) He would not ask for the impossible because prophets are infallible. (3) Therefore, his request was for something possible, proving vision is possible.
The author uses an indirect proof by eliminating the impossible.
3
Reflect on the misconception
Consider why some might think Moses erred. The author counters that even the least knowledgeable Mu'tazilites know what is impossible, so a prophet certainly would not be ignorant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومِنَ الأدلَّةِ السمعيّةِ : سؤال موسى عليهِ السلامُ للرؤيةِ ؛ إِذْ معلوم أنَّهُ لا يجهلُ ما يستحيلُ في حقّهِ تعالى ، وإلا كان جاهلاً بما أدركَتِ استحالته حُثالة المعتزلة ، فتعيَّنَ أَنَّهُ ما سألَ إلا ما هو جائز ؛ إِذْ سؤال ما يستحيلُ ممنوع، والأنبياء معصومونَ مِنْ كُلِّ ذلل على ما يأتي تحقيقه إن شاء الله تعالى.
And among the scriptural proofs is the request of Moses, peace be upon him, for vision; since it is known that he is not ignorant of what is impossible with respect to Him — otherwise he would be ignorant of what even the rabble of the Mu'tazila have grasped as impossible. So it is determined that he only asked for what is permissible, for asking for the impossible is forbidden, and the prophets are protected from every flaw, as its verification will come, God willing.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage presents a scriptural proof for the possibility of seeing God: the story of Moses asking God for vision (Quran 7:143). The argument is that Moses was a prophet and thus knew what is impossible for God. If he had asked for something impossible, he would be ignorant of something even the lowliest Mu'tazilites know — namely, that vision requires direction and body, which are impossible for God. Since prophets are infallible, Moses would not commit such an error. Therefore, he only asked for what is possible, proving that vision of God is possible. The author notes that prophets are protected from all flaws, a point to be detailed later.

Key Terms
سؤال موسى
الرؤية
الأنبياء معصومون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Moses' request was a sin or showed ignorance.
Correction: The author argues that prophets are infallible and knowledgeable, so the request was for something possible, proving vision is possible.

Checkpoint

A) Because Moses was a strong prophet.
B) Because Moses would not ask for the impossible due to his infallibility and knowledge.
C) Because the Mu'tazila said it is impossible.
D) Because the verse explicitly says so.

Key Takeaway
Moses' request for vision demonstrates that seeing God is possible, because a prophet would not ask for the impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make du'a, do you ever pause to consider whether what you're asking is befitting of Allah's majesty and perfection? Moses knew what was impossible for God, so he asked only for what was permissible. How aware are you of Allah's attributes when you call upon Him?
?
The proof shows that even the greatest prophet's request teaches us about God's nature. How does this reshape your view of the stories of the prophets? Are you reading them solely as narratives, or as theological evidence that demands a response from you?
?
The source declares that prophets are protected from every flaw. If you truly believe this, how should it affect your trust in every word and action of the Prophet Muhammad ﷺ? Is there any area of your life where you still hesitate to submit completely to the sunnah?

The Prophet's Vision Refutes Rays and Obstacles
Sanusi refutes rays and obstacles using the Prophet's vision of Paradise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Prophet's vision of Paradise demonstrates that vision is not dependent on rays or obstacles, thereby refuting a key objection.

Core Idea
The Prophet's vision of Paradise proves vision is not limited by physical conditions.

Sanusi presents a powerful argument from the Prophet's own experience. If vision required rays and no obstacles, then seeing something extremely far and behind many veils would be impossible. Yet the Prophet saw Paradise from his place. This direct evidence overturns the rationalist conditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its flow, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the key terms: rays, obstacles, veils.
Notice how Sanusi uses the Prophet's miracle as a concrete counterexample.
2
Identify the two elements being refuted
The passage directly refutes the conditions of 'rays' (ash'aa') and 'obstacles' (mawani'). Find where each is mentioned and how the argument targets them.
3
Understand the logical structure
The argument works by conditional reasoning: if vision were by rays, they could not reach that distance; if veils are obstacles, they would block. Since the Prophet saw Paradise despite these, the conditions are false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum from the opponents' own framework.
4
Connect to opponents' own admissions
Sanusi notes that the opponents themselves list extreme distance and dense veils as obstacles. This strengthens the refutation: their own criteria are contradicted by the Prophet's vision.
5
Conclude the refutation
Reflect on why this argument is decisive: it uses an attested prophetic miracle to directly invalidate a theoretical condition. This shows the priority of revealed experience over human speculation.
Think about how this fits into the broader debate on the beatific vision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً : فما ثبتَ مِنْ رؤية النبي صلى الله عليهِ وسلَّمَ الجنةَ مِنْ موضعِهِ معَ غاية البعدِ وكثافةِ الحُجُبِ الكثيرة. . يمنعُ ما تخيَّلوهُ مِنَ الأشعة والموانع ومما يبطل أيضاً قولهم : رؤية النبيِّ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ الجنَّةَ مِنْ موضعِهِ معَ غايةِ بُعدِها ، وكثافةِ الحُجُبِ التي بينه وبينَها ، فلو كانَتِ الرؤية بانبعاث الأشعَةِ لَمَا وصلَتْ معَ هذا البعد العظيم ، وأيضاً : فالحُجُبُ الكثيفةُ تردُّها ، لا سيّما وهم قد قرَّروا أَنَّ مِنَ الموانع القربَ والبعد المفرطين ، ووجود حجاب كثيف بين الرائي والمرئي
And also, what is established of the Prophet's (peace be upon him) vision of Paradise from his place with extreme distance and the density of many veils prevents what they imagined of rays and obstacles. And among what also invalidates their statement: the Prophet's vision of Paradise from his place with extreme distance and the density of the veils between him and it, for if vision were by the emission of rays, they would not reach with this great distance, and also, the dense veils would repel them, especially since they have established that among the obstacles are extreme closeness and extreme distance, and the presence of a dense veil between the seer and the seen.
Source pages p. 518

Commentary
This passage presents a second refutation based on the Prophet's experience. Sanusi argues that the fact that the Prophet saw Paradise from his place, despite the extreme distance and many dense veils, disproves their theory that vision requires rays (ash'aa') and that obstacles (mawani') such as distance and veils prevent vision. He elaborates: if vision occurred by the emission of rays from the eye, then in the case of the Prophet's vision of Paradise, the rays would not reach that great distance, and also the dense veils would block them. Moreover, the opponents themselves have stated that among the obstacles are extreme proximity and extreme distance, as well as the presence of a dense veil. Yet the Prophet saw Paradise despite all that. Therefore, their conditions are invalid.

Key Terms
الرؤية (ru'ya/vision)
الأشعة (ash'aa'/rays)
الموانع (mawani'/obstacles)
الحجب (hujub/veils)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that physical distance and veils always prevent vision, but the Prophet's miracle shows otherwise.
Correction: The Prophet's vision of Paradise demonstrates that vision can occur without satisfying the conditions of rays and obstacles, as it is a miraculous event.

Checkpoint
What does the Prophet's vision of Paradise demonstrate according to Sanusi?
A) That rays can travel through veils
B) That vision is not dependent on rays and obstacles
C) That Paradise is close to the Prophet
D) That the Prophet had a special eye structure

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's vision of Paradise is a concrete miracle that undermines the rationalist conditions for vision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a miracle or a divine command because it didn't fit your rational expectations? What would it take for you to submit your logic to revelation in that area?
?
When you read that the Prophet saw Paradise despite extreme distance and veils, do you realize that Allah's power is not limited by your understanding of physics? How can you remind yourself of this daily?
?
If you truly believed that Allah can override all natural obstacles, how would that change the way you pray for the impossible things in your life right now?

Introduction to Prophethood: Necessity and Linguistic Definition
Sanusi introduces prophethood, outlines three issues, begins defining nubuwwa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why belief in messengers is obligatory and how the word 'nubuwwa' is derived linguistically.

Core Idea
Reason alone cannot know right from wrong; we need prophets to convey God's commands.

Sanusi now turns from the divine attributes to prophethood. He first establishes that sending messengers is a contingent possibility that God does, but requires belief, because without revelation, the intellect cannot determine moral duties. He then outlines three key issues and begins with the linguistic analysis of 'nubuwwa'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the transition from divine matters to prophethood.
2
Understand the rationale for messengers
Sanusi states that sending messengers is contingent (jā'iz) and obligatory to believe. The rationale is that reason alone cannot know obedience, disobedience, or indifferent actions without divine law.
3
Note the three issues
The discourse on prophethood is confined to three issues: the meaning of nubuwwa/nabī and risāla/rasūl, the ruling of risāla, and the proof for its reality.
These form the structure of the coming lessons.
4
Examine the linguistic derivation
The word 'nubuwwa' has two possible derivations: with hamz (from naba' = news) and without hamz. The passage starts with the hamz derivation.
This is only the beginning; the lesson continues to discuss the second derivation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في النبوات ومن الجائزات ويجب الإيمان به : بعث الرسل إلى العباد ليبلغوهم أمر الله سبحانه ونهيه وإباحته ، وما يتعلق بذلك من خطاب الوضع ؛ لما عرفت أن العقل لا يدرك دون شرع طاعة ولا معصية ولا ما بينهما لما فرغ من الإلهيات وما يتعلق بها شرع في النبوات ، وينحصر الكلام فيها في ثلاث مسائل : الأولى : في معنى النبوة والنبي ، والرسالة والرسول . والثانية : في حكم الرسالة . والثالثة : في إقامة الدليل على ثبوتها ، وما يتعلق بذلك المسألة الأولى في معنى النبوة والنبي لفظ ( النبوة ) في اللغة على وجهين : مهموز ، وغير مهموز : فأما في لغة من همز : فهو مأخوذ من النبأ ؛ وهو الخبر ، ويحتمل
Chapter on Prophethood. And it is among contingent things and it is obligatory to believe in it: the sending of messengers to servants to convey to them Allah's command, His prohibition, and His permission, and what pertains to that of prescriptive speech (khiṭāb al-waḍʿ); because you have known that reason does not perceive, without divine law, obedience, disobedience, nor what is between them. When he finished with the divine matters (ilāhiyyāt) and what pertains to them, he began with prophethoods, and the discourse on them is confined to three issues: The first: concerning the meaning of prophethood (nubuwwa) and prophet (nabī), and message (risāla) and messenger (rasūl). The second: concerning the ruling of the message. The third: concerning the establishment of a proof for its reality, and what pertains to that. The first issue: concerning the meaning of prophethood and prophet. The word (al-nubuwwa) in language is of two types: pronounced with hamz and without hamz. As for the language of those who pronounce the hamz, it is derived from al-nabaʾ (news), and it is possible...
Source pages p. 541

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on prophethood. It begins by stating that the sending of messengers is a contingent possibility (jāʾiz) that must be believed. The reason is that the intellect alone cannot determine what is obedience, disobedience, or indifferent actions without revelation. This reiterates the conclusion from the previous lesson. Then the author outlines three issues to be discussed: (1) the meanings of nubuwwa, nabī, risāla, and rasūl; (2) the ruling on risāla (i.e., whether it is necessary for God); (3) establishing proof for prophethood. The first issue begins with a linguistic analysis of the word 'nubuwwa'. He notes that it has two possible origins: either with hamz (al-nubuwwa) or without hamz (al-nubuwwa). For those who pronounce the hamz, it is derived from 'al-nabaʾ' (news). He then mentions that there is another possibility, but the text is cut off at this point on the page.

Key Terms
النبوة
النبي
الرسالة
الرسول
النبأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the word 'nubuwwa' has only one derivation.
Correction: Sanusi shows there are two linguistic traditions: with hamz (from naba') and without hamz.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why is it necessary to believe in the sending of messengers?
A) Because reason alone cannot know moral duties.
B) Because God must do what is best for His creation.

Key Takeaway
Belief in messengers is obligatory because the intellect alone cannot know moral obligations, and the word 'nubuwwa' linguistically derives from 'news' (naba') in one of its forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often rely on your own judgment to decide what is right and wrong. But this lesson shows that without revelation, your intellect alone cannot perceive obedience or disobedience. In what areas of your life have you unknowingly trusted your reason over God's command? Will you commit to turning back to the Qur'an and Sunnah for moral guidance, even when it contradicts your own reasoning?

Reflect on your own dependence on God's guidance: if you lost access to revelation today, would you be able to distinguish right from wrong? What does that reveal about your need for the Prophet (peace be upon him) right now?
?

Introduction to Prophethood: Its Necessity and Threefold Division
Sanusi introduces the need for prophethood and outlines three issues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi argues that prophethood is necessary despite being contingent, and how he divides the discussion into three issues.

Core Idea
Reason cannot know good and evil without revelation, so prophethood is essential.

In this opening passage of the chapter on prophethood, Sanusi establishes the foundational need for divine guidance. He explains that human reason alone cannot determine moral obligations, making the sending of prophets a rational necessity. He then outlines the three key issues that will structure the entire discussion: definitions, ruling, and proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic script and the translation to get the overall flow. Notice how Sanusi connects the previous chapter on divine matters to this new topic.
The passage is dense but short.
2
Analyze the first sentence
Sanusi says prophethood is among contingent things (al-ja'izat) but belief in it is obligatory. Understand why something contingent can still be obligatory to believe once God has revealed it.
This shows the relationship between reason and revelation.
3
Understand the reason for prophethood
Sanusi states that reason does not perceive obedience or disobedience without divine law. Reflect on why this argument establishes the need for prophets.
This is a key epistemological point in kalam.
4
Identify the three issues
Find the enumeration of three issues: meaning of prophethood/prophet, ruling of messengerhood, and proof for its establishment. This gives you a map for the rest of the chapter.
Each issue will be addressed in the coming lessons.
5
Review the connection
Notice that Sanusi transitions from ilahiyyat to nubuwwat. See how the overall structure of the book progresses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في النبوات ومِنَ الجائزاتِ ويجب الإيمانُ بهِ : بعْتُ الرسل إلى العبادِ ليبلغوهم أمر الله سبحانهُ ونهيه وإباحته ، وما يتعلَّقُ بذلكَ مِنْ خطابِ الوضع ؛ لِمَا عرفْتَ أنَّ العقلَ لا يُدرِكُ دون شرع طاعة ولا معصية ولا ما بينهما لمَّا فرغَ مِنَ الإلهيَّاتِ وما يتعلَّقُ بها شرع في النبوَّاتِ ، وينحصر الكلام فيها في ثلاث مسائل : الأولى : في معنى النبوَّة والنبي ، والرسالة والرسول . والثانية : في حكم الرسالة . والثالثة : في إقامة الدليل على ثبوتها ، وما يتعلَّق بذلكَ
A chapter on prophethood. Among contingent things, and it is obligatory to believe in it: the sending of messengers to servants to convey to them the command of Allah the Exalted, His prohibition, and His permission, and what pertains to that from prescriptive speech; because you know that reason does not perceive, without divine law, obedience nor disobedience nor what is between them. When he finished with the divine matters and what pertains to them, he began with prophethood, and the discussion therein is confined to three issues: The first: concerning the meaning of prophethood and prophet, and messengerhood and messenger. The second: concerning the ruling of messengerhood. The third: concerning establishing the proof for its establishment, and what pertains to that.
Source pages p. 541

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on prophethood. First, Sanusi states that sending messengers is among contingent possibilities (al-ja'izat), yet belief in it is obligatory. He grounds this in the fact that reason alone cannot determine moral categories (obedience, disobedience) without revelation. This establishes the need for prophethood. Then he transitions from the preceding discussion of divine matters (al-ilahiyyat) to prophethood, and informs us that the subject is structured into three masail (issues): 1) the definition of nubuwwa/nabi and risala/rasul; 2) the ruling (hukm) of messengerhood; 3) proving the actual occurrence of prophethood. This enumeration gives the reader a clear blueprint of the upcoming arguments.

The Three Linguistic Derivations of 'Nabi'
Sanusi presents three possible lexical derivations of the word 'Nabi'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possible lexical derivations of the word 'Nabi' (prophet) and their theological significance.

Core Idea
The word 'Nabi' carries meanings of being informed and being elevated.

Before defining prophethood itself, Sanusi first clarifies the linguistic roots of the word 'Nabi'. This passage explores three possible derivations, each highlighting a different aspect of the prophetic role. Understanding these roots helps appreciate the richness of the term when discussing prophethood in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage slowly, then the literal English translation. Notice the three numbered points in the translation.
The passage uses grammatical patterns like فعيل and مفعول.
2
Identify the three derivations
List the three derivations: 1) فعيل بمعنى مفعول (informed), 2) فاعل أو مفعل (informer), 3) from النبوة (height). Understand what each implies about the prophet.
3
Examine the grammatical patterns
Notice how the first two derivations involve the hamza (ء) in different positions, and the third derivation never uses the hamza. This shows linguistic variation.
4
Reflect on theological implications
Consider why these derivations matter: they connect prophethood to receiving revelation (being informed), delivering it (informing others), and being elevated above ordinary human status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنْ يكونَ ( فَعِيل ( بمعنى ( مفعول ) أي : مُنْبَأُ بالغيوبِ (١) ، أو هو بمعنى ( فاعل ) أو ( مُفْعِل ) أي : هو مُنْبِيءٌ بما أطلعه الله تعالى عليه (۲) ، ويصح تركُ الهمزة في هذين الوجهين تسهيلاً وأما في لغةِ مَنْ لم يهمزهُ مِنْ أصل : فهو مأخوذُ مِنَ النَّبْوَةِ بفتح النون ؛ وهو ما ارتفعَ مِنَ الأرضِ ، يُقالُ : نبا الشيء ؛ إذا ارتفعَ . فالمعنى على هذا : أنَّ النبيَّ مُرتفع عن طورِ البشر باختصاصه بالوخي وخطاب الله تعالى (۳)
That it is (fa'eel) in the meaning of (maf'ool), i.e., informed of the unseen (1), or it is in the meaning of (fa'il) or (muf'il), i.e., he is an informer of what God Almighty has shown him (2), and dropping the hamza in these two aspects is permissible for ease. As for the language of those who never hamzate it originally: it is derived from 'an-nabwah' with a fatha on the nun, which is what rises from the earth. It is said: 'naba ash-shay' if it rose. So the meaning according to this: that the Prophet is elevated above the state of humans by his special distinction with revelation and the speech of God Almighty (3).
Source pages p. 542

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic analysis of the word 'Nabi' (prophet). The author presents three possible derivations. First, it can be fa'eel in the sense of maf'ool, i.e., 'one who is informed of the unseen' (munba'). Second, it can be fa'il or muf'il, i.e., 'one who informs' (munbi'). In both cases, the hamza can be dropped for ease. Third, for those who never use hamza, it is derived from 'nabwah' meaning height, so the prophet is elevated above human condition by being singled out for revelation and divine speech. Thus the word carries connotations of both being informed and being elevated.

Correction: Sanusi shows three valid linguistic derivations, each adding a different dimension to the concept of prophethood.

Prophethood: By Divine Choice, Not Human Effort
Sanusi rejects essential and acquired theories of prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why prophethood is neither an inherent attribute of the prophet nor a result of personal spiritual effort, but a divine choice.

Core Idea
Prophethood is a gift of God's selection, not earned or innate.

In this passage, Sanusi corrects two misconceptions about prophethood. Some sects believed it was an essential part of a prophet's nature, while others thought it could be acquired through spiritual discipline. Sanusi rejects both and anchors prophethood in God's sovereign will.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal English translation. Pay attention to the structure: Sanusi begins with two negations before giving the positive statement.
2
Identify the two rejected views
Look for the two groups Sanusi mentions: al-Karramiyya and the philosophers. Understand their positions: essential attribute vs. acquired through self-purification.
Al-Karramiyya held prophethood as inherent; philosophers taught it could be polished like a mirror.
3
Grasp Sanusi's positive affirmation
Sanusi states the basis of prophethood: God's selection (istifa) and revelation (wahy). Note that this emphasizes divine sovereignty over human effort.
Contrast with the two rejected views to see why only the Islamic position preserves divine grace.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليسَتِ النبوَّةُ صفةً ذاتيّةً للنبي كما صارَ إليه الكرَّاميَّة (٤) ، ولا مكتسبةً كما صارَ إِليهِ الفلاسفةُ ؛ فإنَّهم يرون التزكية والتحلية صِقَالاً في مرآة النفس إلى أن يتهيَّاً لما لا يتهيَّأُ لإدراكه غيرُه (٥) ، وإِنَّما مرجع النبوَّةِ إلى اصطفاء الله عبداً مِنْ عبادِهِ بالوحي إليه .
And prophethood is not an essential attribute of the prophet as al-Karramiyya held, nor is it acquired as the philosophers held; for they see self-purification and adornment as a polish on the mirror of the soul until it becomes prepared for what no other is prepared to perceive. Rather, the basis of prophethood is God's selection of a servant from among His servants by revelation to him.
Source pages p. 542

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal statement rejecting two false views about the nature of prophethood. The Karramiyya sect held that prophethood is an essential attribute (dhatiyya) of the prophet, meaning it is inherent. The philosophers held that it can be acquired through spiritual self-purification (tazkiya) and adornment (tahliya) until the soul's mirror is polished enough to receive special knowledge. Sanusi rejects both: prophethood is not essential (otherwise every human would have it) nor acquirable by human effort. Instead, it is purely by God's choice (istifa') to send revelation to a particular servant. This emphasizes divine sovereignty and grace.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophethood can be acquired through self-purification and spiritual effort.
Correction: Sanusi states that prophethood is not acquired; it is solely by God's selection and revelation.

Checkpoint

B) Acquired through self-purification
C) God's selection and revelation
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
Prophethood is solely by God's choice, not an inherent trait nor an acquired skill.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the prophets, do you secretly think they somehow 'earned' their status through extraordinary effort? This lesson says prophethood is purely by God's choice. How does that confront your pride about your own spiritual efforts? Are you willing to admit that even your faith is a gift, not a wage?
?

?
Has there been a moment when you felt spiritually superior because of your worship or knowledge? How does Sanusi’s rejection of acquired prophethood expose the danger of that feeling? Will you today repent of expecting Allah to owe you anything?

Prophethood and the Distinction Between Nabi and Rasul
Sanusi defines prophethood and presents four opinions on the relation between Nabi and Rasul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's definition of prophethood and the four scholarly views on the relationship between a Nabi and a Rasul.

Core Idea
Every messenger is a prophet, but not every prophet is a messenger.

Sanusi begins by defining prophethood itself—what makes a person a prophet? He then refines that definition to distinguish between a prophet who is merely inspired and one who is commanded to deliver a message. The heart of the passage is the precise relationship between 'Nabi' and 'Rasul', a topic on which scholars held four distinct positions. As you read, pay attention to how Sanusi anchors his own view first, then reports alternative opinions.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of prophethood
Examine Sanusi's definition: 'prophethood is the special distinction of hearing revelation from God, by means of an angel or without it.' Notice that the core is receiving divine communication.
2
Trace the condition for becoming a Rasul
If the one who hears revelation is commanded to convey it, that characteristic is called 'risala' (messengership). So the distinguishing feature between a Nabi and a Rasul is the command to deliver the revelation.
3
Understand the absolute specificity
Sanusi states: 'The Rasul is more specific than the Nabi absolutely; so every Rasul is a Nabi, and not every Nabi is a Rasul.' This is the first opinion—the one he presents as 'according to us'.
4
Examine the three alternative opinions
The passage lists three other views: (1) they are synonymous, (2) they have a general-specific relationship in one aspect, (3) they are mutually exclusive. Note how each opinion redefines the overlap or separation of the two categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالنبوَّةُ عندنا : هي اختصاص بسماع وحْيِ مِنَ اللهِ بواسطةِ مَلَكِ أو دونَهُ ، فإنْ أُمِرَ بتبليغِهِ فرسالةٌ ، فالمختص بالأوَّلِ والثاني رسول ، وبالأوَّلِ فقط نبي ، فالرسولُ إذا أخص مِنَ النبي مطلقاً ؛ فكلُّ رسول نبي ، وليس كل نبي رسولاً ، وقيل : هما بمعنى وقيلَ : بينهما عموم وخصوص بوجه ؛ فيجتمعان في الرسولِ مِنَ البشر ، وينفرد النبيُّ فيمَنْ أُوحي إليهِ مِنَ البشرِ ولم يُؤمَرُ بالتبليغ ، وينفرد الرسولُ فيمَنْ أُوحي إليهِ مِنَ الملائكةِ وبُعِثَ إلى غيرِه . وقيل : هما متباينانِ (۱) ؛ وإِنَّ الرسل : هم أصحاب الكتب والشرائع والنبيُّونَ : هم الذين يحكمون بالمنزلِ على غيرِهم معَ أنهم يُوحى إليهم .
So prophethood according to us is: a special distinction of hearing revelation from God by means of an angel or without it. If he is commanded to convey it, then it is a message. So the one distinguished by the first and second is a Rasul, and by the first only is a Nabi. Thus the Rasul is more specific than the Nabi absolutely; so every Rasul is a Nabi, and not every Nabi is a Rasul. And it was said: they are synonymous. And it was said: between them is general and specific in one aspect; they meet in the human Rasul, and the Nabi is specific to one who is revealed to from among humans and not commanded to convey, and the Rasul is specific to one who is revealed to from among angels and sent to others. And it was said: they are mutually exclusive (1); and that the Rusul are the people of books and laws, and the Prophets are those who judge by what is revealed to others even though they receive revelation.
Source pages p. 543

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification. First, Sanusi defines prophethood (nubuwwa) as the special distinction of hearing revelation from God, either through an angel or directly. If the prophet is commanded to convey that revelation, he is a messenger (Rasul). Then he explains the relationship: the Rasul is more specific (akhas) than the Nabi absolutely, meaning all messengers are prophets but not vice versa. However, he also reports three other opinions: (1) they are synonymous (بمعنى); (2) they share a general-specific relationship in one aspect (عموم وخصوص بوجه) — a human who is both a prophet and messenger falls under both, a human prophet who is not commanded to convey is only a prophet, and an angel sent to others is only a messenger; (3) they are mutually exclusive (متباينان) — then messengers are those with books and laws, while prophets are those who judge by revelation given to others. Sanusi does not explicitly choose among these but presents them as views. The first view (according to us) is the one most commonly held by Ash'aris.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'Nabi' and 'Rasul' are interchangeable synonyms without any distinction.
Correction: Sanusi shows multiple nuanced views; his own view is that every Rasul is a Nabi but not vice versa, establishing a clear distinction.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's own view (عندنا), what is the relationship between a Nabi and a Rasul?
A) They are synonymous
B) Every Rasul is a Nabi but not vice versa
C) They are mutually exclusive
D) They are the same but Rasul implies more duties

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's definition of prophethood centers on receiving revelation, and his primary distinction is that a messenger (Rasul) is a prophet (Nabi) commanded to convey the revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The primary definition of prophethood is hearing revelation. You have direct access to divine revelation through the Quran. Are you truly 'hearing' it, or are you just reading words? What would it mean for you to listen to the Quran as if it were being revealed to you now, with the same urgency as the prophets?

The Ruling on Prophethood: Possible by Divine Grace
Sanusi states the three positions on the ruling of prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position on prophethood as a possible act of divine grace, contrasted with the Mu'tazili and Brahmin views, and why the refutation of rational ethics suffices to dismiss both extremes.

Core Idea
Prophethood is a possible gift from Allah, not a duty or an impossibility.

In this passage, Sanusi presents the Ash'ari stance on prophethood — that God may send messengers by His grace, but is not obligated to. He contrasts this with two opposing views: the Mu'tazila who see it as rationally obligatory, and the Brahmins who deny its possibility. Sanusi then points out that the previous refutation of the principles of rational ethics (tahsin and taqbih) already dismantles both extremes, making further argument unnecessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the three positions.
Notice the concise style: Sanusi states each view briefly.
2
Identify the three positions
Locate the Ash'ari position (prophethood is possible and a grace), the Mu'tazili view (obligatory), and the Brahmin view (impossible).
The passage labels them clearly: 'مذهب أهل الحق', 'المعتزلة', 'البراهمة'.
3
Understand the key refutation
Sanusi claims that the corruption of both non-Ash'ari views is clear given the earlier invalidation of rational good/evil (tahsin and taqbih). Reflect on why that matters.
This connects the lesson to previous material on ethical principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية في حكم الرسالة مذهب أهل الحقِّ : أنَّ الرسالةَ ممكنة ، تفضَّلَ مولانا جل وعلا بها ، وأوجبتها المعتزلة عقلاً على أصلهم في وجوب مراعاة الصلاح والأصلح، ومنعتها البراهمة عقلاً ، ولا يخفى فساد المذهبين إن حقق ما مضى مِنْ إبطالِ أصل التحسينِ والتقبيح ومراعاة الصلاحِ والأصلح ، فلا حاجة لنا إلى التطويل بكثرة الحُجَج ، وقدِ اتَّضح الحق وصار نهاراً .
The second issue: On the ruling of the message. The school of Ahl al-Haqq is that the message is possible; our Master, Glorious and Exalted, has graced it. The Mu'tazila made it intellectually obligatory (wajib) based on their principle of the obligation to observe what is beneficial and most beneficial. The Brahmins denied it intellectually. And the corruption of both schools is not hidden if one realizes what has passed of invalidating the principle of rational good and evil (tahsin and taqbih) and the observance of the beneficial and most beneficial. So we have no need for lengthy arguments; the truth has become clear and bright as day.
Source pages p. 543

Commentary
This passage is a concise doctrinal statement about the ruling (hukm) of prophethood. The Ahl al-Haqq (Ash'aris and Maturidis) hold that the sending of messengers is merely possible (mumkin) and that God graces humankind with it by His will. The Mu'tazila, based on their principle of al-salah wa al-aslah (what is beneficial and most beneficial), hold that God must send messengers because it is rationally obligatory (wajib) for Him to do what is in the best interest of His creation. The Brahmins (Indian philosophers) hold that prophethood is rationally impossible (mumtani). Sanusi dismisses both extremes by referring to previously established arguments that invalidate the rational basis of ethical valuation (tahsin wa taqbih) and the obligation to observe the beneficial. Since those principles have been refuted in earlier sections, the falsehood of both Mu'tazila and Brahmins is evident. Therefore no lengthy proofs are needed; the truth is clear.

المعتزلة
البراهمة
صلاح
أصلح

تقبيح
عقلاً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophethood must be either rationally obligatory or rationally impossible.
Correction: Ash'aris affirm it is possible and a grace, neither obligatory nor impossible.

Checkpoint

A) It is impossible
B) It is possible but not necessary
C) It is intellectually obligatory on God
D) It is a matter of human acquisition

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position is that prophethood is a possible act of divine grace, and both the Mu'tazili obligation and Brahmin impossibility are refuted by the invalidation of rational ethical valuation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that prophethood is a pure grace from Allah and not an obligation, how does that affect your gratitude for the Prophet ﷺ? Take a moment to sincerely thank Allah for sending messengers despite having no duty to do so. What changes in your heart?
?
The Mu'tazila thought God must send prophets because it is good for you. Have you ever felt that God owes you something because of your deeds? Confront that thought: Where does it come from? And how does this lesson's refutation of obligatory benefit reshape your understanding of God's generosity?
?
The Brahmins relied entirely on reason to deny prophethood. In your own life, is there an area where you trust your own reasoning more than divine revelation? What would it take to surrender that to Allah and accept His message as pure grace?

Definition of Prophethood and Distinction Between Prophet and Messenger
Sanusi defines prophethood and distinguishes prophet from messenger with multiple opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's definition of prophethood and the relationship between a prophet and a messenger.

Core Idea
A messenger is a prophet commanded to convey revelation; not every prophet is a messenger.

In this passage, Sanusi establishes the technical definitions of 'prophet' and 'messenger' in Islamic theology. He presents his own view and then reports other scholarly opinions. This lesson unpacks the definitions, the distinctions, and the evidence that supports Sanusi's position.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own wording come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi begins with a definition and then lists multiple opinions.
2
Identify Sanusi's core definition and distinction
Find the definition of prophethood and the criteria that distinguish a prophet from a messenger. Understand the phrase 'الرسول أخص من النبي مطلقا'.
3
Examine the alternative opinions and the Qur'anic objection
Sanusi reports three other views. Identify each one and see how the verse from Maryam is used to challenge the last opinion.
This will help you see how kalam scholars argue from revealed texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالنبوَّةُ عندنا : هي اختصاص بسماع وحْيِ مِنَ اللهِ بواسطةِ مَلَكِ أو دونَهُ ، فإنْ أُمِرَ بتبليغِهِ فرسالةٌ ، فالمختص بالأوَّلِ والثاني رسول ، وبالأوَّلِ فقط نبي ، فالرسولُ إذا أخص مِنَ النبي مطلقاً ؛ فكلُّ رسول نبي ، وليس كل نبي رسولاً ، وقيل : هما بمعنى وقيلَ : بينهما عموم وخصوص بوجه ؛ فيجتمعان في الرسولِ مِنَ البشر ، وينفرد النبيُّ فيمَنْ أُوحي إليهِ مِنَ البشرِ ولم يُؤمَرُ بالتبليغ ، وينفرد الرسولُ فيمَنْ أُوحي إليهِ مِنَ الملائكةِ وبُعِثَ إلى غيرِه . وقيل : هما متباينانِ ؛ وإِنَّ الرسل : هم أصحاب الكتب والشرائع والنبيُّونَ : هم الذين يحكمون بالمنزلِ على غيرِهم معَ أنهم يُوحى إليهم . ويشكل بنحو قوله تعالى : ﴿ وَكَانَ رَسُولًا نَبِيًّا ﴾ [مريم : ٥١].
Prophethood with us is: a specialization in hearing revelation from Allah by means of an angel or without him. If he is commanded to convey it, then it is a message. So the one specialized in the first and second is a messenger, and in the first only is a prophet. Thus the messenger is absolutely more specific than the prophet; so every messenger is a prophet, and not every prophet is a messenger. And it is said: they are synonymous. And it is said: between them is general and specific in some respect; so they meet in the messenger from humans, and the prophet is exclusive to the one who was revealed to from humans and was not commanded to convey, and the messenger is exclusive to the one who was revealed to from angels and sent to others. And it is said: they are disparate; and indeed the messengers are the possessors of books and laws, and the prophets are those who judge by what has been revealed upon others while they receive revelation. And it is problematic by the like of His saying the Exalted: 'And he was a messenger, a prophet' [Maryam: 51].
Source pages p. 543

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification. Sanusi begins by defining prophethood as the specific quality of hearing revelation from Allah, either through an angel or directly. If the recipient is commanded to convey that revelation, then it becomes a message (رسالة). Based on this, he distinguishes two types: a messenger (رسول) is one who receives revelation and is commanded to convey it; a prophet (نبي) is one who receives revelation but is not commanded to convey it. Thus, the messenger is more specific (أخص) absolutely: every messenger is a prophet, but not every prophet is a messenger. Then Sanusi reports three other opinions: (1) the two terms are synonymous; (2) they are general and specific in some respect—the overlap occurs in a human messenger, while a prophet exclusively receives revelation without being commanded to convey, and a messenger exclusively can be an angel sent to others; (3) they are completely disparate—messengers are those with books and laws, while prophets judge by previous scriptures. Sanusi then points out a difficulty with the third opinion using the Qur'anic verse 'وكان رسولاً نبياً' (He was a messenger, a prophet), which indicates that both titles can combine in one person, contradicting the claim of complete disparity. This passage thus lays out the technical definitions and the scholarly debate on the relationship.

Key Terms
نبوة
نبي
رسول
رسالة
وحي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophet and messenger are always distinct and never combine in one person.
Correction: The Qur'anic verse 'وكان رسولاً نبياً' shows that a single person can be both a prophet and a messenger, contradicting the view of complete disparity.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's definition, what distinguishes a messenger from a prophet?
A) A messenger receives revelation directly from Allah without an angel.
B) A messenger is commanded to convey the revelation, while a prophet is not necessarily commanded.
C) A prophet is always a human, while a messenger can be an angel.
D) A messenger brings a new law, while a prophet does not.

Key Takeaway
According to Sanusi, a prophet receives revelation, while a messenger is a prophet who is commanded to convey it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that a messenger is a prophet who is commanded to convey. What truth has Allah revealed to you through this lesson that you are now commanded to convey, and what is stopping you from delivering it to those who need to hear it?
?
Sanusi points out that not every prophet is a messenger. Consider your own life: have you been receiving guidance from Allah but failing to act on it or share it because you feel you are not 'commanded'? What would change if you saw every truth as a message to be delivered?
?
The verse 'And he was a messenger, a prophet' shows that a single person can combine both roles. Are you living as if you are only a receiver of knowledge, or are you actively fulfilling the role of a messenger by conveying what you know? What specific step will you take today to convey one truth from this lesson?

The Ruling on Prophethood: Possibility, Not Necessity nor Impossibility
Sanusi states that prophethood is intellectually possible, refuting Mu'tazila and Barahima.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ahl al-Sunna affirm that prophethood is intellectually possible, in contrast to the Mu'tazila and Barahima.

Core Idea
Prophethood is possible, not obligatory nor impossible.

Sanusi now turns to the second major theological issue concerning prophethood: its rational status. While all Muslims agree on the reality of prophethood, they differ on its intellectual grounding. In this passage, Sanusi identifies the correct position and dismisses two extreme views by referring to earlier foundational principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the author's own words set the stage.
Notice how concise Sanusi is; he builds on prior refutations.
2
Identify the three positions
Find the positions of Ahl al-Haqq, Mu'tazila, and Barahima. Note the key Arabic terms: ممكنة, أوجبتها, منعتها.
3
Understand the basis of each position
Ahl al-Haqq see it as a favor; Mu'tazila base it on obligation of benefit; Barahima deny it. Reflect on why each group adopts its stance.
The Mu'tazila and Barahima rely on the rational evaluation of good and bad.
4
See how Sanusi refutes the opposing views
He says their corruption is clear from the earlier refutation of their principles. No need for lengthy proofs.
This shows the interconnectedness of theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية في حكم الرسالة مذهب أهل الحق : أنَّ الرسالةَ ممكنة ، تفضَّلَ مولانا جل وعلا بها ، وأوجبتها المعتزلة عقلاً على أصلهم في وجوب مراعاة الصلاح والأصلح، ومنعتها البراهمة عقلاً ، ولا يخفى فساد المذهبين إن حقق ما مضى مِنْ إبطالِ أصل التحسينِ والتقبيح ومراعاة الصلاحِ والأصلح ، فلا حاجة لنا إلى التطويل بكثرة الحُجَج ، وقدِ اتَّضح الحق وصار نهاراً .
The second issue: on the ruling of the message. The doctrine of the People of Truth is that the message is possible; our Master, Glorious and Exalted, favored with it. The Mu'tazila made it intellectually obligatory based on their principle of the necessity of observing what is beneficial and most beneficial. The Brahmins denied it intellectually. And the corruption of both doctrines is not hidden if one realizes what has passed of refuting the principle of al-tahsin wa al-taqbih (the rational judgment of good and bad) and the observance of what is beneficial and most beneficial. So we have no need to lengthen with many proofs, and the truth has become clear and bright as day.
Source pages p. 543

Commentary
This passage is a concise argument on the ruling of prophethood. Sanusi presents the position of Ahl al-Haqq (the People of Truth) that prophethood is intellectually possible (ممكنة), meaning it is neither necessary nor impossible, but rather a possible event that Allah may or may not do. Allah graciously favored humanity by sending messengers. He then mentions two opposing views: the Mu'tazila held it is rationally obligatory (أوجبتها عقلاً) based on their doctrine that Allah must do what is most beneficial (al-salāh wa al-aslah). The Brahmins (البراهمة) denied it rationally, arguing it is impossible. Sanusi dismisses both by referring to the earlier refutation of the principle of al-tahsin wa al-taqbih (the rational determination of good and evil) and the obligation of the most beneficial. Since those principles have been refuted, the views built upon them collapse. Thus, there is no need for lengthy argument; the truth is clear.

Key Terms
رسالة
ممكنة
تحسين وتقبيح
صلاح وأصلح
معتزلة
براهمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophethood is rationally necessary (Mu'tazila) or rationally impossible (Barahima).
Correction: Ahl al-Sunna affirm it is rationally possible, neither necessary nor impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the position of Ahl al-Haqq regarding the possibility of prophethood?
A) It is intellectually obligatory upon Allah.
B) It is intellectually impossible.
C) It is intellectually possible.
D) It is rationally necessary for societal order.

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Sunna hold that prophethood is intellectually possible, contingent on Allah's will, and not rationally necessary or impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If prophethood were rationally necessary, would you feel less gratitude to Allah for sending messengers? How does knowing that it is a pure favor (تفضل) change your relationship with the Prophet Muhammad ﷺ?
?
The Barahima denied prophethood based on reason alone. What modern objections to prophethood do you encounter in your own mind or from others? Are they built on false premises like al-tahsin wa al-taqbih? How will you guard your heart against them using the truth that has become 'clear as day'?
?
Sanusi says there is no need for lengthy proofs because the earlier refutation is sufficient. Is your certainty in the possibility and reality of prophethood built on solid intellectual foundations, or do you rely on blind acceptance? What step will you take today to strengthen your conviction?
