The Exclusive and Auxiliary Benefits of Prophethood
Sanusi explains the exclusive benefit of messengers and their auxiliary role in rational matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the unique benefit of sending messengers—conveying divine commands—and how prophets also assist reason in rational matters.

Core Idea
Prophets alone deliver divine commands; reason cannot reach them unaided.

Sanusi begins by highlighting a key benefit of prophethood: conveying Allah's commands to humanity. He notes that this benefit is exclusive to messengers—reason cannot discover it on its own. Even in rational matters, prophets guide and refine the intellect, cutting off all excuses. This passage establishes the indispensability of revelation for knowing divine commands and duties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exclusive benefit
Read the passage and find where Sanusi states that conveying Allah's command is a benefit exclusive to messengers. Notice that he emphasizes this is the only way to know such commands.
The phrase 'مقصورة عليهم' indicates exclusivity.
2
Understand the auxiliary role of prophets
Consider how Sanusi explains that even for rational matters, prophets guide the intellect to truth with less effort and alert it to subtleties it would otherwise miss.
He mentions 'دقائق من الأنظار' (subtle points of observation).
3
Reflect on the cutting of excuses
Notice the concluding purpose: prophets cut off all excuses of creation. Ask yourself why this is necessary for accountability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمّا المسألة الثالثةُ : فسنذكرُ ما يتعلَّقُ بها معَ لفظِ العقيدة وقوله : ( ليبلغوهم أمرَ اللهِ . ) إلى آخره : إشارة منه إلى بعض فوائدِ بعثة الرسل ، وخص هذه الفائدة لأنَّها مقصورة عليهم لا يمكنُ وصولُ العقل إليها بدونهم ، وأمَّا غيرُها ممَّا أوضحوهُ مِنَ الأحكام العقلية وأدلَّتِها القطعية .. فقد يَتوصَّلُ العقل بدونهم إلى شيءٍ منها ، لكن ظهرَتِ الفائدة في هذا النوع وشبهه أنَّهم أرشدوا العقول إلى الحق فيه بدونِ كبيرِ تعب ، وفطَّنُوها إلى دقائقَ مِنَ الأنظارِ لم تكن تستقل بإدراكها ، وقطعوا معاذير الخَلْقِ مِنْ كُلِّ وَجْهٍ قوله : ( وما يتعلَّق بذلكَ مِنْ خطاب الوضع ( اسم الإشارة راجع إلى الأمر والنهي والإباحة ، وخطاب الوضع : هو الحكم على أمر بأنَّه سبب أو شرط أو مانع لتلك الأشياء المذكورة ؛ فالسببية كحكم الشرع على دخول الوقتِ بأنَّهُ سبب لوجوب الصلاة والأمر بها ، وعِدَّةِ المرأة بأنَّها سبب لمنعِ النكاح ، وانعقاد البيع بأنه سبب لإباحة التصرُّف في المبيع . قوله : ( ولا ما بينهما ) يعني : وهو ما ليس بطاعة ولا معصية ؛ كالمباح وخطابِ الوضع ؛ إذْ كلُّ ذلكَ لا يُعرَفُ إِلا مِنْ قِبَلِ الشرع
As for the third issue: we will mention what relates to it with the wording of the creed and His statement: 'to convey to them the command of Allah' to the end: an indication from him to some of the benefits of sending messengers. And he specified this benefit because it is exclusive to them; the intellect cannot reach it without them. As for other matters which they clarified from the rational rulings and their definitive proofs, the intellect may reach some of them without them, but the benefit in this type and its like is that they guided the intellects to the truth in it without great effort, and alerted them to subtle points of observations which the intellect could not independently perceive, and they cut off the excuses of creation from every aspect. His statement: 'and what relates to that from the legislative status (khitab al-wad')' (the pronoun refers to the command, prohibition, and permission). And the legislative status (khitab al-wad') is: the ruling on something that it is a cause (sabab), condition (shart), or impediment (mani') for those mentioned things. Thus causation (sababiyya) like the ruling of the law on the entering of the time that it is a cause for the obligation of prayer and the command to it, and the waiting period ('idda) of the woman that it is a cause for the prohibition of marriage, and the conclusion of a sale that it is a cause for the permission of disposal in the sold item. His statement: 'and not what is between them' meaning: that which is neither obedience nor disobedience, like the permissible (mubah) and the legislative status (khitab al-wad'); indeed all that is known only from the side of the Law.
Source pages p. 544

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the benefits of prophethood and a definition of a key legal concept. Sanusi begins by noting that the third issue will be discussed in conjunction with the creed's wording, specifically the phrase 'to convey to them the command of Allah'. He highlights that this benefit—conveying divine commands—is exclusive to messengers; the intellect cannot reach it by itself. As for rational matters, the intellect can reach some of them without prophetic guidance, but the prophets still aid the intellect by making it easier and by alerting it to subtleties it might miss. They also cut off excuses. Then Sanusi explains the phrase 'what relates to that from al-khitab al-wad'' (the legislative/declaratory address). He clarifies that the referent of 'that' is the command, prohibition, and permission (al-amr, al-nahy, al-ibaha). Then he defines khitab al-wad' as the legal ruling that something is a cause (sabab), condition (shart), or impediment (mani') for those previously mentioned categories (obligation, prohibition, etc.). He gives examples: the entry of time is a cause for the obligation of prayer; a woman's 'idda (waiting period) is a cause for the prohibition of marriage; the conclusion of a sale is a cause for the permission to dispose of the sold item. Finally, he explains the phrase 'and not what is between them' (ولا ما بينهما) from the creed: it refers to actions that are neither obedience nor outright disobedience, such as permissible acts (mubah) and matters of khitab al-wad'. All these legislatively determined matters are known only from revelation (shar'). This passage thus connects the necessity of prophethood with the concept that many legal rulings (especially causes, conditions, and impediments) are purely revealed and cannot be known by unaided reason.

Key Terms
بعثة الرسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That human reason can independently know all religious truths without the need for prophets.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly states that conveying divine commands is a benefit exclusive to messengers and unreachable by reason alone. Even in rational matters, prophets guide and refine the intellect, alerting it to subtleties it cannot perceive on its own.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, which benefit of sending messengers is exclusive to them?
A) Clarifying rational rulings and proofs.
B) Conveying the command of Allah.
C) Providing moral examples.
D) Cutting off excuses alone.

Key Takeaway
Prophets are indispensable for conveying divine commands, which reason alone cannot discover.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever relied solely on your own reasoning to determine right from wrong, ignoring what the prophets have commanded? What was the result?
?
The passage says prophets alert us to subtle points of observation we couldn't perceive alone. In what area of your life are you most in need of prophetic guidance to see what you cannot see?
?
Prophets cut off excuses. When you stand before Allah, what excuse will you have for not following the commands that only prophets could have brought? How can you ensure you are not making excuses now?

The Legislative Status (Khitab al-Wad'): Causes, Conditions, and Impediments
Sanusi defines khitab al-wad' and gives examples of its three categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of legislative status (khitab al-wad') and its three categories: cause, condition, and impediment.

Core Idea
Khitab al-wad' declares things as causes, conditions, or impediments—purely revealed rulings.

Sanusi now defines a key legal concept—al-khitab al-wad' (the legislative/declaratory address). He explains that it is not a command, prohibition, or permission, but rather a ruling that something is a cause, condition, or impediment for those actions. He illustrates with examples from prayer, marriage, and sales, and emphasizes that such rulings are known only from revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of khitab al-wad'
Locate where Sanusi defines khitab al-wad' as 'the ruling that something is a cause, condition, or impediment'. Understand that this is different from commands and prohibitions.
The pronoun 'ذلك' refers to command, prohibition, and permission.
2
Study the examples of causation
Examine the three examples given: entry of time as a cause for prayer obligation, 'idda as a cause for marriage prohibition, and sale conclusion as a cause for permission to dispose. Note that these are legislatively assigned causes.
These examples show how sabab operates in different areas.
3
Identify the category of 'what is between'
Understand the phrase 'nor what is between them'—actions that are neither obedience nor disobedience but permissible and matters of khitab al-wad'. Recognize that all these are known only from the Law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often rely on common sense to decide why something is obligatory or permissible. After learning that causes, conditions, and impediments are known only from the Law, what is one area of your religious practice where you might have assumed a rational cause instead of checking the revealed source? Will you investigate that this week?
?
The text includes 'mubah' (the permissible) as known only from revelation. Think of an action you consider permissible simply because 'everyone does it'—how can you ensure that your permissibility judgment is based on the Qur'an and Sunnah rather than culture? What will you change?
?
Sanusi emphasizes that even the categories of cause, condition, and impediment are legislated. This means your obedience to Allah includes accepting His legislative choices, not just following moral intuitions. Is there a divine ruling you find hard to accept because its rationale is not obvious to you? How will you train your heart to submit fully?

The Exclusive Benefit of Prophets: What Reason Cannot Reach
Sanusi distinguishes the exclusive benefit of prophets from other intellectual benefits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the prophets' conveyance of divine commands is a unique benefit that the intellect cannot attain independently.

Core Idea
Prophets reveal what reason alone cannot access.

In this passage, Sanusi explains that while reason can arrive at some intellectual rulings, there are certain benefits exclusive to prophets. He emphasizes that the conveyance of Allah's command is a special function that humanity cannot access through reason alone. This lesson explores that unique prophetic role.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on prophetic benefits
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi distinguishes between two types of benefits.
The passage starts with 'وأما المسألة الثالثة'.
2
Identify the exclusive benefit
Sanusi says he 'singled out this benefit' because it is restricted to prophets. Find that benefit and note why it is exclusive.
Hint: it relates to conveying something.
3
Compare with intellectual rulings
The passage mentions that the intellect may sometimes reach intellectual rulings without prophets. But what added value do prophets provide for these?
They guide the intellect with ease and alert it to subtleties.
4
Reflect on 'cutting off excuses'
The final phrase says prophets 'cut off the excuses of creation from every aspect'. What does this imply about the necessity of prophethood?
This shows that after revelation, no one can claim ignorance of the truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المسألة الثالثة : فسنذكر ما يتعلق بها مع لفظ العقيدة وقوله : ( ليبلغوهم أمر الله . ) إلى آخره : إشارة منه إلى بعض فوائد بعثة الرسل ، وخص هذه الفائدة لأنها مقصورة عليهم لا يمكن وصول العقل إليها بدونهم ، وأما غيرها مما أوضحوه من الأحكام العقلية وأدلتها القطعية .. فقد يتوصل العقل بدونهم إلى شيء منها ، لكن ظهرت الفائدة في هذا النوع وشبهه أنهم أرشدوا العقول إلى الحق فيه بدون كبير تعب ، وفطنوها إلى دقائق من الأنظار لم تكن تستقل بإدراكها ، وقطعوا معاذير الخلق من كل وجه

قوله : ( وما يتعلق بذلك من خطاب الوضع ) اسم الإشارة راجع إلى الأمر والنهي والإباحة ، وخطاب الوضع : هو الحكم على أمر بأنه سبب أو شرط أو مانع لتلك الأشياء المذكورة ؛ فالسببية كحكم الشرع على دخول الوقت بأنه سبب لوجوب الصلاة والأمر بها ، وعدة المرأة بأنها سبب لمنع النكاح ، وانعقاد البيع بأنه سبب لإباحة التصرف في المبيع . قوله : ( ولا ما بينهما ) يعني : وهو ما ليس بطاعة ولا معصية ؛ كالمباح وخطاب الوضع ؛ إذ كل ذلك لا يعرف إلا من قبل الشرع
And as for the third question: we will mention what relates to it with the word of the creed and His statement: 'that they may convey to them the command of Allah' etc.: an indication from him of some of the benefits of sending messengers, and he singled out this benefit because it is restricted to them, the intellect cannot reach it without them. As for other matters which they clarified from the intellectual rulings and their definitive proofs, the intellect may sometimes reach some of them without them, but the benefit appeared in this type and its like that they guided the intellects to the truth in it without great effort, and alerted them to subtleties of viewpoints that they were not independent in perceiving, and cut off the excuses of creation from every aspect.

His statement: 'and what relates to that from the command of disposition' – the demonstrative pronoun refers to command, prohibition, and permission. And the command of disposition: it is the ruling concerning a matter that it is a cause, condition, or obstacle for those mentioned things. For causality is like the ruling of the Law that the entry of time is a cause for the obligation of prayer and the command of it, and the waiting period of a woman is a cause for the prohibition of marriage, and the conclusion of a sale is a cause for permitting disposal of the sold item. His statement: 'and not what is between them' – meaning: that which is neither obedience nor disobedience; like the permitted and the command of disposition; for all that is not known except from the Law.
Source pages p. 544

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary on the third question of the creed. It first highlights a unique benefit of prophets: they convey matters that the intellect cannot reach independently, contrasting with intellectual rulings where reason can sometimes arrive on its own. Prophets also simplify access to truth and refine reasoning. The passage then explains the phrase 'command of disposition' (khiṭāb al-waḍʿ), distinguishing it from the commands of obligation (amr, nahy, ibāḥa). The command of disposition involves rulings that designate things as causes, conditions, or obstacles—e.g., the entry of time as a cause for prayer obligation, the waiting period as a cause prohibiting marriage, and sale conclusion as a cause for legal disposal. Finally, it addresses the category 'what is between them' (lā mā baynahumā): actions neither obedience nor disobedience, such as permissible acts and commands of disposition, which are known only through revelation.

Key Terms
فوائد بعثة الرسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all prophetic knowledge is accessible to reason alone.
Correction: The passage emphasizes that the benefit of conveying Allah's command is exclusive to prophets and cannot be reached by reason without them.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what benefit of prophets is exclusive to them and cannot be reached by reason alone?
A) Clarifying intellectual rulings and their proofs
B) Conveying matters that are beyond the reach of reason
C) Demonstrating miracles to prove truthfulness
D) Cutting off excuses through definitive proofs

Key Takeaway
Prophets have an exclusive role in conveying divine commands that reason cannot access, and they also guide reason to truth with ease.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If prophets had never come, what excuse would you have had for not knowing Allah's command? Are you still living as if that excuse exists, despite the clear guidance you have received?
?

The Command of Disposition: Causes, Conditions, and Obstacles
Sanusi defines the command of disposition and its three components, with examples of causality, and clarifies the category of actions neither obedient nor disobedient.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of 'command of disposition' (khiṭāb al-waḍʿ) and how it classifies actions as causes, conditions, or obstacles for legal rulings.

Core Idea
The command of disposition designates things as causes, conditions, or obstacles — known only through revelation.

After discussing the exclusive benefit of prophets, Sanusi turns to another category of divine communication: the command of disposition. This refers to rulings that establish something as a cause, condition, or obstacle for other legal rules. In this lesson, we explore these three types and the related concept of actions that are neither obedience nor disobedience.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of 'command of disposition'
Read the passage and find the definition: 'the ruling concerning a matter that it is a cause, condition, or obstacle'. Note that it differs from commands of obligation.
The demonstrative pronoun in 'وما يتعلق بذلك' refers to command, prohibition, and permission.
2
Identify the three categories
The passage mentions three categories: cause (sabab), condition (shart), and obstacle (māniʻ). Although only cause is exemplified, all three are part of the command of disposition.
The author uses 'سبب أو شرط أو مانع'.
3
Examine the examples of causality
Sanusi gives three examples: entry of time as a cause for prayer obligation, waiting period as a cause for marriage prohibition, and sale conclusion as a cause for permitting disposal.
These show how the Law creates causality.
4
Recognize the category 'what is between them'
The passage also addresses actions that are neither obedience nor disobedience: the permissible (mubāḥ) and the command of disposition itself. These are known only from revelation.
This category is distinct from the three types.

The Condition That Miracles Must Be Extraordinary
Commentator explains why miracles must be extraordinary and excludes ordinary phenomena.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why miracles are required to be extraordinary acts that break normal customs, and why ordinary or even rare natural events cannot serve as proofs of prophethood.

Core Idea
Miracles must break the norm because only the extraordinary can be a divine seal of truth.

The commentator begins by clarifying a key condition in defining miracles: they must be 'khāriq lil-‘ādah', i.e., beyond normal human capability. This lesson explains the rationale behind this condition, distinguishing genuine miracles from magic and natural wonders.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice how the commentator defines 'khāriq lil-‘ādah' by contrast with ordinary events.
2
Identify why ordinary acts are excluded
The passage states that ordinary acts can be performed by both truthful and liars, so they cannot serve as exclusive divine attestation. Find this reasoning.
Also note the examples: magic and magnetic attraction.
3
Reflect on the condition's purpose
Consider why the miracle must be extraordinary: without that, the miraculous nature (i'jāz) is not established. The passage also mentions that the act need not be specified in advance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( كالضرب والقتلِ ) مثال للفعل الذي وقع التحدي بعدمه واحترز بقوله : ( خارق للعادة ) من المعتاد ؛ فإنه يستوي فيه الصادق والكاذب ، ومن المعتاد : السحر ونحوه ، وإن كان سببه العادي نادراً ، خلافاً لمن جعل السحر خارقاً لكن بسبب خاص به ، ومن المعتاد أيضاً : ما يوجد في بعض الأجسام من الخواص ؛ كجذب الحديد بحجر المغناطيس إنما اشتُرِطَ كون الفعل خارقاً ؛ لعدم ثبوت الإعجاز بدونه ، وأيضاً : فإن المعجزة تتنزل منزلة التصديق بالقول ، ومعتاد الوقوع لا يدل على ذلك ؛ لعدم اختصاصه ، ولا يشترط كون الخارق معيناً من جهته اتفاقاً

في الكثرة أو الندورِ ، ولأجل أن هذا النوع النادر من المعتاد ولا يدل على شيء.. أورد البراهمة على هذه الشريطة أن قالوا : قد استقر في أذهان العقلاء ما توصل إليه الحكماء من العلوم ؛ كالطلسمات وأنواع الحيل ؛ كجر الثقيل بالخفيف ، وقد اشتهر في أسرار الموجودات عجائب ، حتى إن من لم يعرف حكم حجر المغناطيس في جذب الحديد فرآه .. تعجب من ذلك في أول رؤيته وقضى بأنه مما يخالف العادات ، فما الذي يؤمنكم أن مدعي النبوة اطلع على علم من العلوم وظهر له من أسرار الموجودات ما إذا أتى به لمن لا يعرف ذلك .. عده خارقاً ؟

والجواب : أنا إنما نستدل بالخارق إذا علمنا أنه من قبيل المعجزات ، ونحن نعلم قطعاً أن إحياء الموتى ، وقلب العصا حية ، وإبراء الأكمه والأبرص من غير معاناة .. ليس مما يدخل تحت الحيل ، ولا مما يتوصل إليه بغوص في هذه العلوم . وقد تقترن بالشيء قرائن تفيد العلم واليقين بأن ما أتى به ليس من القبيل الذي ذكرتموه ، وقد طرد الله عادته في حق أنبيائه وأصفيائه بأنه يقطع عنهم الوهم ببعدهم عن أرباب هذه العلوم ؛ فشخص يخرج إلى شعب شعيب بحيث لا يتوهم فيه مخالطة السحرة ، وآخر يخلقه أمياً
His statement: (such as beating and killing) is an example of the act whose non-occurrence was challenged. And he guarded by his statement: (breaking the norm) against the ordinary; for the ordinary is equally possible for the truthful and the liar. Among the ordinary is magic and the like, even if its ordinary cause is rare, contrary to the one who made magic extraordinary but by a cause specific to it. Also among the ordinary are the properties found in some bodies, such as the attraction of iron by the magnet. The act is required to be extraordinary only because without it the miraculous nature is not established. Also, the miracle serves as a confirmation by word, and what occurs ordinarily does not indicate that, because it is not specific. And it is not required that the extraordinary act be specified in its type, by agreement.

Concerning abundance or rarity, and because this rare type is ordinary and indicates nothing... the Barāhima raised an objection to this condition, saying: It is established in the minds of rational people what the sages have attained of sciences, such as talismans and types of artifices, like pulling a heavy object with a light one. Marvels have become famous in the secrets of existing things, such that whoever does not know the property of the magnet in attracting iron and sees it... is amazed at it upon first sight and judges that it contradicts norms. So what assures you that a claimant to prophethood has gained knowledge of some science and there has become apparent to him from the secrets of existing things that which, if he brings it to one who does not know that, he would count it as extraordinary?

And the answer: We only infer from the extraordinary when we know that it is of the type of miracles. And we know with certainty that reviving the dead, turning a stick into a snake, and healing the blind and leprous without any effort... are not among things that fall under artifices, nor are they attainable by delving into these sciences. And there may accompany the thing indications that provide knowledge and certainty that what he brought is not of the type you mentioned. And God has made it His custom concerning His prophets and saints that He cuts off from them the suspicion (of being distant from the masters of these sciences). So one person goes out to a remote mountain path such that it is not suspected that he mixes with sorcerers, and another He creates unlettered.
Source pages p. 551

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on Sanusi's definition of a miracle (mu'jiza). It begins by explaining the condition that a miracle must be 'extraordinary' (khāriq lil-‘ādah), i.e., breaking the normal course of events. The commentator gives examples of acts that are challenged to be produced (like beating and killing) and clarifies that the term 'extraordinary' excludes ordinary events, because ordinary events can be performed by both a truthful person and a liar. Among ordinary events are magic and similar phenomena, even if their causes are rare—some mistakenly consider magic extraordinary, but it has a specific natural cause. Also ordinary are the inherent properties of objects, like a magnet attracting iron. The reason for requiring the act to be extraordinary is that without it, the miraculous nature (i‘jāz) cannot be established; moreover, a miracle serves as a divine attestation, and ordinary occurrences cannot serve that purpose because they are not exclusive to prophets. It is also agreed that the exact type of extraordinary act need not be specified in advance.

The commentator then addresses an objection from the Barāhima (Indian theologians who denied prophethood). They argue that rare natural phenomena, like magnetism to someone unfamiliar, can be mistaken for miracles. They ask: what assurance do we have that a claimant to prophethood has not simply mastered a rare science or discovered a secret of nature that appears miraculous to the ignorant? The answer is twofold: First, we only accept as miraculous acts that are clearly beyond any human artifice or natural science, such as reviving the dead, turning a stick into a snake, or healing the blind and leprous without any means. These are categorically different from what can be achieved by talismans or tricks. Second, God's established custom regarding His prophets and saints is that He protects them from any suspicion of being associated with such arts; for example, a prophet may be brought up in a remote valley far from sorcerers, or be unlettered, so that it is impossible to attribute his miracles to human learning.

Key Terms
خارق للعادة
المعجزة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any rare natural phenomenon (like magnetism) or magic can be considered a miracle.
Correction: The passage clarifies that such phenomena have natural causes and are not exclusive to prophets, thus they are 'ordinary' in the sense of being possible for others, and do not qualify as miracles.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main reason miracles must be extraordinary rather than merely rare?
A) To make them more impressive to the audience.
B) Because ordinary events can be performed by both truthful and liars, and thus do not serve as exclusive divine attestation.
C) Because miracles must be impossible by any means.
D) Because only extraordinary acts can be attributed to God.

Key Takeaway
Miracles must be extraordinary because ordinary events cannot serve as exclusive divine attestation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a potential miracle in your life as mere coincidence or a natural explanation? What does this lesson reveal about your tendency to rationalize the extraordinary? Will you now be more open to seeing God's hand in events that break the norm?
?
If miracles are meant to be exclusive signs for prophets, why do you sometimes expect God to prove Himself to you through ordinary means? What does that expectation say about your trust in the prophets' messages?
?
The passage argues that ordinary acts cannot serve as divine attestation. In your daily life, do you look for extraordinary signs from God, or have you settled for a faith that requires no miracles? How can you cultivate a heart that longs for the miraculous without demanding it?

Refuting the Barāhima Objection: Miracles vs. Rare Sciences
Commentator presents and refutes the Barāhima objection regarding rare sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Barāhima objection that rare sciences could be mistaken for miracles, and how the commentator refutes it by appealing to the categorical difference of prophetic miracles and God's custom.

Core Idea
Prophets' miracles are categorically beyond all human art and safeguarded by God's custom.

The Barāhima raised a powerful objection: how can we be sure that a prophet's miracle is not just a rare natural phenomenon or a clever trick? This lesson examines their argument and the commentator's decisive response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage of the Barāhima's argument and its translation. Note their use of the magnet example to question the condition of extraordinariness.
2
Understand the two-part answer
The commentator gives two reasons: first, true miracles like reviving the dead are clearly beyond all human artifice; second, God's custom is that prophets are removed from suspicion of using such arts.
Look for the examples given: reviving the dead, turning a stick into a snake, healing the blind and leprous.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how the answer upholds the condition that miracles must be extraordinary while distinguishing them from rare sciences.
Note the final statement about prophets being unlettered or raised in isolation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( كالضرب والقتلِ ) مثال للفعل الذي وقع التحدي بعدمه واحترز بقوله : ( خارق للعادة ) من المعتاد ؛ فإنه يستوي فيه الصادق والكاذب ، ومن المعتاد : السحر ونحوه ، وإن كان سببه العادي نادراً ، خلافاً لمن جعل السحر خارقاً لكن بسبب خاص به ، ومن المعتاد أيضاً : ما يوجد في بعض الأجسام من الخواص ؛ كجذب الحديد بحجر المغناطيس إنما اشتُرِطَ كون الفعل خارقاً ؛ لعدم ثبوت الإعجاز بدونه ، وأيضاً : فإن المعجزة تتنزل منزلة التصديق بالقول ، ومعتاد الوقوع لا يدل على ذلك ؛ لعدم اختصاصه ، ولا يشترط كون الخارق معيناً من جهته اتفاقاً

في الكثرة أو الندورِ ، ولأجل أن هذا النوع النادر من المعتاد ولا يدل على شيء.. أورد البراهمة على هذه الشريطة أن قالوا : قد استقر في أذهان العقلاء ما توصل إليه الحكماء من العلوم ؛ كالطلسمات وأنواع الحيل ؛ كجر الثقيل بالخفيف ، وقد اشتهر في أسرار الموجودات عجائب ، حتى إن من لم يعرف حكم حجر المغناطيس في جذب الحديد فرآه .. تعجب من ذلك في أول رؤيته وقضى بأنه مما يخالف العادات ، فما الذي يؤمنكم أن مدعي النبوة اطلع على علم من العلوم وظهر له من أسرار الموجودات ما إذا أتى به لمن لا يعرف ذلك .. عده خارقاً ؟

والجواب : أنا إنما نستدل بالخارق إذا علمنا أنه من قبيل المعجزات ، ونحن نعلم قطعاً أن إحياء الموتى ، وقلب العصا حية ، وإبراء الأكمه والأبرص من غير معاناة .. ليس مما يدخل تحت الحيل ، ولا مما يتوصل إليه بغوص في هذه العلوم . وقد تقترن بالشيء قرائن تفيد العلم واليقين بأن ما أتى به ليس من القبيل الذي ذكرتموه ، وقد طرد الله عادته في حق أنبيائه وأصفيائه بأنه يقطع عنهم الوهم ببعدهم عن أرباب هذه العلوم ؛ فشخص يخرج إلى شعب شعيب بحيث لا يتوهم فيه مخالطة السحرة ، وآخر يخلقه أمياً
His statement: (such as beating and killing) is an example of the act whose non-occurrence was challenged. And he guarded by his statement: (breaking the norm) against the ordinary; for the ordinary is equally possible for the truthful and the liar. Among the ordinary is magic and the like, even if its ordinary cause is rare, contrary to the one who made magic extraordinary but by a cause specific to it. Also among the ordinary are the properties found in some bodies, such as the attraction of iron by the magnet. The act is required to be extraordinary only because without it the miraculous nature is not established. Also, the miracle serves as a confirmation by word, and what occurs ordinarily does not indicate that, because it is not specific. And it is not required that the extraordinary act be specified in its type, by agreement.

Concerning abundance or rarity, and because this rare type is ordinary and indicates nothing... the Barāhima raised an objection to this condition, saying: It is established in the minds of rational people what the sages have attained of sciences, such as talismans and types of artifices, like pulling a heavy object with a light one. Marvels have become famous in the secrets of existing things, such that whoever does not know the property of the magnet in attracting iron and sees it... is amazed at it upon first sight and judges that it contradicts norms. So what assures you that a claimant to prophethood has gained knowledge of some science and there has become apparent to him from the secrets of existing things that which, if he brings it to one who does not know that, he would count it as extraordinary?

And the answer: We only infer from the extraordinary when we know that it is of the type of miracles. And we know with certainty that reviving the dead, turning a stick into a snake, and healing the blind and leprous without any effort... are not among things that fall under artifices, nor are they attainable by delving into these sciences. And there may accompany the thing indications that provide knowledge and certainty that what he brought is not of the type you mentioned. And God has made it His custom concerning His prophets and saints that He cuts off from them the suspicion (of being distant from the masters of these sciences). So one person goes out to a remote mountain path such that it is not suspected that he mixes with sorcerers, and another He creates unlettered.
Source pages p. 551

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on Sanusi's definition of a miracle (mu'jiza). It begins by explaining the condition that a miracle must be 'extraordinary' (khāriq lil-‘ādah), i.e., breaking the normal course of events. The commentator gives examples of acts that are challenged to be produced (like beating and killing) and clarifies that the term 'extraordinary' excludes ordinary events, because ordinary events can be performed by both a truthful person and a liar. Among ordinary events are magic and similar phenomena, even if their causes are rare—some mistakenly consider magic extraordinary, but it has a specific natural cause. Also ordinary are the inherent properties of objects, like a magnet attracting iron. The reason for requiring the act to be extraordinary is that without it, the miraculous nature (i‘jāz) cannot be established; moreover, a miracle serves as a divine attestation, and ordinary occurrences cannot serve that purpose because they are not exclusive to prophets. It is also agreed that the exact type of extraordinary act need not be specified in advance.

The commentator then addresses an objection from the Barāhima (Indian theologians who denied prophethood). They argue that rare natural phenomena, like magnetism to someone unfamiliar, can be mistaken for miracles. They ask: what assurance do we have that a claimant to prophethood has not simply mastered a rare science or discovered a secret of nature that appears miraculous to the ignorant? The answer is twofold: First, we only accept as miraculous acts that are clearly beyond any human artifice or natural science, such as reviving the dead, turning a stick into a snake, or healing the blind and leprous without any means. These are categorically different from what can be achieved by talismans or tricks. Second, God's established custom regarding His prophets and saints is that He protects them from any suspicion of being associated with such arts; for example, a prophet may be brought up in a remote valley far from sorcerers, or be unlettered, so that it is impossible to attribute his miracles to human learning.

Key Terms
البراهمة
الحيل
الطلسمات
خارق للعادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A prophet could produce a miracle simply by mastering a rare science or secret knowledge that appears miraculous to the ignorant.
Correction: The passage argues that true miracles like reviving the dead are categorically beyond any human science, and God ensures that prophets are not associated with such arts.

Checkpoint
How does the commentator answer the Barāhima's concern that a prophet might have learned rare sciences?
A) By saying prophets are all-knowing.
B) By distinguishing miracles from all human artifice and noting God's custom.
C) By admitting the possibility but ignoring it.
D) By saying only prophets can perform magic.

Key Takeaway
True miracles are unmistakably beyond human ability, and God's custom protects prophets from being confused with sorcerers or scientists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a divine sign as merely coincidental or explainable by science? What in your heart prevents you from seeing God's hand clearly?
?
God protects His prophets from the suspicion of sorcery. Where in your life have you placed yourself in situations that could weaken your faith in the miraculous? What step will you take today to distance yourself from doubts?
?
The Barāhima doubted because they lacked certainty. Are you relying on worldly knowledge to judge matters of faith? What would it take for you to submit to a miracle without demanding an explanation?

Reply to the Brahmins on Miracles
Sanusi replies to the Brahmins' objection that miracles could be natural tricks.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the Brahmins' claim that miracles could be explained by hidden natural sciences, by distinguishing miracles from tricks and citing contextual proofs.

Core Idea
Miracles are known by their content and the prophet's context, not just by being extraordinary.

The Brahmins argued that miracles could be no more than clever tricks based on obscure natural sciences. Sanusi responds by showing that true miracles are qualitatively different, involving acts like reviving the dead, and that prophets are providentially kept from such sciences. This lesson examines that response and its logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through before interpreting.
Notice that Sanusi uses a concise objection-answer format.
2
Identify the criteria for miracles
Sanusi states that we only use the extraordinary as evidence when we know it belongs to the category of miracles. Find the examples he gives (reviving the dead, etc.) and note why they are beyond tricks.
3
Understand contextual proofs (qara'in)
Sanusi explains that God's custom is to keep prophets away from sciences that could be used for deception. Identify the two examples he provides (remote mountain pass and the Prophet's illiteracy).
The Qur'anic verse on the Prophet's illiteracy is a key proof.
4
Reflect on enemy investigation
Sanusi argues that even enemies who investigate prophets thoroughly find no natural explanation, and they only deny out of stubbornness. Consider why this strengthens the argument.
This shows that the denial is not based on evidence but on prejudice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الكثرة أو الندورِ ، ولأجلِ أنَّ هذا النوع النادرَ مِنَ المعتادِ ، ولا يدلُّ على شيء.. أوردَ البراهمة على هذه الشريطة أن قالوا : قد استقر في أذهان العقلاء ما توصل إليه الحكماء مِنَ العلومِ ؛ كالطَّلِّسْماتِ وأنواع الحيل ؛ كجرّ الثقيل بالخفيف ، وقد اشتهر في أسرار الموجوداتِ عجائب ، حتى إِنَّ مَنْ لم يعرف حكم حجرِ المغناطيس في جذبِ الحديدِ فرآه .. تعجَّبَ مِنْ ذلكَ في أَوَّلِ رؤيتِهِ وقضى بأنَّهُ ممَّا يخالف العاداتِ ، فما الذي يؤمنكم أنَّ مدَّعي النبوَّةِ اطَّلع على علم مِنَ العلوم وظهرَ لهُ مِنْ أسرار الموجوداتِ ما إذا أتى به لمن لا يعرفُ ذلك .. عده خارقاً ؟ والجواب : أنَّا إِنَّما نستدل بالخارقِ إذا علمنا أنَّهُ مِنْ قبيل المعجزاتِ ، ونحن نعلم قطعاً أنَّ إحياء الموتى ، وقلبَ العصاحية ، وإبراء الأكمه والأبرص مِنْ غيرِ معاناة .. ليسَ ممَّا يدخل تحتَ الحِيَلِ ، ولا ممَّا يُتوصَّلُ إليه بغوص في هذه العلومِ . وقد تقترن بالشيء قرائن تفيد العلم واليقين بأنَّ ما أتى بهِ ليسَ مِنَ القبيل الذي ذكر تُموهُ ، وقد طردَ الله عادتهُ في حقٌّ أنبيائِهِ وأصفيائِهِ بأَنَّهُ يقطع عنهم الوهم ببعدهم عن أرباب هذه العلوم ؛ فشخص يخرج إلى شعب شَعِيب بحيثُ لا يُتوهم فيهِ مخالطة السحرة ، وآخرُ يخلقُهُ أُميّاً يمنعُهُ مِنَ المخالطة لأرباب العلوم وتعلُّم الكتب ، ﴿ وَمَا كُنتَ نَتْلُوا مِن قبْلِهِ مِن كِتَبٍ وَلَا تَخُطُرُ بِيَمِينِكَ إِذَا لَاَرْتَابَ الْمُبْطِلُونَ ﴾ [العنكبوت : ٤٨] ، فقرائن الصدق المقترنة .. مما يرفعُ اللَّبْسَ والمخالطونَ للأنبياءِ الباحثون عن أحوالهم والساعون في إبطال دعواهم.. يجدونَ مِنْ أحوالهم ما يحيلُ نسبتهم إلى ذلك ، حتى ينتهوا إلى البوح بأنَّهم في عنادٍ في إنكارِ نبوتهم وجحدهم ، هذا مع في نفوس الأعداء والحسدةِ ما يُحرِّكُ الدواعي إلى البحث والتفتيش ، والعادةُ تحيلُ أنْ يكونَ لشخص نسبة إلى ما ذكروه إلا أنَّ
Regarding frequency or rarity, and because this rare type [of phenomenon] is from the customary, and does not indicate anything... The Brahmins objected to this condition, saying: What the sages have attained of sciences has become established in the minds of the rational, such as talismans and types of tricks, like pulling a heavy object with a light one; and wonders have become famous among the secrets of existent things, such that one who does not know the property of a magnet in attracting iron, when he sees it, marvels at it in his first sight and judges that it is contrary to customs. So what assures you that a claimant of prophethood has come upon a science from among the sciences, and the secrets of existent things have become manifest to him such that when he brings it to one who does not know that, he would count it as extraordinary? The answer: We only use the extraordinary as evidence when we know that it is from the category of miracles; and we know with certainty that reviving the dead, turning a stick into a serpent, curing the blind and the leprous without treatment, are not things that fall under tricks, nor are they attained by delving into these sciences. And there may accompany a thing indications (qara'in) that provide knowledge and certainty that what he brought is not of the kind they claim. And God has made it His custom regarding His prophets and chosen ones that He cuts off from them the illusion [of being associated with such sciences] by their being far from the practitioners of these sciences: a person goes out to a remote mountain pass such that it is not suspected that he associates with sorcerers; another He creates illiterate, preventing him from associating with practitioners of sciences and learning books. {And you did not recite before it any book, nor did you inscribe it with your right hand; then the falsifiers would have doubted.} [al-Ankabut: 48]. So the indications of truth that accompany [a miracle] remove the confusion. And those who associate with prophets, who investigate their conditions and strive to refute their claims, find from their conditions that which precludes attributing [their acts] to that, until they end up admitting that they are stubborn in denying their prophethood and rejecting them. This is despite the fact that in the souls of enemies and the envious there is what moves incentives to investigation and scrutiny, and custom precludes that a person could have an association with what they mentioned except that...
Source pages p. 552

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. Sanusi first presents the Brahmins' objection: they argue that natural sciences produce wonders that appear extraordinary to the uninformed, such as magnets attracting iron; similarly, a claimant to prophethood could exploit obscure natural knowledge to deceive people. Thus, how can we be sure that an alleged miracle is not just a natural trick? Sanusi's answer is structured in several steps. First, he asserts that we only accept an extraordinary event as evidence when we know it belongs to the category of miracles. He gives examples: reviving the dead, turning a stick into a serpent, curing the blind and leprous without treatment — these are clearly beyond any trick or hidden science. Second, he explains that there are contextual indications (qara'in) that provide certainty: God's custom is that prophets are kept away from such sciences. He gives two concrete examples: a prophet who goes out to a remote mountain pass where he cannot be suspected of associating with sorcerers, and the Prophet Muhammad's illiteracy — he could not have learned such things from books, as confirmed by the Qur'anic verse. Third, he notes that even the enemies of prophets, who investigate them thoroughly, find that their conditions preclude any natural explanation, and they only deny out of stubbornness. Finally, he argues that human nature would drive enemies to expose any trick, yet they find none. The passage ends with an incomplete clause ('illa anna...'), likely introducing a further point about their being confounded. Sanusi thus defends the epistemological uniqueness of miracles against naturalistic reduction.

معجزة
براهمة
قرائن الصدق
حيل
سحر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any unusual phenomenon could be a miracle.
Correction: Miracles are distinguished by their unique content (e.g., reviving the dead) and by contextual proofs (qara'in) that show the prophet could not have used trickery.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the Brahmins' objection that Sanusi answers?
A) That prophets are not protected from error.
B) That alleged miracles might actually be tricks based on hidden natural sciences.
C) That the Qur'an is not a miracle.
D) That magic is not real.

Key Takeaway
True miracles are distinguished from natural wonders by their content (e.g., reviving the dead) and by the contextual proofs (qara'in) that accompany the prophet, such as his illiteracy or isolation from sorcerers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a claim about something miraculous today—whether from a preacher, a friend, or a video—do you check its content and context as Sanusi teaches, or are you easily impressed by the unusual? What would it take for you to demand the same rigor for yourself?
?
Sanusi says even enemies of the prophets investigated and found no trick, yet they denied out of stubbornness. Is there an area of your life where you have all the evidence but refuse to act, perhaps because submission would be uncomfortable? What are you protecting by your hesitation?
?
The Prophet's illiteracy was a sign that his knowledge came from God alone—not from study. Are you willing to admit that some truths in your life may come from sources you do not control or understand, and that your pride in 'knowing' might be blocking you from deeper certainty?

Defining Magic and Distinguishing It from Miracles
Sanusi defines sihr and distinguishes it from miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of magic (sihr) according to Ibn 'Arafa, its distinction from miracles, and the nuance raised by al-Qarafi.

Core Idea
Magic is extraordinary but bound to a cause; miracles are not.

Sanusi continues his discussion of extraordinary events by clarifying the boundary between miracles and magic. While both are extraordinary, magic follows a consistent customary cause, whereas a miracle transcends all natural causality. To ground this distinction, he cites the precise definition of magic by the Maliki scholar Ibn 'Arafa and notes a dissenting view from al-Qarafi. Understanding this difference is essential for appreciating the unique status of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic text and translation
Read the passage once to grasp the core distinction: magic has a customary cause linked to it, unlike a miracle.
Focus on the phrase 'mutarid al-irtibat' — consistent connection.
2
Analyze Ibn 'Arafa's definition
Notice that he calls magic 'extraordinary' (khariq lil-'ada) but adds 'consistent in its connection.' This captures both its marvel and its regularity.
This distinguishes magic from mere trickery.
3
Consider al-Qarafi's objection
Al-Qarafi claims magic is not truly extraordinary; its strangeness is due to a hidden cause. Compare this with Ibn 'Arafa's view.
Sanusi presents both views neutrally.
4
Reflect on theological implications
Why does this distinction matter? Miracles confirm prophethood; magic does not. Understanding the difference protects the unique status of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا تعرفُ الفرقَ بينَ المعجزة والسحرِ ؛ وهو أَنَّ السحر لهُ سبب عادي يرتبط بهِ ، بخلاف المعجزة ، ولهذا عرَّفَ الشيخ ابنُ عرفة السحر بقولِهِ : أمر خارق للعادة ، مُطْرِدُ الارتباطِ به ، قال : وزَعْمُ القرافي أَنَّهُ غيرُ خارق للعادة ، وغرابتُهُ إِنَّما هو بسبب خاص للمبالغة في البعد.
And by this you know the difference between the miracle (mu'jiza) and magic (sihr). It is that magic has a customary cause (sabab 'adi) connected to it, unlike the miracle. For this reason, Shaykh Ibn 'Arafa defined magic as: an extraordinary matter (amr khariq lil-'ada), consistent in its connection (mutarid al-irtibat bihi). He said: 'And al-Qarafi's claim that it is not extraordinary, but rather its strangeness is due to a special cause for exaggeration in concealment.'
Source pages p. 553

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification. Sanusi introduces it by stating that from the preceding discussion the difference between a miracle and magic is known. He gives the core distinction: magic has a customary cause (sabab 'adi) that is consistently linked to it, whereas a miracle has no such cause. He then cites the definition of magic by the Maliki scholar Ibn 'Arafa: magic is an extraordinary matter (khariq lil-'ada) that is consistent in its connection (mutarid al-irtibat) — meaning it regularly occurs when its cause is present. This distinguishes magic from miracles, which are not bound by any customary cause. Sanusi then notes that al-Qarafi disputed this, claiming magic is not truly extraordinary; its apparent strangeness is only because its cause is particularly obscure or hidden. Sanusi presents this alternate view without further comment, leaving the reader with the standard definition that magic, while extraordinary in appearance, follows a discernible custom, unlike the miracle which is utterly unprecedented and uncaused by any natural means.

Key Terms
معجزة (miracle)
سحر (magic)
سبب عادي (customary cause)
خارق للعادة (extraordinary)
مطرد الارتباط (consistent connection)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think magic is not truly extraordinary, claiming it is merely trickery with no real effect.
Correction: Ibn 'Arafa's definition affirms that magic is truly extraordinary (khariq lil-'ada) but bound to a customary cause, distinguishing it from both miracles and ordinary trickery.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn 'Arafa's definition, what distinguishes magic from a miracle?
A) Magic is not extraordinary at all.
B) Magic has a customary cause that is consistently linked to it.
C) Magic is performed by prophets.
D) Magic is always evil.

Key Takeaway
Magic is an extraordinary event that follows a consistent customary cause, unlike a miracle which has no natural cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness an unusual event, do you immediately search for a natural explanation, or are you open to the possibility of a miracle? How does the definition of magic in this lesson expose your bias?
?
If a claim of a miracle were presented to you, would you know how to distinguish it from mere magic? What personal experiences or assumptions might cloud your judgment? Commit to applying the criterion from this lesson before dismissing or accepting such claims.
?
Think of a time you attributed a blessing to coincidence or luck rather than divine intervention. How does the reality that miracles have no customary cause challenge you to see God's hand more clearly in your life?

Refutation of the Claim that Miracles Are Prevented on Saints
Sanusi refutes a claim that the Prophet's statement prevents miracles on saints.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the objection that the Prophet's statement prevents miracles on saints by clarifying the restricted nature of the challenge.

Core Idea
The Prophet's challenge only targets opponents and liars, not saints.

This lesson addresses a common objection against miracles of saints. Some argue that the Prophet's statement 'No one will come with the like of what I have brought' prohibits any miracle from appearing on a saint. Sanusi responds by showing that the challenge is specifically restricted to those who intend to oppose or fabricate, not to sincere followers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the objectors claim that the Prophet's statement prevents miracles on saints to avoid disconfirmation.
Notice the reasoning: if a saint does a miracle, people might think it disconfirms the Prophet.
2
Understand Sanusi's refutation
Sanusi says the challenge is restricted to those who intend to oppose or fabricate. Saints do not fall into that category.
He uses the term 'مقيّد' (restricted) to limit the scope.
3
See the supporting evidence
Sanusi adds that if a miracle appears on another prophet, it does not impair the original prophet's miracle by consensus.
This strengthens his point that the restriction is about intent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهؤلاء يزعمون أن قول النبي : ( لا يأتي أحد بمثل ما أتيت به ) يمنع من وقوع شيء من معجزات الأنبياء على أيدي الأولياء؛ لئلا يؤدي إلى تكذيب من ثبت صدقه، وهذا مندفع؛ فإن تحدِّيَ النبي مقيَّد بأنه لا يظهر ما أتى به على يد من يبغي معارضته ومناقضته، ولا على يد مفتر كذاب، ويدل على هذا التقييد: أن ظهور ما أتى به على يد نبي آخر لا يقدح في معجزته اتفاقاً.
And these claim that the Prophet's statement: 'No one will come with the like of what I have brought' prevents the occurrence of anything from the miracles of prophets upon the hands of saints, so that it does not lead to the disconfirmation of one whose truthfulness is established. And this is repelled; for indeed the challenge of the Prophet is restricted to that it does not appear what he brought upon the hand of one who intends to oppose and contradict him, nor upon the hand of a lying fabricator. And this restriction is indicated by the fact that the appearance of what he brought upon the hand of another prophet does not impair his miracle by consensus.
Source pages p. 555

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objectors claim that the Prophet's statement (no one will come with the like of what I brought) prevents any miracle from occuring on saints, otherwise people might mistake a saint's extraordinary act for a miracle and thus deny the Prophet's truth. Sanusi refutes this by pointing out that the challenge (tahaddi) is specifically restricted to those who intend to oppose the Prophet or fabricate lies. Saints, who are followers, do not fall into that category. Moreover, if a miracle appears on another prophet, it does not impair the original prophet's miracle by scholarly consensus. Thus the objection is invalid.

Key Terms
تحدّي
مقيّد
معجزة
تكذيب
اتفاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, to whom is the Prophet's challenge restricted?
A) To all people including saints
B) To those who intend to oppose or fabricate
C) To other prophets only
D) To disbelievers only

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes the objection by showing that the Prophet's challenge is limited to opponents and liars, so it does not prevent miracles on saints.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The Prophet's challenge is restricted to those who intend to oppose. Are you, in your heart, a sincere follower or a hidden opponent? What would it take for you to fully submit to what you know is true?

Karamat: Extraordinary Acts of Saints
Sanusi states the muhaqqiqun position on karamat and distinguishes them from miracles and magic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the position of the verifiers (muhaqqiqun) regarding karamat and how they distinguish karamat from miracles and magic.

Core Idea
Karamat are extraordinary acts granted to saints, confirming the truth of prophethood.

This passage from Sanusi's Umm al-Barahin presents the orthodox Sunni stance on the extraordinary acts of saints (awliya). It clarifies that such acts are permissible and distinguishes them from prophetic miracles and from magic. The key difference lies in the claim of prophethood and the moral character of the person.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very concise style here.
2
Identify the three types of extraordinary events
The passage discusses miracles (mu'jizat), karamat, and magic (sihr). Identify the defining feature of each according to the text.
3
Understand the role of following the prophet
The saint's karamat occur by virtue of following the prophet. Consider how this makes karamat a confirmation of prophethood, not a rival claim.
This is a key point: karamat support the prophet, not the saint.
4
Compare the distinctions
Contrast the basis for distinguishing karamat from miracles (claim of prophethood) and from magic (righteousness vs. sinfulness).
The passage gives two distinct criteria.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومذهب المحققين: جواز وقوع الخوارق كلها على يد الولي باختياره وبغير اختياره، وأن الفرق بينها وبين المعجزة ما قدمناه أولاً من دعوى النبوة وعدمها، والولي إنما يظهر على يده ما يظهر من الكرامات ببركة متابعته للرسول والاقتداء به، فهو أحق بالدلالة على صدق المتبوع وعاضد له. وأما الفرق بين الكرامة والسحر: فهو أن الكرامة ظهور الخارق على يد عبد ظاهر الصلاح، بخلاف السحر؛ فإن الخارق فيه إنما يظهر على أيدي الكفرة والفساق.
And the school of the verifiers is: the permissibility of the occurrence of all extraordinary things upon the hand of the saint, whether by his choice or without his choice, and that the difference between them and the miracle is what we previously mentioned first from the claim of prophethood and its absence. And the saint only appears upon his hand what appears of karamat by the blessing of his following the messenger and his emulation of him, so it is more deserving of indicating the truthfulness of the followed and is a support for him. And as for the difference between karamat and magic: it is that karamat is the appearance of the extraordinary upon the hand of a servant who is apparently righteous, unlike magic; for the extraordinary in it only appears upon the hands of disbelievers and sinners.
Source pages p. 555

Commentary
This passage presents the position of the verifiers (muhaqqiqun) regarding extraordinary acts (kharq al-'ada). They affirm that all such acts can occur on saints (awliya), whether voluntary or involuntary. The distinction between a miracle (mu'jiza) and a karamat is based on whether the person claims prophethood; saints do not claim it, while prophets do. The saint's karamat occur because of his following the prophet, thereby confirming the prophet's truth. Then Sanusi distinguishes karamat from magic (sihr): karamat appears on a servant who is outwardly righteous, while magic appears on disbelievers and sinners.

Misconception: That karamat and miracles are the same kind of event.
Correction: Karamat differ from miracles because the saint does not claim prophethood, whereas the miracle is a proof of prophethood.

Karamat are extraordinary acts that occur through saints who follow prophets, confirming the prophet's truth, and are distinct from magic by the righteousness of the saint.

Definition of Karamat and Its Conditions
Sanusi defines karamat with exclusions and explains irhas and tahaddi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of karamat (wonder of a saint) and how it is distinguished from miracles, magic, istidraj, and irhas.

Core Idea
Karamat is a wonder worked on a righteous non-prophet, not challenged beforehand.

In Islamic theology, karamat are extraordinary events granted to saints. But not every extraordinary event is a karamat. This lesson presents Sanusi's definition and explains each condition, showing how karamat differs from miracles of prophets, deceptive acts of the wretched, and signs preceding prophethood. By understanding these distinctions, you will avoid common confusions.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic definition of karamat and its literal translation. Notice the three key restrictions: being righteous, not a prophet, and not a prophet in the future.
2
Identify the four exclusions
The definition excludes magic/istidraj, miracles, irhas, and challenged wonders. For each, understand what it is and why it is excluded.
3
Understand irhas and tahaddi
Irhas are signs before prophethood; tahaddi is a challenge. The text explains that a karamat should not be preceded by a challenge that would make it like a miracle.
Note the derivation of irhas from 'rahs' meaning foundation.
4
Reflect on the claim of sainthood
The passage mentions a disagreement about claiming sainthood. Consider why this matters in defining karamat.
This is a secondary point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحد بعضهم الكرامة فقال: هي عبارة عن ظهور خارق للعادة على يد عبد ظاهر الصلاح ليس بنبي في الحال ولا في المآل. فخرج بقوله: (على يد عبد ظاهر الصلاح) السحر والاستدراج؛ وهو خلق الخارق على يد الأشقياء؛ كالدجال وفرعون والجهلة الضالين المضلين. وبقوله: (ليس بنبي) خرجت المعجزة. وبقوله: (لا في الحال ولا في المآل) خرج الإرهاص؛ وهو عبارة عن العلامات الدالة على بعثة نبي قبل بعثه؛ كالنور الذي كان يظهر في جبين عبد المطلب، مأخوذ من الرهص بكسر الراء؛ وهو أساس الحائط، فأطلق على هذه العلامات الإرهاص؛ لأنها تأسيس لقاعدة النبوة. قوله: (كدعوى الولاية) يعني: على القول بجواز ادعائها وفيه خلاف. وبقوله: (مُتحدّى به قبل وقوعه) أي: يقول: آية صدقي كذا، مما وقع بدون تحديه؛ كالإرهاص ونحوه، أو تحدى به لكن بعد وجوده. التحدي: هو طلب المعارضة، وأصله في الحدا[؟]: أن يتبارى.
And some of them defined karamat and said: It is an expression for the appearance of an extraordinary habit upon the hand of a servant who is apparently righteous, not a prophet at the present nor in the future. So by his statement: 'upon the hand of a servant who is apparently righteous' there is excluded magic and istidraj; and istidraj is the creation of the extraordinary upon the hand of the wretched, such as the Dajjal, Pharaoh, and the ignorant misleaders. And by his statement: 'not a prophet' there is excluded the miracle. And by his statement: 'not at the present nor in the future' there is excluded irhas; and irhas is an expression for the signs indicating the mission of a prophet before his mission, like the light that used to appear on the forehead of 'Abd al-Muttalib, derived from rahs (with kasr of ra') which is the foundation of a wall, so these signs are called irhas because they are the establishment of the basis of prophethood. His statement: (like the claim of sainthood) means: according to the position on the permissibility of claiming it, and there is disagreement about it. And by his statement: (challenged with before its occurrence) meaning: he says: the sign of my truthfulness is such and such, from what occurred without his challenge, like irhas and the like, or he challenged with it but after its existence. Al-tahaddi is the request for opposition, and its origin in al-huda[؟] is that they compete.
Source pages p. 555

Commentary
This passage provides a formal definition of karamat (wonder of a saint) and then explains each condition. The definition has three key restrictions: 1) The person must be outwardly righteous (thereby excluding magic and istidraj, which occur on wretched people). 2) The person must not be a prophet (excluding miracles). 3) The person must not be a prophet either now or in the future (excluding irhas, which are signs occurring before prophethood). The text then comments on two additional points from the source being explained: the claim of sainthood (some permit claiming it, with disagreement), and the condition that the karamat should not be preceded by a challenge (tahaddi). If the saint says 'my sign is such and such' before it occurs, it becomes a form of challenge, which is characteristic of miracles. Tahaddi is defined as a request for opposition. This guards against confusing karamat with challenged miracles.

خارق للعادة
صلاح
استدراج
إرهاص
تحدّي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think irhas is a type of karamat.
Correction: Irhas occurs before prophethood and indicates a future prophet, while karamat occurs on a non-prophet after prophethood has started.

Checkpoint

A) Miracle

Key Takeaway

Definition and Conditions of Tahaddi
Sanusi defines tahaddi and explains its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of tahaddi (challenge) and its necessary conditions for a miracle.

Core Idea
Tahaddi is the prophet's claim that a specific sign proves his truthfulness.

Sanusi now turns to explain the concept of tahaddi, which is the challenge that accompanies a miracle. Understanding what constitutes a valid challenge is crucial for distinguishing a true miracle from other extraordinary events. In this passage, he defines tahaddi and clarifies that it does not require an explicit statement of impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall meaning, then read the translation to understand the details.
Notice the parenthetical expressions and the footnote marker.
2
Identify the definition of tahaddi
Find where Sanusi explicitly defines tahaddi and note its linguistic origin.
The definition starts with 'التحدي : هو طلب المعارضة'.
3
Understand the condition about the prophet's statement
Sanusi states that it is not a condition for tahaddi that the prophet says 'no one can bring its like'. Instead, it suffices to say 'my sign is that Allah does such-and-such'.
This clarifies a common misunderstanding.
4
Reflect on the evidence from fulfillment
Consider why the fulfillment of the claimed sign is evidence of truthfulness.
The passage concludes with this point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبقوله : ( مُتحدّى بهِ  َقبلَ وقوعِهِ ) أي : يقولُ : آيةُ صدقي كذا ، مما وقع بدونِ تحدّيه ؛ كالإرهاص ونحوه ، أو تحدى به لكن بعد وجودِهِ . التحدي : هو طلب المعارضة ، وأصلُه في الحدا(۳) : أنْ يتبارى فيه الحاديانِ ، ويُقالُ : تحدَّيْتُ فلاناً ؛ إذا ماريته ونازعتُهُ الغلبة وهو هنا عبارة عن قولِ النبي : ( آيةً صدقي كذا ) ، وليسَ مِنْ شرط التحدّي أنْ يقول : لا يأتي أحدٌ بمثلها ، بل يكفي أن يقول : ( آيتي : أنْ يفعلَ الله كذا ( فيفعلُهُ لهُ ، فإجابة دعواه دليلٌ على صدقه في مقالتِهِ .
And by his saying: 'challenged with it before its occurrence' means he says: 'The sign of my truthfulness is such-and-such', which occurred without his challenge, like irhas and the like, or he challenged with it but after its existence. Al-tahaddi (challenge) is the request for opposition; its origin in song is that two singers vie with each other, and it is said: 'I challenged so-and-so' when I contended and disputed with him for victory. It here is the prophet's saying: 'The sign of my truthfulness is such-and-such'. And it is not a condition of tahaddi that he says: 'No one can bring its like'; rather, it suffices that he says: 'My sign is that Allah does such-and-such,' and He does it for him. So the answering of his claim is evidence of his truthfulness in his statement.
Source pages p. 556

Commentary
This passage defines and explains tahaddi (the challenge) which is a condition for a miracle. The author begins by interpreting a phrase from the definition: 'challenged with it before its occurrence'. This means that the prophet declares his miracle, either something that occurred without prior challenge (like irhas) or something he challenges with after its occurrence. Tahaddi is literally 'request for opposition'; its root is from two singers competing. Here, it means the prophet's declaration: 'My sign of truth is such-and-such.' Importantly, the author clarifies that it is not a condition for tahaddi that the prophet explicitly says 'no one can bring its like.' It is sufficient for him to say 'my sign is that Allah does such-and-such' and then it happens. The fulfillment of his claim is evidence of his truthfulness.

دعوى النبوة

Misconception: One might think that tahaddi requires an explicit statement of impossibility of imitation.
Correction: The author clarifies that it suffices for the prophet to simply identify his miracle without adding a phrase like 'no one can bring its like'.

Checkpoint

B) The prophet's statement identifying his miracle
C) The people's request for a miracle

Key Takeaway
Tahaddi is the prophet's declaration of his miracle, and its fulfillment proves his truthfulness without needing an explicit impossibility clause.

Why Opposition Must Be Impossible for a Miracle to Be Specific
Sanusi explains why the miracle must be impossible to oppose to establish its specificity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the impossibility of opposition is a necessary condition for a miracle to be specific to the prophet, and why supernatural events before or without a challenge do not prove prophethood.

Core Idea
A miracle's uniqueness proves the prophet's truthfulness, not the challenge itself.

Sanusi explains a subtle but crucial point: the impossibility of opposing a miracle is not primarily for the sake of the challenge (tahaddi). Instead, it is to establish that the miracle is uniquely from the prophet (ikhtisas). Without this specificity, the miracle could be attributed to anyone, and it would not prove prophethood. This passage clarifies why certain supernatural events—like those before the claim or unchallenged ones—do not qualify as miracles.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key terms: ikhtisas (specificity), ta'addhur al-mu'arada (impossibility of opposition), khariq li'l-'ada (breaking of custom), and da'wa (claim).
Pay attention to the phrase 'la li-ajli al-tahaddi, bal li-ajli thubuti al-ikhtisas'.
2
Understand the necessity of specificity
Reflect on why a miracle must be specific to the prophet. Sanusi argues that without specificity, the miracle would not prove his truthfulness. The impossibility of opposition ensures that no one else can produce the same act.
Think about why a supernatural event before the claim (like irhas) is not specific.
3
Analyze the cases of non-specific miracles
The passage mentions two cases: a supernatural event before the claim, and a supernatural event after the claim but not challenged. In both, the event does not establish specificity. Work through why each case fails the condition.
The key is that without the challenge (tahaddi), the event could be attributed to others.
4
Apply the lesson to the larger argument
Understand how this nuance defends the prophetic miracles from counterexamples. This is a fine point in kalam that strengthens the proof of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم ؛ تعذر صدورِها مِنْ مثلِهِ إِذا كانَ يبغي معارضته . . لا بدَّ منه ، لا لأجل التحدّي ، بل لأجل ثبوتِ الاختصاص ؛ فإنَّ المعجزة لا بدَّ أن تكونَ مُختصَّةً بالنبيَّ ، ولهذا شُرِطَ أنْ تكونَ خارقة للعادةِ واقعةً على وَفْقِ دعواه ؛ فإنَّ المعتاد وما لا تسبقه الدعوى مِنَ الخوارق .. لا اختصاص لهُ بهِ ، وإذا كانَ لا بدَّ مِنَ الاختصاص فالخارقُ الواقع قبل الدعوى تتساوى فيه الأقوال ، وتتكافأ فيه الدعاوي ، وكذا الواقع بعد دعوى الرسالة ، ولكن لم يتحدَّ بهِ أصلاً
Yes; the impossibility of its issuance from someone like him – if he intends to oppose it – is necessary, not for the sake of the challenge, but for the sake of establishing specificity. For the miracle must be specific to the prophet. For this reason it is conditioned that it be a breaking of custom occurring in accordance with his claim. For that which is customary and that which is supernatural not preceded by the claim have no specificity to him. And if specificity is necessary, then a supernatural event occurring before the claim – all statements are equal regarding it, and all claims are equivalent in it. And likewise that which occurs after the claim of prophethood but was not challenged with at all.
Source pages p. 557

Commentary
This passage justifies a key condition for a miracle: the impossibility of opposition. The author states that the impossibility (ta'addhur) of someone like the prophet producing the same miracle is necessary, but not for the sake of the challenge itself. Rather, it is needed to prove that the miracle is specific to the prophet (ikhtisas). A miracle must be specific to the prophet to demonstrate his truthfulness. If a supernatural event is ordinary (mu'tad) or not preceded by a claim, it cannot be specifically attributed to him. Similarly, if a supernatural event occurs before the claim of prophethood (like irhas), it does not prove specificity because all claimants are equal in that case. The same applies to a supernatural event that occurs after the claim but was never challenged with. Without the impossibility of opposition, the event cannot be established as the prophet's exclusive miracle.

Key Terms
الاختصاص
تعذر المعارضة
خارق للعادة
الدعوى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The impossibility of opposition is necessary for the challenge (tahaddi) to be effective.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies it is for establishing specificity (ikhtisas), not for the challenge. The challenge is a secondary aspect; the primary purpose is to show that the miracle is uniquely from the prophet.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the impossibility of opposition necessary?
A) To make the challenge effective
B) To establish that the miracle is specific to the prophet
C) To prove the miracle is supernatural
D) To satisfy the condition of tahaddi

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of opposition is necessary to prove that the miracle is uniquely from the prophet, not merely to issue a challenge.

The Condition of Correspondence in Opposing Miracles
Sanusi distinguishes the condition for opposing specified vs. unspecified miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between specified and unspecified miracles and whether correspondence is required when opposing each.

Core Idea
For specified miracles, correspondence is required; for unspecified, opinions differ.

Sanusi clarifies a nuanced point about miracles: if someone claims a specific miracle, an opponent must match it exactly. But if the miracle is not specified, the requirement of correspondence is disputed among scholars, with the stronger view being that it is not required. This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two cases
The passage distinguishes between a miracle claimed as a specific event and one left general. Identify which case is addressed first.
The author first addresses the specified case.
2
Identify the condition for specified miracles
For a specified miracle, the opponent must produce an exact correspondence. The text states this as a clear condition.
3
Examine the disagreement for unspecified miracles
For unspecified miracles, al-Amidi reports most Ash'aris stipulate correspondence, but al-Qadi holds it is not required. The author endorses al-Qadi's view.
Note the authorities cited.
4
Reflect on the reasoning
Consider why correspondence might not be necessary for unspecified miracles—perhaps because the opponent doesn't know what to match.
This connects to the logical principle behind the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم المعجزة : إنِ ادعِيَتْ مُعيَّنَةً فشرط المعارض مماثلته لها ، وإن كانَتْ غير مُعيَّنة (۱) فقال سيف الدين الآمدي : ( أكثر أصحابنا اشترط المماثلة، : والذي اختاره القاضي : أنَّ المماثلة غير مشترطة ، وهو الحق (۲) .
Then the miracle: if it is claimed as specified, then the condition for the opponent is its correspondence (mumathala) to it. And if it is unspecified, then Sayf al-Din al-Amidi said: 'Most of our companions stipulated correspondence, and what al-Qadi chose is that correspondence is not stipulated, and it is the truth.'
Source pages p. 557

Commentary
This passage addresses the condition of correspondence (mumathala) when someone tries to oppose a miracle. The author distinguishes two cases. First, if the miracle is claimed as specified (mu'ayyanah) – for example, 'my sign is the splitting of the moon' – then anyone who opposes must produce an event exactly matching that specified miracle. Second, if the miracle is unspecified (ghayr mu'ayyanah) – for example, 'my sign is that Allah breaks His custom tomorrow' without specifying what – then there is a difference of opinion. Sayf al-Din al-Amidi reports that most of the Ash'ari scholars stipulated correspondence even in unspecified miracles. However, al-Qadi (likely al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani) chose that correspondence is not stipulated in that case, and the author declares this to be the correct (al-haqq) view.

Key Terms
مماثلة
معينة
غير معينة
سيف الدين الآمدي
القاضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Correspondence is always required for any miracle challenge.
Correction: The passage shows it is not required for unspecified miracles.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the correct view regarding correspondence for unspecified miracles?
A) It is required
B) It is not required
C) It depends on the type of miracle
D) Only the opponent can decide

Key Takeaway
For a specified miracle, correspondence is mandatory; for an unspecified miracle, the stronger opinion is that it is not required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
