Why Challenge Condition Is Additional to Correspondence
Sanusi explains why the condition of challenge is not redundant even though the miracle must accompany the claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the condition of challenge (tahaddi) is not redundant even though the miracle must accompany the claim of prophethood.

Core Idea
A miracle must be both present and presented as a challenge.

Sanusi anticipates an objection: if a miracle already must accompany the claim, why also require a challenge? He answers with a subtle but crucial point: the two conditions are not identical. A miracle can be present without being actively challenged. This lesson unpacks that distinction and reinforces the need for a public challenge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then its literal translation. Notice the logical structure: the author states his reason for not dispensing with the challenge condition.
Pay attention to the key terms muqaranah and tahaddi.
2
Identify the two conditions
The passage mentions two conditions: (1) the miracle's concomitance with the claim (muqaranah), and (2) the challenge (tahaddi). Understand how they differ.
Concomitance means happening together; challenge means the prophet dares others to match it.
3
Understand the potential objection
Imagine someone saying: 'If the miracle must accompany the claim, then challenge is automatically present. So why add it?' This is the objection Sanusi responds to.
4
See Sanusi's answer
Sanusi explains that a miracle can accompany the claim without being challenged. For example, a prophet might perform a miracle privately or without explicitly saying 'bring something like it.'
The key phrase: 'li-annahu qad taqtarinu bi-da'wa al-risala wa la tuhadda biha.'
5
Reflect on why this matters
If the challenge is missing, the miracle does not serve as a public proof. Therefore, both conditions are needed for a miracle to be valid evidence of prophethood.
This shows the precision of kalam methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما لم أستغن بما قدَّمْتُ مِن اشتراطِ كون المعجزة مقارنة لدعوى الرسالة عن هذا الشرط ، وهو التحدي بها . . لأنها قد تقترن بدعوى الرسالة ولا تحدَّى بها .
And I did not dispense, by what I have presented of the condition that the miracle be concomitant with the claim of prophethood, with this condition, namely the challenge with it, because it (the miracle) may be concomitant with the claim of prophethood without being challenged with.
Source pages p. 557

Commentary
This passage explains why the condition of tahaddi (challenge) is additional to the condition that the miracle accompany the claim of prophethood. The author anticipates an objection: if the miracle already must accompany the claim (muqaranah li-da'wa al-risala), why also require tahaddi? He answers that it is possible for a miracle to accompany the claim but not be explicitly challenged with. In such a case, the miracle would not serve as proof because the prophet did not present it as a sign. Therefore, the condition of tahaddi is not redundant; it ensures that the miracle is actually used as evidence of truthfulness.

Key Terms
مقارنة
التحدي
دعوى الرسالة
الاستغناء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a miracle accompanies the claim, challenge is automatically present.
Correction: Challenge is a separate act; the miracle must be explicitly presented as a sign for it to serve as proof.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the condition of challenge is not redundant given the condition of concomitance?
A) Because challenge is more important
B) Because a miracle may accompany the claim but not be challenged with
C) Because challenge is a separate condition from al-Amidi
D) Because the claim itself implies challenge

Key Takeaway
The condition of challenge (tahaddi) is necessary because a miracle may accompany the claim without being presented as a challenge, so it would not serve as proof.

Objection and Answer on Prophethood and Miracles After Death
Objection-and-answer on posthumous miracles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi answers al-Qadi's objection that a miracle after death cannot confirm prophethood because prophethood requires address which is impossible after death.

Core Idea
Miracles after death confirm past prophethood, not new address.

Al-Qadi raises a logical puzzle: if prophethood means receiving divine address, and that ends at death, how can a posthumous miracle prove prophethood? Sanusi's answer shows that the miracle serves as evidence for the prophet's past claim, not for a present address. This lesson unpacks the objection and its elegant resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: objection then answer.
2
Identify al-Qadi's objection
Al-Qadi argues that prophethood is about divine address (khiṭāb) to the messenger. After death, address is impossible, so a miracle at that time cannot prove prophethood. Understand why he thinks this is a problem.
He assumes the miracle must correspond to a present claim.
3
Trace Sanusi's response
Sanusi answers that the miracle confirms the prophet's previous claim of prophethood. It does not require new address. The impossibility of address at the time of the miracle does not invalidate the evidence.
The miracle points back to the past claim.
4
Note the analogy with delay during life
Sanusi notes that delaying a miracle to a later time during the prophet's life is allowed, so delaying it to after death is also logically permissible. This strengthens the answer.
Consistency in allowing delayed miracles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القاضي رضيَ اللهُ عنهُ : فقد يحتج بأنَّ الرسالة مرجعها إلى تعلق الخطاب بالرسول ، وذلك ممتنع بعد الموتِ ، فكيف تكونُ الآية لا تتحقق إلا في وقتِ امتناع ما هي آيةٌ عليه ؟!
ورُدَّ : بأَنَّهُ تَبَيَّنَ بعد موتِهِ أَنَّهُ كانَ مُخاطَباً بتبليغ ما بلَّغَهُ ، ولا يضرُّ امتناع تعلُّقِ الخطاب عند وجودِ الآية ؛ فإنَّها تدلُّ على ما سبقَ مِنْ دعواه ، وقد جوّزنا تأخير الآيةِ إلى زمن مضروب في حال الحياة ، فيتّجه أن يتأخَّرَ إلى أجل مضروب بعد الوفاة ، ويتبيَّنُ بذلكَ صدقُهُ في الدعوى السابقة
And as for al-Qāḍī (may Allah be pleased with him): he might argue that prophethood’s reference is to the attachment of the address (khiṭāb) to the messenger, and that is impossible after death. So how can the miracle (āyah) occur only at a time when that of which it is a sign is impossible?! It was responded: that after his death it becomes clear that he was addressed with conveying what he conveyed. And the impossibility of the attachment of the address at the time of the miracle does not harm; for it indicates his previous claim. And we have permitted delaying the miracle to a designated time during life, so it is fitting that it be delayed to a designated time after death, and thereby his truthfulness in the previous claim is made evident.
Source pages p. 559

Commentary
This passage records an objection from al-Qadi and the answer to it. The objection is based on the nature of prophethood: prophethood essentially involves divine address (khiṭāb) directed to the prophet. After death, such address is impossible because the prophet can no longer receive it. So how can a miracle (which serves as proof of prophethood) appear after the prophet’s death, at a time when prophethood itself (as defined by address) no longer exists? The answer: the miracle after death does not require new address; it simply confirms that the prophet was indeed addressed during his life (i.e., he truly was a prophet). The miracle points back to his past claim. Since delaying miracles during the prophet’s life is allowed, delaying them until after death is also logically permissible. The truth of his claim becomes evident when the miracle appears posthumously.

Key Terms
القاضي
الرسالة
الخطاب
الآية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is that miracles after death are impossible in themselves, or that they would be pointless if prophethood has ended.
Correction: Sanusi shows that posthumous miracles are not pointless; they serve as evidence for the prophet's past claim, not for a present address.

Checkpoint
What is the core of al-Qadi's objection in this passage?
A) Miracles after death are impossible because the prophet is dead
B) Prophethood requires continuous divine address, which is impossible after death, so a miracle after death would be meaningless
C) The miracle must appear at the same time as the claim
D) The miracle cannot be delayed at all

Key Takeaway
A posthumous miracle confirms the truth of the prophet's past claim of prophethood, not a present divine address, thus resolving the logical difficulty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you rely on the Prophet's miracles—like the Qur'an—as proof of his truthfulness even though you never saw them firsthand? How does that affect your certainty that he was sent by Allah? Will you commit to studying one miracle in depth this week to reinforce your conviction?
?
If a miracle after death can confirm a past claim, then the Prophet's message remains alive even after his departure. Are you living as if his message is still addressing you today, or have you treated it as a historical relic? What would change if you truly believed his words are as relevant now as when he first spoke them?
?
The objection in this lesson assumes prophethood ends with death, but the answer shows its truth endures beyond death. Is there an area of your faith where you act as if it's only for this life, not for the Hereafter? How can you realign your priorities to reflect that your deeds are witnessed even after you die?

Objection and answer: posthumous miracles and karamat
Al-Qadi argues that permitting posthumous miracles nullifies karamat; Sanusi answers by distinguishing certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the possibility of posthumous miracles does not undermine the category of karamat, because karamat are not definitive proofs of sainthood.

Core Idea
Karamat are never certain proofs; they could be istidrāj or from enemies.

This lesson examines a key objection to allowing posthumous miracles: that it would erase the distinction between karamat and mu‘jizat. Al-Qadi raises this concern, and Sanusi responds by clarifying the nature of evidence from karamat. The response reinforces the Sunni position that karamat are not definitive proofs, even without this objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the flow of argument. Note how al-Qadi's objection is stated and how Sanusi answers.
The answer concedes the logical point but reinterprets its significance.
2
Identify the concession
Find the sentence where Sanusi concedes that karamat are not definitive proofs. Understand why this concession is harmless to the Sunni position.
The concession actually aligns with the existing Sunni caution regarding karamat.
3
Consider the extra factor: istidrāj
Notice that even without the posthumous miracle objection, karamat are already uncertain due to the possibility of istidrāj (divine enticement). This is why early Muslims feared karamat rather than trusting them.
This reinforces that the objection does not introduce a new problem.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما احتج القاضي : بأنَّ القولَ بذلك يؤدّي إلى إبطال الكرامةِ ؛ إذْ ما مِنْ كرامة إلا ويجوز على هذا أن تكون معجزةً لنبي تأخَرَتْ بعد وفاتِهِ.
وأُجيبَ : بأنَّ غايتَه بطلان كون الكرامة دليلاً قطعياً على ولايةِ مَنْ ظهرَتْ على يديهِ ؛ لتطرُّقِ هذا الاحتمالِ فيها ، ولا ريبَ أَنَّا نقولُ بمُوجَبِهِ ؛ فإنَّ دلالةَ الكرامة على الولاية ليسَتْ قطعيَّةً ولو أَمِنَّا فِيهَا مِنْ هذا الاحتمالِ الذي ذُكِرَ ؛ لاحتمال كونها استدراجاً ، ويكونَ مَنْ ظهرَتْ على يديهِ مِنْ أهل عداوةِ اللهِ تعالى ، وممَّنْ سبقَ القضاء بأَنَّهُ لا يُختم له بالسعادة ، ولهذا كانَ الأوَّلونَ لا يثقون بها ، بل لا يزدادونَ معَها إلا خوفاً .
And perhaps al-Qāḍī argued: that the statement [permitting posthumous miracles] leads to nullifying the karamah (miracle of a saint); for there is no karamah except that on this view it could be a mu‘jizah (prophetic miracle) of a prophet that was delayed after his death. And it was answered: that its ultimate conclusion is the invalidity of the karamah being a definitive proof for the sainthood (wilāyah) of the one on whose hands it appeared, due to this probability entering into it. And there is no doubt that we say accordingly; for the indication of the karamah upon sainthood is not definitive, even if we are safe in it from this mentioned probability, due to the probability that it is an istidrāj (gradual enticement), and that the one on whose hands it appears is from the people of enmity to Allah Almighty, and from those over whom the decree has passed that he will not be sealed with happiness. For this reason, the early ones did not trust them, but rather only increased in fear with them.
Source pages p. 560

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection from al-Qadi and its answer. The objection is that if we allow miracles to appear after a prophet’s death, then any miracle of a saint (karamah) could potentially be interpreted as a delayed prophetic miracle (mu‘jizah). This would destroy the category of karamah as distinct from mu‘jizah. The answer concedes the point, but argues that it only shows that karamah is not a certain proof of sainthood (wilāyah). However, this is already the Sunni position: karamat are never definitive evidence because they could also be istidrāj (a divine enticement leading to punishment) or from an enemy of Allah. Thus, even without this specific objection, karamat are not relied upon as certain signs. The early righteous predecessors (al-awwalūn) did not place confidence in karamat but rather feared them. Hence, allowing posthumous miracles does not undermine any established certainty.

Key Terms
الكرامة
المعجزة
الولاية
الاستدراج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Karamat are definitive proofs of sainthood.
Correction: Sanusi states explicitly that the indication of karamah upon sainthood is not definitive (laysat qat‘iyyah), and even without the posthumous miracle objection, it could be istidrāj.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does Sanusi consider al-Qadi's objection harmless?
A) Because karamat are still definitive proofs of sainthood.
B) Because karamat were never definitive proofs due to the possibility of istidrāj and other factors.
C) Because posthumous miracles are impossible.
D) Because the objection is ignored.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni position holds that karamat, while possible, are never certain proofs of sainthood because they can be simulated by divine enticement (istidrāj).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Are you willing to examine your own heart and repent from relying on visible signs rather than the unseen reality of your relationship with Allah?

Objection and Answer: Preserving the Mission's Benefit
Sanusi rebuts al-Qadi's objection that delayed miracles waste the mission's benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi rebuts al-Qadi's objection that delaying miracles wastes the benefit of prophethood, by showing it only proves improbability and that preservation is possible through writing.

Core Idea
An objection based on practical difficulty does not prove logical impossibility.

Al-Qadi raises a third objection: if the proof of prophethood is delayed until after death, people during the prophet's life have no motivation to preserve his teachings, thus wasting the mission's benefit. Sanusi answers that this argument only shows improbability, not impossibility, and the law can be preserved in writing. He then adds a theological nuance about the permissibility of obligating the unbearable, which renders the objection moot.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection from al-Qadi: delaying the miracle until death may waste the benefit of the mission because people lack motivation to preserve the law.
Notice the practical concern: without a visible miracle during the prophet's life, followers may not be diligent in preserving teachings.
2
Examine the rebuttal's first part
Sanusi responds that the objection only shows improbability (isti‘bād) of preservation, not logical impossibility. Therefore, it does not prove that delaying a miracle is impermissible.
The rebuttal shifts the burden: improbability is not a proof of impossibility.
3
Consider the second rebuttal point
Sanusi adds that the law can be preserved through writing, which ensures its preservation after the prophet's death. This counters the objection practically.
Writing as a means of preservation was a known practice even in early Islamic history.
4
Understand the concluding condition
The rebuttal is based on the assumption that obligating what is unbearable (taklīf mā lā yuṭāq) is not permissible. If one permits it, the objection becomes irrelevant because Allah could obligate belief without any miracle.
This shows the depth of the argument: even if the objection were valid, it would only apply under the more restrictive theological position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج أيضاً للقاضي : بما أشرنا إليه في أصل العقيدةِ مِنْ أَنَّ تأخير ما يدلُّ على الرسالة إلى الوفاة قد تضيعُ معه فائدة البعثة ؛ وهي العلمُ بأحكامِ اللهِ تعالى ؛ لعدم وجودِ الباعثِ لهم عادةً على حفظ ذلك عنه
وهو مردود ; لأنَّ قصاراه استبعاد الحفظ لشرعه ، فلا يصلحُ أنْ يكون دليلاً على عدم الجواز ، على أَنَّهُ يمكن تدوينه على وجْهِ يتأَنَّى حفظُهُ بعدَ موتِهِ
هذا إن قلنا بأنَّ تكليف ما لا يُطاقُ غيرُ سائغ ، وأَمَّا إِنْ سَوَّعْناهُ فالأمرُ في ذلك واضح (۱) ، وبالله التوفيق .
And he also argued for al-Qāḍī: with what we pointed out in the creed that delaying the proof of prophethood until death may waste the benefit of the mission (al-bi‘thah), which is knowledge of the rulings of Allah Almighty, due to the customary lack of motivation for them to preserve that from him. And it is rebutted; because its ultimate conclusion is the improbability of the preservation of his law, so it does not suffice as evidence for impermissibility. Moreover, it can be written down in a manner that ensures its preservation after his death. This [is] if we say that obligating what is unbearable (taklīf mā lā yuṭāq) is not permissible. But if we permit it, then the matter is clear (1). And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 560

Commentary
This passage presents a third objection from al-Qadi and its answer, followed by a concluding remark about the principle of taklīf mā lā yuṭāq. The objection is practical: if the miracle (the proof of prophethood) is delayed until after the prophet's death, people during his life have no compelling reason to accept his message and preserve his teachings. Thus, the very purpose of sending a prophet – to teach people Allah's rulings – would be lost. The answer argues that this objection only shows improbability (isti‘bād), not impossibility, and the law can still be preserved by writing. The author then adds a further point: this entire discussion proceeds on the assumption that obligating what is unbearable is not permissible. But if one holds that Allah can obligate what is unbearable, then the objection becomes moot because Allah could simply demand belief in the prophet without any miracle at all. The footnote explains that in that case, obligation to believe without a miracle is conceivable. Thus, the objection is fully rebutted.

Key Terms
فائدة البعثة
حفظ الشريعة
تكليف ما لا يطاق
استبعاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The rebuttal must guarantee that the law will be preserved perfectly.
Correction: The rebuttal only needs to show that the objection does not prove logical impossibility; practical difficulties do not render something impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the rebuttal to al-Qadi's third objection?
A) The preservation of the law is impossible without a miracle
B) The objection only shows improbability, not impermissibility, and the law can be preserved in writing
C) The mission's benefit is irrelevant
D) The objection is valid

Key Takeaway
Al-Qadi's objection from loss of mission benefit is rebutted because it only shows improbability, not impossibility, and preservation can be achieved through writing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Second objection: loss of prophethood's benefit and its rebuttal
Sanusi refutes the second objection regarding lost benefit of prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the claim that delaying miracles would nullify the benefit of prophethood, showing that the objection only suggests improbability and that preservation through writing is possible.

Core Idea
Delayed miracles do not necessarily mean lost benefit; preservation can be arranged.

This passage tackles a second objection to the possibility of delayed miracles: if the miracle is only shown after the prophet's death, wouldn't the whole purpose of prophethood—conveying God's rulings—be lost? Sanusi responds by showing that the objection only establishes improbability, not impossibility, and that the message can be preserved in writing. He also points out that if one allows that God may obligate the impossible, the objection collapses entirely.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the reasoning: delaying the miracle until death would cause the benefit of the mission (knowledge of divine rulings) to be lost because people would have no usual motive to preserve the message.
The objection assumes that without a visible miracle during the prophet's lifetime, people would not be motivated to preserve the law.
2
Identify the first rebuttal point
Sanusi argues that the objection's strongest claim is that preservation is improbable, not impossible. Improbability does not constitute a proof of impossibility.
This is a key logical move: distinguishing between what is merely unlikely and what is impossible.
3
Recognize the second rebuttal point
Next, Sanusi points out that the message can be written down, allowing for its preservation after the prophet's death. This directly counters the idea that benefit would be lost.
Writing was a common practice even in the time of the prophet, so this is a practical solution.
4
Note the additional argument from taklif ma la yutaq
Finally, Sanusi mentions that if one holds the view that God can obligate what is beyond human capacity (taklif ma la yutaq), then the objection is even weaker: people could be obligated to believe without any miracle at all.
This is a conditional argument: it only applies if one accepts that theological position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج أيضاً للقاضي : بما أشرنا إليه في أصل العقيدةِ مِنْ أَنَّ تأخير ما يدلُّ على الرسالة إلى الوفاة قد تضيعُ معه فائدة البعثة ؛ وهي العلمُ بأحكامِ اللهِ تعالى ؛ لعدم وجودِ الباعثِ لهم عادةً على حفظ ذلك عنه . وهو مردود ; لأنَّ قصاراه استبعاد الحفظ لشرعه ، فلا وجود منهم يصلحُ أنْ يكون دليلاً على عدم الجواز ، على أَنَّهُ يمكن تدوينه على وجْهِ يتأَنَّى حفظُهُ بعدَ موتِهِ . هذا إن قلنا بأنَّ تكليف ما لا يُطاقُ غيرُ سائغ ، وأَمَّا إِنْ سَوَّعْناهُ فالأمرُ في ذلك واضح (۱) ، وبالله التوفيق . (۱) فحينئذٍ : يُتصوّر التكليف بتصديقه من غير إبراز معجزة .
He also argued on behalf of al-Qadi: by what we indicated in the principle of the creed, that delaying what indicates the message until death would cause the benefit of the mission to be lost with it; and it is knowledge of the rulings of Allah Almighty; due to the usual lack of a motive for them to preserve that from him. And it is rebutted; because its utmost is the improbability of the preservation of his law, so there is nothing from them that is suitable to be a proof for the impossibility, and that it is possible to write it down in a manner that its preservation can be arranged after his death. This if we say that taklif ma la yutaq (obligating what cannot be borne) is not permissible, but if we allow it, then the matter in that is clear (1). And with Allah is success. (1) In that case: one can conceive of the obligation to believe him without producing a miracle.
Source pages p. 560

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection from al-Qadi and its rebuttal. The objection is that if the miracle that proves prophethood is delayed until after the prophet's death, then the benefit of the prophetic mission—which is to convey God's rulings—would be lost, because people would have no motive to preserve the message. The answer rebuts: at most, this shows that it is improbable (not impossible) that the law would be preserved. But it does not prove impossibility; indeed, the message could be written down and preserved after the prophet's death. Additionally, if one holds that God may obligate what is beyond human capacity, then the objection is even weaker: one can be obligated to believe without any miracle at all (as the footnote explains).

Key Terms
فائدة البعثة
الحفظ
تكليف ما لا يطاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Without a miracle during the prophet's lifetime, people would have no reason to believe and the benefit of prophethood would be entirely lost.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the message can be preserved in writing, and that even without a miracle, belief may still be obligatory under the principle of taklif ma la yutaq.

Checkpoint
How does Sanusi rebut the objection that delayed miracles would cause the loss of the benefit of prophethood?
A) He argues that miracles are not needed for belief because prophethood is self-evident.
B) He says that preservation through writing is possible after the prophet's death.
C) He claims that the objection is invalid because the benefit is knowledge of God's existence, not rulings.
D) He asserts that the prophet's death is irrelevant to the benefit of the mission.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes the second objection by showing that preservation through writing is possible and that improbability does not equal impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a truth because it seemed improbable, just because preserving it required effort? How can you train yourself to distinguish between what is improbable and what is impossible, and what is at stake if you confuse them?
?
If God could obligate you to believe even without clear signs, do you truly believe from conviction or only when it's easy? What would change in your life if you took that responsibility seriously?
?
The objection assumes people need a miracle to preserve faith. When has a lack of immediate, dramatic proof caused you to neglect preserving the truth in your own heart? What steps can you take to write the truth down—literally or figuratively—so it is not lost?

Why Falsifying the Hand Miracle Does Not Harm the Proof
Sanusi refutes that falsifying the hand miracle harms the proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that falsifying a specific miracle like the hand does not undermine the proof of prophethood because the challenge was only in the utterance, not in the confirmation.

Core Idea
The challenge was only in utterance; its occurrence is enough.

Sanusi addresses an objection that the falsification of the hand miracle harms the overall argument for prophethood. He clarifies that the challenge was never about the entire miracle being confirmed; it was only about the utterance, which did occur. Therefore, the objection fails, and the proof remains sound.

Guided Path
1
Read the Refutation
Read the first part of the passage where Sanusi responds to the objection about the hand miracle. Notice the key terms: 'تكذيب' (falsification), 'اليد' (hand), 'التحدي' (challenge), 'النطق' (utterance).
2
Identify the Key Distinction
Observe that Sanusi distinguishes between the challenge being in the utterance (النطق) and the confirmation (التصديق). The challenge occurred only in utterance, which happened, so its confirmation was not challenged.
3
Reflect on the Implication
Think about why this distinction protects the miracle argument: if the challenge were about the miracle confirming truth, a falsification would harm it. But here, the challenge was only in the act of utterance, which was realized.
This shows how precise theological reasoning can address objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن تكذيب اليد ونحوها لا يقدح أيضاً ؛ لما أشرنا إليه في الأصل مِنْ أَنَّ التحدّي إنَّما وقع فيهِ أيضاً بمجرَّدِ النطقِ ، وقد وقع ، والتصديق لم يقع التحدي به حتى يضر تخلفه
قالَ المُقترَحُ : ( والتحقيقُ في هذه المسألة مبني على البحث في وجه دلالة المعجزة ، وأنَّها لا تدلُّ دلالة أدلَّةِ العقول ، وإنَّما هي مرتبطة عند اجتماع شرائطها بالصدق ضرورةً .
فإذا تمهَّدَ ذلكَ قلنا في المسألةِ : ليراجع العاقل نفسَهُ أَنَّ ما يجدُهُ مِنْ نزول هذا الفعلِ مِنَ اللهِ منزلة قولِهِ لمدَّعي النبوَّةِ : « صدقْتَ » هل يجده ضرورةً عندَ كونِ الآية الخارقة مُكذِّبةً أم لا ؟ فإذا لم يجده علم أنَّ المعجزة المستعقِبةَ العلم الضروري لم تحصل ، وهذا مأخذُ الكلام )
That the falsification of the hand and the like does not harm either; because of what we indicated in the original that the challenge occurred in it also merely by utterance, and it occurred, and the confirmation was not challenged so that its absence would be harmful. Al-Muqtarah said: (The investigation in this issue is based on the inquiry into the aspect of the miracle's indication, and that it does not indicate like the indication of rational proofs, but it is connected, when its conditions are met, to truth necessarily. So when that is established, we say in the issue: Let the rational person refer to himself: that what he finds of this act being from God in the position of His saying to the claimant of prophethood: 'You have spoken truthfully' – does he find it necessarily when the extraordinary sign is false or not? If he does not find it, he knows that the miracle that leads to necessary knowledge has not occurred, and this is the basis of the discourse.)
Source pages p. 562

Commentary
This passage is a refutation and a methodological premise. First, it refutes an earlier objection that the falsification of the 'hand' (a specific miracle) does not harm the proof, because the challenge was only in the utterance, which occurred. Then it presents al-Muqtarah's methodological foundation: the investigation of the miracle's indication must be based on understanding how miracles indicate truth. They are not like rational proofs; rather, when their conditions are met, they are necessarily connected to truth. The rational person should introspect: when an extraordinary act occurs, does he necessarily perceive it as God's statement 'You have spoken truthfully' to the claimant? If not, then the miracle that yields necessary knowledge has not occurred. This is the basis of the discussion.

Key Terms
تكذيب
اليد
التحدي
النطق
التصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Falsifying any miracle disproves the prophethood of the claimant.
Correction: The challenge was only in the utterance, not in the confirmation; thus falsification does not harm the proof.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why does falsifying the hand miracle not harm the proof?
A) Because the hand miracle is too weak to be a proof
B) Because the challenge was only in utterance, not in confirmation
C) Because the hand miracle was not a real miracle
D) Because the objector is not rational

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because the challenge was only in utterance, not in the confirmation of truthfulness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you evaluate evidence for your faith, do you sometimes demand proof beyond what was actually challenged by Allah, like the opponent who required confirmation beyond the utterance? How will you refocus on the essence?
?
Think of a time you dismissed a valid Islamic argument because you found a minor flaw in its presentation. How does Sanusi’s precise reasoning here call you to separate the core challenge from peripheral details?
?
The opponent insisted on a broader standard. In your own life, are there areas where you set criteria for accepting truth that are stricter than what Allah has required? What would it mean for you to accept the sufficiency of the challenge as Allah intended?

Al-Muqtarah's Premise: Miracles Indicate Truth Necessarily
Al-Muqtarah presents his methodological premise on miracle indication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Muqtarah's methodological premise that miracles indicate truth necessarily, not like rational proofs, and that introspection reveals this necessary connection.

Core Idea
Miracles are necessarily connected to truth; introspection confirms this.

Al-Muqtarah lays a foundational premise for understanding how miracles prove prophethood. He argues that miracles are not like rational proofs; rather, when their conditions are met, they are necessarily connected to the truth of the claimant. The rational person can verify this by introspection: when a miracle occurs, does one necessarily perceive it as God's affirmation? If not, the miracle is not conclusive.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Muqtarah's Statement
Read the second part of the passage, starting from 'قالَ المُقترَحُ'. Notice the terms: 'دلالة المعجزة' (indication of the miracle), 'ضرورة' (necessity), 'العلم الضروري' (necessary knowledge).
2
Understand the Distinction from Rational Proofs
Al-Muqtarah states that miracles do not indicate like rational proofs (أدلة العقول). Instead, their indication is necessary (ضرورة) when conditions are met. This means the connection between the miracle and the truth of the claimant is immediate and certain.
3
Reflect on the Introspection Method
Al-Muqtarah asks the rational person to introspect: does one find that an extraordinary act from God is necessarily in the position of God saying 'You have spoken truthfully'? If one does not find this necessary connection, then the miracle that yields necessary knowledge has not occurred.
This introspection is the basis of the argument.

Three Opinions on Miracle Indication
Sanusi enumerates three opinions on miracle indication and argues impossibility on the first two.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three opinions on how a miracle indicates a prophet's truthfulness and why the first two are impossible.

Core Idea
A miracle from a liar would break either rational proof or God's truthfulness.

Sanusi presents three scholarly views on the basis of a miracle's indication. He then argues that if the indication is rational or conventional, a miracle from a liar is impossible. This passage establishes a key principle in proving prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the enumeration of three opinions and the argument that follows.
The bracketed opening is part of the original text.
2
Identify the three opinions
The passage lists three possible ways a miracle indicates truth: rational (عقلية), conventional (وضعية), and habitual (عادية). Understand each.
These are technical terms in Islamic theology.
3
Understand the argument against the first two
Sanusi argues that on the first two opinions, a miracle from a liar is impossible. For the first, it would break rational proof; for the second, it would imply God confirms a liar, which is a lie.
The argument relies on God's truthfulness and knowledge.
4
Note the status of the third opinion
The passage does not reject the habitual opinion. It only argues impossibility for the rational and conventional views.
This is a common misunderstanding to avoid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجه دلالة المعجزة ]
وهل دَلالة المعجزة على صدْقِ الرسولِ دَلالة عقليَّةٌ . أو وضعية ، أو عادية بحسب القرائن ؟ أقوال
أمَّا على الأوَّلين فيستحيلُ صدورها على يد الكاذب ؛ لما يلزمُ على الأَوَّلِ مِنْ نقضِ الدليل العقلي ، وعلى الثاني مِنَ الخُلْفِ في خبرِهِ جلَّ وعلا ؛ إذْ تصديق الكاذب كذب
والكذبُ عليهِ جلَّ وعلا محال ؛ لأنَّ خبرَهُ على وَفْقِ علمِهِ ، فيكون صدقاً ، فلو انتفى لانتفى العلم ملزومه وهو محال ؛ لما عرفتَ مِنْ وجوبه
[Aspect of the miracle's indication] And is the indication of the miracle upon the truthfulness of the messenger a rational indication, or conventional, or habitual according to circumstances? [These are] opinions. As for the first two, it is impossible for it to occur through the hand of a liar; because what follows from the first is the breaking of the rational proof, and from the second is the breach of His, may He be exalted, report; since confirming a liar is a lie. And lying upon Him, may He be exalted, is impossible; because His report is in accordance with His knowledge, so it is truth; if it were absent, then knowledge its necessary condition would be absent, which is impossible, because you have known its necessity.
Source pages p. 563

Commentary
This passage is a classification and argument. It first states three opinions on how a miracle indicates the truthfulness of a prophet: rational, conventional (based on divine convention), or habitual (based on external circumstances). Then it argues that on the first two opinions, it is impossible for a miracle to occur through a liar. The reason: if the indication were rational, then a miracle from a liar would break the rational proof (contradiction). If it were conventional, then God's report that the miracle is truthful would be contradicted by confirming a liar, which is itself a lie. Lying is impossible for God because His report is always in accordance with His knowledge, hence true. If the report were false, then its necessary condition (knowledge) would be absent, which is impossible because God's knowledge is necessary.

Key Terms
دلالة
عقلية
وضعية
عادية
الرسول
الكاذب
نقض الدليل
الخلف
العلم
ملزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage rejects all three opinions on miracle indication.
Correction: Only the first two opinions are argued impossible; the habitual opinion is not addressed and remains a valid possibility.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence of the miracle's indication being conventional (وضعية)?
A) It would break the rational proof
B) It would imply that God confirms a liar, which is a lie
C) It would make the miracle impossible
D) It would require external circumstances

Key Takeaway
The first two opinions on miracle indication (rational and conventional) would entail impossible consequences if a liar performed a miracle, so they are invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If a miracle from a liar would break rational proof or imply that God confirms a lie, what does that reveal about the seriousness with which you should examine a claim to prophethood? How do you currently approach such claims when you hear them today?
?
God's report is true because it aligns with His knowledge. When you speak about God—whether in teaching, conversation, or even in your own heart—are you careful to speak only what is true and based on sound knowledge? When was the last time you said something about God without being sure it was correct?
?
Sanusi argues that lying is impossible for God because His report is always in accordance with His knowledge. How does this truth affect the way you trust the Quran and the words of the Prophet ﷺ? In moments of doubt, do you still hold that God's report is absolutely truthful, even when it challenges your desires?

The Rational Indication of Miracles
Sanusi argues for the rational basis of miracle indication and presents the first opinion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the indication of a miracle is considered rational according to the first opinion, and how an objection challenges this view.

Core Idea
Miracles indicate God's will, not as independent proofs.

Sanusi now addresses a key question: how do miracles prove prophethood? He first insists that the proof cannot come from scripture itself—that would be circular. He then presents the first of three scholarly opinions: the miracle's indication is rational, based on God's will to affirm the prophet. This lesson unpacks that argument and the objection it faces.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi builds from a general principle to a specific argument.
The passage is dense. Take your time.
2
Understand why the indication cannot be scriptural
Sanusi begins by ruling out scripture as the source of the miracle's proof. Why? Because scripture itself depends on miracles for its validation. Identify this circularity.
This sets the stage for a rational proof.
3
Follow the argument for rational indication
The proponents argue that God's creation of a supernatural event in the context of a challenge, without anyone able to oppose it, shows God's will to affirm the prophet. This is similar to how any unique specification of an act indicates will.
4
Examine the objection
The objection claims that 'affirmation' (tasdiq) is an eternal report from God, not a temporal attribute. Therefore, it cannot be the object of will, which only applies to temporal events. Consider why the opponents see a problem.
The answer is incomplete in this passage.
5
Reflect on the overall structure
Notice how Sanusi sets up a problem, presents one solution, and then shows a criticism. This is typical of kalam debate. Summarize in your own words the key moves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلمْ : أَنَّ دَلالة المعجزة لا يصح أن تكونَ مِنْ جملة الأدلَّةِ السمعيَّة ؛ إذ يستحيلُ أنْ تثبت صحة الأدلَّةِ السمعيّةِ قبل ثبوت دلالة المعجزة . ثم اختلف الأئمَّةُ بعد ذلكَ في وجهِ دلالتِها على ثلاثة أقوال : الأول : أنَّ دلالتها عقلية : وإليه ميل الأستاذ . قالوا : لأنَّ خَلْقَ اللهِ تعالى لهذا الخارقِ على وَفْقِ دعواه وتحدّيهِ معَ العجز عن معارضتِهِ ، وتخصيصه بذلكَ . . يدلُّ على إرادةِ اللهِ تعالى لتصديقِهِ ، كما يدلُّ اختصاص الفعل بالوقتِ المعيَّن والمحلّ المعيَّن على إرادتِهِ تعالى لذلك بالضرورة . وبالجملة : فقد جعلوا التصديق في هذا القول صفة للخارقِ الواقع على الوجه المخصوص ، مع جواز أنْ يعرى ذلك الخارقُ عن صفة التصديق بانعدام شرطٍ مِنْ شروط المعجزة ، فصارَتْ صفةً التصديق للخارقِ الحادثِ كسائر صفاتِ الأفعال الحادثة ، وقد علمت أنَّ اتّصاف الحادث بصفةٍ بدلاً عن نقيضها الجائز يدلُّ عقلاً على إرادة الفاعل ؛ وهو البارى تعالى لذلكَ ؛ لما تقرَّرَ أنَّ الطبيعة والعلَّةَ لا تُخصصانِ جائزاً بالوقوع بدلاً عن جائز يساويهِ . واعتُرِضَ على هذا القولِ (۱) : بأنَّ التصديق عندنا خبر عن الصدق ، وخبر الله تعالى أزلي لا يصحُ أنْ يكونَ حادثاً ولا صفةً ، فلا يصح أن تتعلَّقَ بهِ الإرادةُ ؛ لأنَّها كالقدرة لا تتعلَّقُ إلا للحادث بالممكن . وقد يُجابُ : بأنَّ التصديق الذي تعلَّقَتْ بهِ الإرادة هو التصديق
Know that the indication of the miracle cannot be part of the scriptural evidences, because it is impossible to establish the soundness of the scriptural evidences before the establishment of the miracle's indication. Then the imams differed after that concerning the manner of its indication into three opinions: The first: that its indication is rational – and the master inclines to it. They said: Because God's creation of this extraordinary act in conformity with his claim and his challenge, along with the inability to oppose it, and its specification to that... indicates God's will to affirm him, just as the specification of an action to a particular time and place indicates His will for that necessarily. In summary: they made affirmation (tasdiq) in this opinion an attribute of the extraordinary act occurring in that specific manner, while it is possible for that extraordinary act to be devoid of the attribute of affirmation due to the absence of a condition of the miracle. So the attribute of affirmation became for the occurring extraordinary act like other attributes of occurring acts. And you have known that the description of an occurrence with an attribute instead of its possible opposite indicates rationally the will of the agent – that is, the Exalted Deity – for that, because it has been established that nature and cause do not specify a possible occurrence instead of an equal possible. And an objection was raised against this opinion: that affirmation (tasdiq) with us is a report about truth, and God's report is eternal; it cannot be temporal nor an attribute, so it is not valid for will to attach to it, because will, like power, only attaches to the possible temporal. And it may be answered: that the affirmation to which will attaches is the affirmation...
Source pages p. 563

Commentary
This passage begins a new topic: the indication (dalāla) of miracles. Sanusi first states that the miracle's indication cannot be from scriptural evidences (adilla sam‘iyya) because that would be circular: one cannot prove the soundness of scripture before establishing the miracle's indication. He then notes that scholars differed on the nature of this indication into three opinions, and he presents the first: it is rational ('aqliyya), to which the master (al-ustādh) inclines. The proponents argue: God's creation of a supernatural event (khāriq) that matches the prophet's claim and challenge, with inability to oppose it, and its specification to that context, indicates God's will to affirm (tasdiq) the prophet – just as the specification of an action to a particular time and place necessarily indicates God's will. They make 'affirmation' an attribute of the occurring miracle itself, though it can be absent if a condition is missing. This attribute is temporal, and an occurrence being qualified by one of two possible opposites rationally indicates the agent's will, since nature or a [natural] cause cannot specify a possible without a preference. Then an objection is raised: affirmation is a report about truth, and God's report is eternal, not temporal, so it cannot be an object of will (which only attaches to temporally possible things). The beginning of an answer is given but cut off, stating that the affirmation to which will attaches is... (the answer continues in later pages).

سمعيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that miracles prove prophethood by themselves, without reference to God's will.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the miracle's indication is through God's will attached to it, not as an intrinsic property.

Checkpoint
Why can't the miracle's indication be from scriptural evidences according to Sanusi?
A) Because miracles are not mentioned in scripture.
B) Because scriptural evidences depend on miracles, so it would be circular.
C) Because scripture is not reliable.
D) Because miracles are rational proofs.

Key Takeaway
The first opinion grounds miracle indication in God's will, making it a rational proof, but faces an objection about the nature of affirmation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness something extraordinary that seems like a sign from God, do you immediately attribute it to God's will, or do you tend to explain it away as coincidence? Sanusi argues that the specificity of events demands rational acknowledgment of divine will. How specific must an event be for you to stop calling it chance? What would that require of your heart?
?
The objection in this passage challenges whether 'affirmation' (tasdiq) is eternal or temporal. In your own life, do you seek affirmation from God in temporal signs, or do you rest solely in His eternal word? Are you tempted to demand continuous spectacular signs instead of trusting the finality of revelation? Examine your expectation of God's communication with you.
?
If miracles indicate God's will rationally, not independently, then every blessing or trial you experience carries potential meaning. How would your daily habits change if you truly believed that every specific occurrence is a message from the Creator, not a random event? Would you become more attentive? More grateful? More fearful? Commit to a specific way you will treat one event today as a sign.

Prophethood

The Customary Evidence of Miracles and Certainty
Sanusi presents the customary view of miracle evidence and its implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the customary view of miracle evidence yields certain knowledge of a prophet's truthfulness and why God's custom prevents liars from performing genuine miracles.

Core Idea
Miracles provide certainty through God's consistent custom, not logical necessity.

Sanusi begins by explaining the 'customary' view (دلالة عادية) of miracle evidence: when a miracle occurs and we gain necessary knowledge of the prophet's truthfulness, it is impossible for him to be a liar. This certainty is rooted in God's unbroken custom of not enabling liars to perform miracles. In this passage, you will see how Sanusi establishes this foundational principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then its literal translation. Notice the key terms: 'دلالة عادية', 'علم ضروري', and 'خرق العادة'.
2
Understand the core claim
Sanusi states that when necessary knowledge (علم ضروري) of the prophet's truthfulness is obtained through a miracle, it is impossible for him to be a liar. Grasp why this is a strong claim.
3
Examine God's custom
The author explains that God's custom from the beginning of the world has been to not enable liars to perform miracles. If a liar tries to fake a miracle, God exposes him. This is the basis for certainty.
This custom is a matter of divine grace and generosity.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how this view differs from a rational necessity view. The certainty here comes from God's consistent action, not logical impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا إِنْ قلنا : إنَّ دلالة المعجزة عاديَّةٌ بحسَبِ القرائن .. فحيثُ حصل العلمُ الضروري عنها بصدقِ الآتي بها فإنَّهُ يستحيلُ أنْ يكونَ كاذباً ، وإلا انقلب العلم الضروري جهلاً ، ولم يُجْرِ سبحانه وتعالى عادتَهُ مِنْ أَوَّلِ الدنيا إلى الآنَ إلا بعدم تمكين الكاذب مِنَ المعجزاتِ وإذا خيَّلَ بسحرٍ ونحوِهِ أظهرَ اللهُ فضيحتَهُ عن قُرْبٍ ، فللَّهِ الحمد على معاملته في ذلك ونحوه بمخض الفضل والكرم . ويجوز أن تظهر المعجزة على يد الكاذب لو انخرقَتِ العادةُ ، ولا يحصلُ حينَئِذٍ بها علمُ صدقِهِ ، وإلا كانَ الجهلُ علماً ، وتجويز خرْقِ العادةِ عند حصولِ العلمِ بالصدق في حقِّ المحقِّ لا يقدح في العلم ؛ إِذْ لا يلزمُ مِنْ جواز الشيء وقوعه ، ألا ترى أَنَّا نُجوِّزُ استمرار عدم العالم معَ علمنا ضرورةً بوجودِهِ ؛ إذْ معنى الجواز : أَنَّهُ لو قُدَّرَ واقعاً لم يلزم منه محال لذاته ؛ لأنَّه محتمل الوقوع هذا الكلام في غاية الوضوح ، وحاصله : أنَّه يجوز على القول بأنَّ دلالة المعجزة عادية أن تظهر المعجزة على أيدي الكاذبينَ ولا يكون العلمُ حينَئِذٍ حاصلاً بنبوتهم ، وإلا انقلبَ العلم جهلاً ، إلا أنَّهُ سبحانَهُ تفضّل بعدم خرْقِ العادةِ في هذا الأمر ، فلم يُظهِرِ المعجزة قط على يد كاذب ، بل عادته سبحانَهُ أنْ يفضحَ كُلَّ مَنْ أَرَادَ أَنْ يَبرُزَ بمنصب النبوَّةِ وليسَ مِنْ أهلها هذا فيما عُلِمَ بالاستقراءِ مِنْ عادتِهِ تعالى فيما مضى ، وأما في المستقبل فقد كفانا الله هذه المؤنة بحصولِ العلم القطعي بأنَّ نبينا محمداً صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ خاتم النبيِّينَ ، فكلُّ مَنِ ادَّعى بعدَهُ منصبَ النبوَّةِ فليسَ إلا الإسلام أو السيفُ ، ولا يُلتفتُ إلى قولِهِ ، ولا إلى الخارقِ الذي يظهرُ على يدِهِ وقد ألزم المعتزلة الأصحابَ جواز صدور المعجزة على أيدي الكاذبينَ مِنْ جهةٍ أخرى؛ وهي أن قالوا: مِنْ مذهبكم أنَّ الله سبحانَهُ : يُضِلُّ مَنْ يشاءُ ، ولا يتعيَّنُ في حقه مراعاة صلاح ولا أصلح ، فما الذي يؤمنُكم مِنْ خَلْقِ خوارق العاداتِ على وَفْقِ دعوى المدعينَ للنبوَّاتِ ، ويكون المرادُ مِنْ ذلكَ إظهار الضلالاتِ ؟ فأما القائلون بالرأيين الأولين في وجهِ دلالة المعجزة فأجابوا على مقتضى الوجهين : أما على الأول (۲) فقالوا: نعم، يجوز للباري تعالى الإضلال لكن لا بالمعجزة؛ لاستحالة ذلك معها، كما يجوزُ خلْقُ السوادِ في محلّ مُعيَّن للكنْ لا مع وجودِ البياضِ، والمعيَّة في النقيضين النقيضين محال
And if we say: that the evidence of the miracle is customary according to the indications... then when the necessary knowledge of the truthfulness of its bringer is obtained through it, it is impossible that he be a liar; otherwise necessary knowledge would turn into ignorance. And He, exalted, has not conducted His custom from the beginning of the world until now except by not enabling the liar to perform miracles. And if he fakes with sorcery or the like, God exposes his disgrace quickly. So praise be to God for His dealing in that and similar matters with pure grace and generosity. And it is permissible that the miracle appear on the hand of a liar if the custom is broken; and then knowledge of his truthfulness is not obtained through it, otherwise ignorance would be knowledge. And the possibility of breaking the custom when knowledge of truthfulness is obtained in the case of the truthful does not detract from knowledge, for the permissibility of a thing does not necessitate its occurrence. Do you not see that we permit the continued non-existence of the world while we know necessarily its existence? For the meaning of permissibility is that if it were actual, no impossibility would follow from it inherently, because it is possible of occurrence. This discourse is extremely clear. Its summary is: on the view that the evidence of the miracle is customary, it is permissible that the miracle appear on the hands of liars and then knowledge of their prophethood would not be obtained; otherwise knowledge would turn into ignorance. But He, exalted, has graciously refrained from breaking the custom in this matter, so He has never caused a miracle to appear on the hand of a liar. Rather, His custom, exalted, is to expose everyone who desires to claim the station of prophethood without being its people. This is what is known by induction from His custom, exalted, in the past. As for the future, God has spared us this trouble by the acquisition of definitive knowledge that our Prophet Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace, is the Seal of the Prophets; so everyone who claims the station of prophethood after him, there is nothing but Islam or the sword, and no attention is paid to his speech nor to the miracle that appears on his hand. And the Mu'tazila have imposed on the companions the permissibility of miracles occurring on the hands of liars from another aspect, which is that they said: from your doctrine is that God, exalted, leads astray whom He wills, and He is not obliged to observe what is beneficial or most beneficial, so what assures you that He would not create supernatural events in accordance with the claims of claimants of prophethood, intending thereby to manifest misguidances? As for those who hold the first two views concerning the manner of the evidence of the miracle, they answered according to the two views: As for the first (2), they said: Yes, it is permissible for the Creator to lead astray, but not by means of a miracle, because that is impossible with it, just as it is permissible to create blackness in a specific place but not with the existence of whiteness, and the conjunction of two opposites is impossible.
Source pages p. 569

Commentary
This passage is a detailed treatment of the argument from miracles for prophethood, addressing the nature of miracle evidence and responding to a Mu'tazila objection. The author begins by explaining the 'customary' view (دلالة عادية) of miracle evidence: when necessary knowledge of the prophet's truthfulness is obtained through a miracle, it is impossible for him to be a liar, otherwise knowledge would become ignorance. God's custom has consistently been to prevent liars from performing miracles; if a liar tries to fake it, God exposes him. However, it is theoretically possible for a miracle to occur on the hand of a liar if the custom is broken, but then no knowledge of truthfulness would arise. The author then clarifies that the mere possibility of breaking custom does not undermine certain knowledge, because possibility does not imply actual occurrence, and gives the example of the possible non-existence of the world. The summary reiterates that on the customary view, liars could potentially bring miracles, but God's grace has prevented that. Then the author addresses the future: since Muhammad is the final prophet, any claim to prophethood after him is false regardless of miracles. The Mu'tazila then present an objection: if God can lead people astray and is not bound to what is beneficial, why couldn't He create miracles for false prophets to mislead? The answer given by those who hold the first two views (that miracle evidence is rational or conventional) is that it is permissible for God to lead astray, but not by a miracle, because miracles inherently indicate truthfulness—it would be contradictory for a miracle to accompany a lie, just as it is impossible for black and white to coexist in the same spot. Thus, the objection is refuted without conceding that God would create a miracle for a liar.

Key Terms
دلالة عادية
علم ضروري
خرق العادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the customary view makes miracle evidence uncertain or weak.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the customary view yields certain knowledge because God's custom is consistent and He never permits liars to perform genuine miracles.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why is it impossible for a liar to perform a genuine miracle?
A) Because it is logically contradictory
B) Because God's custom has always prevented it
C) Because miracles are rational proofs
D) Because liars cannot claim miracles

Key Takeaway
Miracles provide certain knowledge of the prophet's truthfulness because God's consistent custom prevents liars from performing them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear someone claim to have a divine message, do you rely on God's custom as described here to discern truth from falsehood, or do you react based on your own assumptions? How can you cultivate deeper trust in God's consistent protection of truth?
?
This passage says that if a liar could perform a miracle, 'necessary knowledge would turn into ignorance.' Has there been a time when you allowed doubt to cloud what you knew with certainty from God's signs? What would it take for you to treat that certainty as non-negotiable?
?
Sanusi ends with praise for God's grace in exposing falsehood. When you see false claims being exposed, do you see it as God's grace, or do you take it for granted? How can you train your heart to recognize and thank God for His custom in guarding truth?

Miracles on Liars and the Seal of Prophethood
Sanusi discusses the possibility of miracles on liars and the finality of prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the possibility of a miracle occurring on a liar does not undermine certainty, and how the finality of Muhammad's prophethood eliminates future false claims.

Core Idea
Possibility does not nullify certainty; and Muhammad is the final prophet.

Having established the customary certainty of miracles, Sanusi now addresses a potential objection: if the custom could be broken, a miracle could theoretically appear on a liar's hand. He explains that such a possibility does not affect actual certainty, just as the possible non-existence of the world does not negate our knowledge of its existence. He then affirms that the Seal of Prophethood (Muhammad) provides definitive closure to all future claims.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the discussion of 'جواز' (permissibility) and the example of the world's possible non-existence.
2
Understand the key argument
Sanusi argues that the mere permissibility of a miracle occurring on a liar does not undermine the knowledge we have from a miracle, because possibility does not entail actual occurrence.
He gives the example: we know the world exists, but we can still conceive of its non-existence.
3
Grasp the role of divine grace
God has graciously not broken His custom in this matter, so no liar has ever performed a genuine miracle. This is a matter of divine grace, not necessity.
4
Recognize the finality argument
Sanusi states that we have definitive knowledge that Muhammad is the final prophet. Therefore, any future claim to prophethood is false, regardless of miracles.
This is based on the Quranic declaration and consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

إظهار الضلالاتِ ؟ فأما القائلون بالرأيين الأولين في وجهِ دلالة المعجزة فأجابوا على مقتضى الوجهين : أما على الأول (۲) فقالوا: نعم، يجوز للباري تعالى الإضلال لكن لا بالمعجزة؛ لاستحالة ذلك معها، كما يجوزُ خلْقُ السوادِ في محلّ مُعيَّن للكنْ لا مع وجودِ البياضِ، والمعيَّة في النقيضين النقيضين محال
And if we say: that the evidence of the miracle is customary according to the indications... then when the necessary knowledge of the truthfulness of its bringer is obtained through it, it is impossible that he be a liar; otherwise necessary knowledge would turn into ignorance. And He, exalted, has not conducted His custom from the beginning of the world until now except by not enabling the liar to perform miracles. And if he fakes with sorcery or the like, God exposes his disgrace quickly. So praise be to God for His dealing in that and similar matters with pure grace and generosity. And it is permissible that the miracle appear on the hand of a liar if the custom is broken; and then knowledge of his truthfulness is not obtained through it, otherwise ignorance would be knowledge. And the possibility of breaking the custom when knowledge of truthfulness is obtained in the case of the truthful does not detract from knowledge, for the permissibility of a thing does not necessitate its occurrence. Do you not see that we permit the continued non-existence of the world while we know necessarily its existence? For the meaning of permissibility is that if it were actual, no impossibility would follow from it inherently, because it is possible of occurrence. This discourse is extremely clear. Its summary is: on the view that the evidence of the miracle is customary, it is permissible that the miracle appear on the hands of liars and then knowledge of their prophethood would not be obtained; otherwise knowledge would turn into ignorance. But He, exalted, has graciously refrained from breaking the custom in this matter, so He has never caused a miracle to appear on the hand of a liar. Rather, His custom, exalted, is to expose everyone who desires to claim the station of prophethood without being its people. This is what is known by induction from His custom, exalted, in the past. As for the future, God has spared us this trouble by the acquisition of definitive knowledge that our Prophet Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace, is the Seal of the Prophets; so everyone who claims the station of prophethood after him, there is nothing but Islam or the sword, and no attention is paid to his speech nor to the miracle that appears on his hand. And the Mu'tazila have imposed on the companions the permissibility of miracles occurring on the hands of liars from another aspect, which is that they said: from your doctrine is that God, exalted, leads astray whom He wills, and He is not obliged to observe what is beneficial or most beneficial, so what assures you that He would not create supernatural events in accordance with the claims of claimants of prophethood, intending thereby to manifest misguidances? As for those who hold the first two views concerning the manner of the evidence of the miracle, they answered according to the two views: As for the first (2), they said: Yes, it is permissible for the Creator to lead astray, but not by means of a miracle, because that is impossible with it, just as it is permissible to create blackness in a specific place but not with the existence of whiteness, and the conjunction of two opposites is impossible.
Source pages p. 569

Commentary
This passage is a detailed treatment of the argument from miracles for prophethood, addressing the nature of miracle evidence and responding to a Mu'tazila objection. The author begins by explaining the 'customary' view (دلالة عادية) of miracle evidence: when necessary knowledge of the prophet's truthfulness is obtained through a miracle, it is impossible for him to be a liar, otherwise knowledge would become ignorance. God's custom has consistently been to prevent liars from performing miracles; if a liar tries to fake it, God exposes him. However, it is theoretically possible for a miracle to occur on the hand of a liar if the custom is broken, but then no knowledge of truthfulness would arise. The author then clarifies that the mere possibility of breaking custom does not undermine certain knowledge, because possibility does not imply actual occurrence, and gives the example of the possible non-existence of the world. The summary reiterates that on the customary view, liars could potentially bring miracles, but God's grace has prevented that. Then the author addresses the future: since Muhammad is the final prophet, any claim to prophethood after him is false regardless of miracles. The Mu'tazila then present an objection: if God can lead people astray and is not bound to what is beneficial, why couldn't He create miracles for false prophets to mislead? The answer given by those who hold the first two views (that miracle evidence is rational or conventional) is that it is permissible for God to lead astray, but not by a miracle, because miracles inherently indicate truthfulness—it would be contradictory for a miracle to accompany a lie, just as it is impossible for black and white to coexist in the same spot. Thus, the objection is refuted without conceding that God would create a miracle for a liar.

Key Terms
جواز
خرق العادة
خاتم النبيين
استقراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if a miracle could theoretically occur on a liar, then we can never be certain of a prophet's truthfulness.
Correction: Sanusi explains that possibility does not undermine actual certainty, and God's consistent custom ensures that genuine miracles only accompany truth.

Checkpoint
Why does the possibility of a miracle on a liar not undermine the certainty we have from a miracle of a truthful prophet?
A) Because miracles are logically necessary
B) Because possibility does not entail actual occurrence
C) Because liars never claim miracles
D) Because the custom is broken only for truthful prophets

Key Takeaway
The theoretical possibility of a miracle on a liar does not affect the certainty of genuine miracles, and Muhammad's finality definitively closes future claims.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if Muhammad is the final prophet, or do you sometimes look to other sources—philosophers, gurus, or modern ideologies—as if seeking a new 'prophethood' to guide your life? What would change if you fully internalized that no further revelation will come?
?
When doubts about the truth of Islam surface in your heart, do you allow the theoretical possibility that a miracle could appear on a liar to shake your certainty? Or do you hold firmly to the actual certainty God has given you through the Quran and the Prophet's life? What will you do the next time such a doubt arises?
?
This lesson uses the example: we know the world exists yet can conceive of its non-existence. In your own faith, is your certainty of Muhammad's finality based on mere possibility or on the definitive proof God has provided? Are you willing to submit your future to that certainty, closing the door to every competing claim?

Refuting the Mu'tazila Objection on Miracles
Sanusi presents and refutes the Mu'tazila objection to miracle evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila objection that God could create miracles for false prophets to mislead, and the response that a miracle cannot accompany a false claim because it would be contradictory.

Core Idea
Miracles inherently indicate truth; God cannot conjoin a miracle with falsehood.

The Mu'tazila raise a further objection: if God can lead astray and is not obliged to act for the best, why could He not create supernatural events (khawariq) in support of false prophets, thereby misleading people? Sanusi presents the answer from those who hold the first two views on miracle evidence. The response is that it is permissible for God to lead astray, but not through a miracle, because a miracle by its nature indicates truthfulness—it would be impossible for a miracle to accompany a lie, just as black and white cannot coexist in the same place.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the Mu'tazila argument and the response beginning with 'فأما القائلون بالرأيين الأولين...'.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila objection
The Mu'tazila argue that since God can lead astray and is not bound by what is beneficial, He could create miracles for false prophets. This would make miracle evidence unreliable.
3
Grasp the response for the first two views
The response for the first view (rational evidence) is that it is possible for God to lead astray but not by a miracle, because a miracle is intrinsically linked to truthfulness. It would be contradictory for a miracle to accompany falsehood.
The analogy: black and white cannot coexist in the same spot.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this response preserves the evidential value of miracles. It shows that miracles have an inherent connection to truth, making them reliable indicators of prophethood.
