Mu'tazila Objection and Three Ash'ari Responses on Miracles' Proof
Sanusi presents Mu'tazila objection and three Ash'ari responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila objection that miracles could be used to mislead, and how Ash'ari scholars responded from three different perspectives on the nature of miracle proof.

Core Idea
Miracles cannot mislead; they produce certainty or divine testimony.

This passage presents a critical challenge from the Mu'tazila: if Allah can lead people astray, what prevents Him from creating miracles to support false prophets? Sanusi then provides three answers, each corresponding to a different theory of how miracles prove a prophet's truthfulness—rational, conventional, and customary. Understanding these responses deepens our grasp of Ash'ari theology on prophethood and divine justice.

Guided Path
1
The past and future are settled
Read the opening lines: for the past, induction (istiqra') shows Allah's custom; for the future, certain knowledge that Muhammad (saw) is the final prophet.
2
The Mu'tazila objection
Identify the objection: since Allah can mislead, He could create miracles for false prophets. This challenges the Ash'ari position that miracles prove truthfulness.
The objection is based on Ash'ari doctrine of divine freedom.
3
First response: rational proof
The first response argues that miracle and misguidance cannot coexist, like blackness and whiteness. Leading astray through evidence is impossible.
This view holds that miracle's proof is rational ('aqli).
4
Second response: conventional proof
The second response compares miracle to divine speech; since Allah cannot lie, a miracle (by convention) cannot accompany falsehood.
This view holds that miracle's proof is by convention (wad').
5
Third response: customary proof
The third response says miracle produces certain knowledge; logical possibility of falsehood does not undermine actual certainty, just as we know our existence while deeming non-existence possible.
This view holds that miracle's proof is customary ('adi).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا فيما عُلِمَ بالاستقراءِ مِنْ عادتِهِ تعالى فيما مضى ، وأما في المستقبل فقد كفانا الله هذه المؤنة بحصولِ العلم القطعي بأنَّ نبينا محمداً صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ خاتم النبيِّينَ ، فكلُّ مَنِ ادَّعى بعدَهُ منصبَ النبوَّةِ فليسَ إلا الإسلام أو السيفُ ، ولا يُلتفتُ إلى قولِهِ ، ولا إلى الخارقِ الذي يظهرُ على يدِهِ وقد ألزم المعتزلة الأصحابَ جواز صدور المعجزة على أيدي الكاذبينَ مِنْ جهةٍ أخرى ؛ وهي أن قالوا : مِنْ مذهبكم أنَّ الله سبحانَهُ : يُضِلُّ مَنْ يشاءُ ، ولا يتعيَّنُ في حقه مراعاة صلاح ولا أصلح ، فما الذي يؤمنُكم مِنْ خَلْقِ خوارق العاداتِ على وَفْقِ دعوى المدعينَ للنبوَّاتِ ، ويكون المرادُ مِنْ ذلكَ إظهار الضلالاتِ ؟
فأما القائلون بالرأيين الأولين في وجهِ دلالة المعجزة فأجابوا على مقتضى الوجهين :
أما على الأول (۲) فقالوا : نعم ، يجوز للباري تعالى الإضلال لكن لا بالمعجزة ؛ لاستحالة ذلك معها ، كما يجوزُ خلْقُ السوادِ في محلّ مُعيَّن للكنْ لا مع وجودِ البياضِ ، والمعيَّة في النقيضين محال
والإضلال بالدليل : قلبُ الدليل شبهةً ، والعلم الحاصل عنه جهلاً ، وذلك محال
وأما على الثاني - وهو أنَّ الدليلَ مِنْ جهة المواضعة - فقالوا : يجوز أنْ يُضِلَّ لا بالخلْقِ في القولِ ، وإذا كانَتِ المعجزة تتنزَّلُ منزلةَ التصريح بكلام ناص على التصديقِ ، ولا يصح الإضلال به ؛ لاستحالة الخلْفِ في خبره تعالى .. فكذا لا يصح الإضلال بما يدلُّ على التصديق وإنْ كانَ بحكم المواضعةِ .
وأما على الرأي الثالث - وهو أنَّ دلالة المعجزة عادية - : فأمرُ الجواب أيضاً سهل ؛ وهو أنَّ آيةَ صدقِ النبي حصولُ العلم تلك المعجزة ، فإذا حصل انتفى معَهُ احتمال عدم الصدقِ ؛ لأنَّ العلم لا يحتمل النقيض بوجهِ مِنَ الوجوه ، وإلا انقلب جهلاً وتجويزنا عقلاً كذبَ المحقِّ الذي تيقنا صدقه لا يقدح في العلم بصدقِهِ ؛ لأنَّ معنى جواز الكذب في حقِّهِ : أَنَّهُ لو وقع بدلاً عن الصدْقِ الواقع في حقِّهِ لم يلزم منه محال ، لا أنَّ معناه احتمال وقوع الكذب في حقه تَقَّهِ ، وكثيراً ما نعلمُ وقوع أشياءَ علماً ضرورياً مع تجويزنا عقلاً نقيض ذلك الواقع ؛ وذلك كعلمنا بوجودنا ؛ فإنَّهُ لا يستريبُ فيهِ عاقل ، وإنْ كنَّا نُجوِّرُ عدَمنا ؛ بمعنى : لو استمر عدمُنا ولم نُوجَدْ أصلاً .. لم يلزم منه محال ، لا بمعنى : أنَّ عدمنا محتمل الحصول لنا حالَ علمنا بوجودنا .
قوله : ( في حقٌّ المحقِّ ( الأولى : تعلقه بـ ( خرْقِ العادةِ ) أي : تجويزنا عقلاً خرْقَ العادةِ في حق المحقِّ ; بمعنى : لو كان الواقع في
This is known by induction from His custom (may He be exalted) in the past. As for the future, Allah has spared us this trouble by the attainment of certain knowledge that our Prophet Muhammad (may Allah bless him and grant him peace) is the seal of the prophets. So whoever claims the station of prophethood after him, there is nothing but Islam or the sword, and no attention is paid to his statement, nor to the miraculous event that appears at his hand. And the Mu'tazila have obliged the companions [the Ash'aris] to permit the occurrence of miracles at the hands of liars from another aspect: they said, 'It is part of your doctrine that Allah (exalted) leads astray whom He wills, and He is not required to observe what is beneficial or most beneficial. So what assures you that He will not create miraculous events consistent with the claims of those who claim prophethood, intending thereby to manifest misguidance?'
As for those who hold the first two views on the manner of the miracle's proof, they answered according to the two views:
As for the first [view that the proof is rational], they said: Yes, it is permissible for the Creator (exalted) to lead astray, but not through a miracle; because that is impossible along with it, just as it is permissible to create blackness in a specific location but not with the presence of whiteness—simultaneity of contraries is impossible. And leading astray through evidence is turning evidence into doubt and the knowledge resulting from it into ignorance, and that is impossible.
As for the second—that the evidence is by way of convention—they said: It is permissible for Him to lead astray, but not by creating a contradictory statement. And since the miracle stands in the place of an explicit statement that clearly indicates affirmation, and it is not valid to lead astray through it—because breaking a promise is impossible for Allah (exalted)—so too it is not valid to lead astray through what indicates affirmation, even if it is by way of convention.
As for the third view—that the proof of a miracle is customary—the answer is also easy: it is that the sign of a prophet's truthfulness is the attainment of certain knowledge from that miracle. So when it is attained, the possibility of untruthfulness is negated along with it; because knowledge does not admit its opposite in any way—otherwise it would turn into ignorance. And our deeming logically possible the untruthfulness of the truthful person whose truthfulness we are certain of does not undermine the knowledge of his truthfulness; because the meaning of the possibility of lying in his case is that if it occurred instead of the truth that actually occurred in his case, no impossibility would follow—not that it means that the occurrence of lying in his case is probable. We often know the occurrence of things with necessary knowledge while deeming logically possible the opposite of that actual occurrence—like our knowledge of our own existence: no rational person doubts it, even though we deem the possibility of our non-existence—meaning: if our non-existence had continued and we had never been brought into existence at all, no impossibility would follow—not that our non-existence is likely to occur while we are aware of our existence.
His statement: 'in the case of the truthful one'—preferably: it is connected to 'breaking of custom', i.e., our deeming logically possible the breaking of custom in the case of the truthful one; meaning: if the actual occurrence were
Source pages p. 570

Commentary
This passage is a response to a Mu'tazila objection against the Ash'ari doctrine of miracles. The Mu'tazila argue that since Ash'aris hold that Allah can lead people astray without regard to benefit, He could create miracles (khawariq al-'adat) at the hands of false prophets to mislead people. The author presents three answers based on different views of how miracles prove a prophet's truthfulness.

First, the passage begins by affirming that for the past, we know by induction that Allah's custom is to expose false claimants. For the future, we have certain knowledge that Muhammad (saw) is the final prophet, so any later claim is invalid, and any miracle performed by a false claimant is irrelevant.

Then the Mu'tazila objection is stated: according to Ash'ari doctrine, Allah can lead astray whom He wills, so nothing prevents Him from creating miracles to support false prophets.

Answer one (rational proof view): It is permissible for Allah to lead astray, but not through a miracle, because a miracle is intrinsically linked to truth. Just as blackness and whiteness cannot coexist, leading astray and a miracle cannot coexist. Moreover, leading astray through evidence would require turning the evidence into doubt—which is impossible.

Answer two (conventional proof view): Allah can lead astray, but not by creating a contradictory statement. A miracle functions like a verbal affirmation from Allah. Since Allah cannot break His promise, He cannot create a miracle to support a lie.

Answer three (customary proof view): The miracle itself produces certain knowledge of the prophet's truth. Once that certain knowledge is attained, the possibility of falsehood is excluded. The mere logical possibility that the miracle could have been used to mislead does not undermine actual certain knowledge. This is like our knowledge of our own existence: we are certain of it while acknowledging that non-existence is logically possible. Logical possibility does not imply actual doubt.

The final paragraph appears to be an editorial gloss clarifying the phrase 'in the case of the truthful one' from earlier, but the text is cut off.

Key Terms
استقراء
علم قطعي
خاتم النبيين
معجزة
إضلال
دلالة
عقلية
مواضعة
عادية
تجويز عقلي
علم ضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the logical possibility of a miracle being used to mislead implies actual uncertainty about the prophet's truthfulness.
Correction: The third response clarifies that logical possibility (tajwiz 'aqli) does not create doubt; certain knowledge (al-ilm al-qati') excludes its opposite, just as we are certain of our existence despite the logical possibility of non-existence.

Checkpoint
According to the third response (customary proof view), why does the logical possibility of a prophet lying not undermine certain knowledge of his truthfulness?
A) Because logical possibility does not entail actual doubt; certain knowledge excludes its opposite.
B) Because Allah cannot create a miracle to support a liar.
C) Because the miracle is a direct verbal testimony from Allah.
D) Because we know by induction that Allah exposes false prophets.

Key Takeaway
The three responses demonstrate that regardless of how a miracle proves truthfulness, the possibility of divine misguidance through miracles is excluded.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After learning that miracles produce certain knowledge like our knowledge of our own existence, ask yourself: Do you treat the truth of Islam with the same level of certainty as you treat the fact that you exist? If not, what is holding you back from that certainty, and what change in your life would reflect that certainty?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that if God can lead astray, He could create miracles for false prophets. Are there areas in your life where you allow the mere logical 'what if' to create doubt about God's truth? What would it cost you to act on the certainty that God has given you through clear proofs?
?
This lesson shows that God's power is not limited, yet He does not use miracles to mislead. How does this shape your trust in God's wisdom and truthfulness? Will you commit to strengthening your certainty by reflecting on the clear signs God has placed in your life, rather than entertaining hypothetical doubts?

The General Principle of Prophetic Infallibility
Sanusi defines prophetic infallibility and its scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundation of prophetic infallibility: that once a prophet's truthfulness is confirmed by miracles, they must be believed, and that both lying and sins are impossible for them.

Core Idea
Prophets are protected from lying and sin because we must follow them.

This passage opens the discussion of prophetic infallibility ('isma). Sanusi first establishes the general rule: the miracle proves the prophet's truthfulness, so we must believe everything they bring. He then explains that lying is rationally impossible and sins are legally impossible, because we are commanded to follow the prophets. The passage extends this protection even to disliked acts and certain permissible acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the general principle
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning up to the discussion of disliked acts. Notice how Sanusi builds his argument from the miracle to the impossibility of lying and sins.
The verse from Surah al-A'raf is used as evidence.
2
Identify the three categories
Identify what is impossible for prophets: 1) lying rationally, 2) sins legally, 3) disliked acts (makrūh) and even permissible acts in an inappropriate manner.
This expands the concept of infallibility.
3
Understand the reasoning
Ask yourself: why can't prophets sin? Because we are commanded to follow them; if they could sin, we would be commanded to sin, which contradicts Allah's command against immorality.
This logical argument is central.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في عصمةِ الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام وإذا عُلِمَ صدق الرسل بدلالة المعجزة وجب تصديقهم في كل ما أتوا به عن الله تعالى ويستحيل منهم الكذب عقلاً والمعاصي شرعاً لأننا مأمورون بالاقتداء بهم فلو جازت عليهم المعصية لكنا مأمورين بها {قل إن الله لا يأمر بالفحشاء} [الأعراف:28] وبهذا تعرف عدم وقوع المكروه منهم أيضاً بل والمباح على الوجه الذي يقع من غيرهم الكلام في عصمة الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام في موضعين أحدهما قبل النبوة والثاني بعدها أما حكمهم قبل النبوة فالذي ذهب إليه أكثر الأشاعرة وطائفة كثيرة من المعتزلة إلى أنه لا يمتنع عقلاً على الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام قبل البعثة معصية كبيرة كانت أو صغيرة وذهب بعض أصحابنا إلى أنه يمتنع ذلك وهو مختار القاضي
Discussion on the infallibility of the prophets, peace be upon them. And when the truthfulness of the messengers is known through the evidence of the miracle, it is obligatory to believe them in everything they bring from Allah, may He be exalted. And lying is impossible from them rationally, and sins legally, because we are commanded to follow them; so if sin were permissible for them, we would be commanded to commit it. {Say: Indeed Allah does not command immorality} [Al-A'raf:28]. And by this you know the non-occurrence of disliked acts from them as well, and even permissible acts in the manner that occurs from others. The discussion on the infallibility of the prophets, peace be upon them, is in two places: one: before prophethood, and the second: after it. As for their ruling before prophethood: the view held by most Ash'aris and a large group of the Mu'tazilites is that sin, whether major or minor, is not rationally impossible for the prophets, peace be upon them, before the mission. And some of our companions held that it is impossible, and this is the choice of al-Qadi.
Source pages p. 572

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on the infallibility ('isma) of prophets. It first establishes the general principle: once the truthfulness of messengers is confirmed by the miracle, it is obligatory to believe everything they report from Allah. Lying is rationally impossible for them, and sins are legally impossible — because we are commanded to follow them. If they could sin, we would be commanded to sin as well, but Allah does not command immorality (quoting Qur'an 7:28). The author then extends this to disliked acts (makrūh) and even permissible acts (mubāḥ) that occur in a manner different from others — meaning that prophets are protected from committing disliked acts and from engaging in permissible acts in a way that would be inappropriate. After this general statement, the passage divides the discussion into two parts: before prophethood and after prophethood. For the period before prophethood, the author presents two positions. The majority of Ash'aris and many Mu'tazilites hold that sin (both major and minor) is not rationally impossible for prophets before their prophetic mission. In other words, it is logically possible, though it may not actually occur. The second position, held by 'some of our companions' (a group of Ash'aris), asserts that such sin is impossible even before prophethood. This is the view preferred by al-Qadi (likely Abu Bakr al-Baqillani or another Ash'ari authority). The passage does not detail the position regarding sin after prophethood, but it implies it is also discussed in the second 'place' of the subject.

المعاصي شرعاً

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think infallibility only covers major sins.
Correction: The passage shows it covers lying, all sins, disliked acts, and even inappropriate permissible acts.

Checkpoint

A) The miracle
B) The consensus of scholars
C) The Qur'anic verse
D) Reason alone

Key Takeaway
Prophets must be believed in all they bring because of the miracle; lying and sin are impossible for them because we must follow them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that every word and action of the Prophet (peace be upon him) is free from any flaw, what specific aspect of his sunnah do you find hardest to accept? Will you commit to following it without reservation starting today?
?
The argument says that if sin were possible for the Prophet, we would be commanded to sin. Does your heart hold any excuse that the Prophet might have made a mistake in a matter you dislike? Confront that thought: how does this lesson dismantle it?
?
Since the Prophet is infallible even in permissible acts (so they are done in the best manner), what does that demand from you when you perform a permissible act like eating or sleeping? How can you align your intention to follow his example in those moments?

The Ruling on Prophets Before Prophethood
Sanusi presents two positions on whether prophets can sin before prophecy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two scholarly positions on whether prophets can sin before they receive prophethood, according to the Ash'ari and Mu'tazili schools.

Core Idea
Most Ash'aris and Mu'tazilites allow sin before prophethood; some Ash'aris disallow it.

After establishing the general principle of infallibility, Sanusi divides the topic into two time periods: before and after prophethood. In this passage, he presents the ruling on the period before prophethood. He outlines two positions: the majority view from the Ash'aris and many Mu'tazilites that sin (major or minor) is rationally possible before the prophetic mission, and the minority view held by some Ash'aris (including al-Qadi) that such sin is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the two positions
Read the Arabic text beginning from 'allāma fī mawḍi'ayn' to the end. Note how the author sets up the discussion as two places and then gives the rulings.
The first position is attributed to 'most Ash'aris and a large group of Mu'tazilites'.
2
Identify the two positions
Find the two positions: 1) Sin is not rationally impossible before prophethood. 2) Sin is impossible before prophethood. Note which groups hold each view.
The second view is attributed to 'some of our companions' and chosen by al-Qadi.
3
Understand the significance
Consider why this distinction matters. The majority view emphasizes that prophethood is a divine gift that protects after prophecy, but before it, the person is like any other. The minority view stresses the dignity of the prophetic lineage.

Proof of Prophetic Infallibility After Prophethood
Sanusi proves the necessity of believing prophets and the impossibility of sin for them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi proves that prophets must be believed and cannot sin after prophethood, using the evidence of miracles and the command to follow them.

Core Idea
Prophets cannot sin because we are commanded to emulate them.

Sanusi provides a concise but powerful argument for prophetic infallibility. He links the proof of a prophet's truthfulness—through miracles—to the obligation to believe everything they convey. Then he argues that sin is impossible for them, both rationally and by religious law, because if they could sin, we would be commanded to sin, which God forbids. This short passage thus establishes a key doctrine in Sunni theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the English translation. Note the flow from the opening topic to the concluding prayer.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two types of impossibility
The passage states that lying is rationally impossible (mustahil 'aqlan) and sins are impossible by religious law (mustahil shar'an). Understand the distinction between rational and legal impossibility.
Rational impossibility means it cannot occur by definition; legal impossibility means it is forbidden and never happens due to divine protection.
3
Understand the proof from emulation
The proof is that we are commanded to emulate the prophets. If they could sin, we would be commanded to sin, but God does not command evil. This is supported by Quran 7:28. Reflect on how this logic extends to disliked and even permissible actions.
This proof shows that prophetic infallibility is a necessary consequence of our obligation to follow them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في عصمة الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام وإذا عُلِمَ صدق الرسل بدلالة المعجزة وجب تصديقهم في كل ما أَتَوا به عن الله تعالى ويستحيل منهم الكذب عقلاً والمعاصي شرعاً لأننا مأمورون بالاقتداء بهم فلو جازت عليهم المعصية لكنا مأمورين بها قل إن الله لا يأمر بالفحشاء وبذلك تعرف عدم وقوع المكروه منهم أيضاً بل والمباح على الوجه الذي يقع من غيرهم وبالله تعالى التوفيق
The discussion on the infallibility of the prophets, peace and blessings be upon them. And when the truthfulness of the messengers is known by the evidence of the miracle, it is obligatory to believe them in everything they bring from Allah the Exalted. And lying is rationally impossible for them, and sins are impossible by religious law; because we are commanded to emulate them, so if sin were possible for them, we would be commanded to sin. Say: Indeed Allah does not command immorality [Al-A'raf: 28]. And by this you know that disliked things also do not occur from them, nor even permissible things in the manner that occurs from others. And success is from Allah the Exalted.
Source pages p. 572

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the infallibility of prophets after prophethood. It begins with the heading 'The discussion on the infallibility of the prophets.' Then it states that once we know the truthfulness of the messengers through the evidence of a miracle, we must believe them in everything they convey from God. Lying is rationally impossible; sins are impossible by religious law. The proof: we are commanded to follow them, so if they sinned, we would be commanded to sin, but God does not command evil (citing Quran 7:28). This logic extends to disliked and even permissible actions performed in the same way as others, implying prophets are free from all moral defects. The passage ends with a prayer for success.

Correction: This passage refutes that by the argument from divine command: if prophets could sin, we would be commanded to sin, but God does not command immorality.

Key Takeaway

Prophets' Infallibility Before Prophethood: Scholarly Positions
Sanusi classifies the discussion of infallibility before prophethood and enumerates the positions of different schools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four main scholarly positions on whether prophets could sin before their prophetic mission, and the rational or revelatory basis for each position.

Core Idea
Before prophethood, infallibility is debated even among Ash'aris.

This lesson explores the different opinions on whether prophets were infallible before their mission. While most Ash'aris and many Mu'tazila allow the possibility of sin, some Ash'aris, the Rawafid (Shi'a), and many Mu'tazila argue otherwise, each based on different premises involving reason and revelation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two aspects of the discussion
Sanusi begins by stating that the discussion on infallibility has two aspects: before prophethood and after. This lesson focuses on the before aspect.
This sets the overall structure of the topic.
2
List the four positions
Read the passage and list the four distinct views presented regarding the possibility of sin before prophethood. Notice the groups involved and whether they consider sin possible or impossible.
Pay attention to which groups agree or disagree.
3
Understand the basis for each position
For each position, identify the reasoning given. Some rely on rational possibility, others on the nature of sin requiring revelation, and some on rational evil (taqbīḥ ʿaqlī).
The basis determines the strength of the argument.
4
Note the reference to rational good and evil
Sanusi mentions that the corruption of rational good and evil has been discussed earlier. This is a key Ash'ari critique of the Mu'tazila and Rawafid position.
This shows the interconnectedness of theological principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في عصمة الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام في موضعين أحدهما قبل النبوة والثاني بعدها أما حكمهم قبل النبوة فالذي ذهب إليه أكثر الأشاعرة وطائفة كثيرة من المعتزلة إلى أنه لا يمتنع عقلاً على الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام قبل البعثة معصية كبيرة كانت أو صغيرة وذهب بعض أصحابنا إلى أنه يمتنع ذلك وهو مختار القاضي عياض على أنه قال تصور المسألة كالممتنع فإن المعاصي إنما تكون بعد تقرير الشرع إذ لا يعلم كون الفعل معصية إلا من الشرع وقال بعض أصحابنا الامتناع بالسمع إذ لا مجال للعقل لكن دل السمع بعد ورود الشرع على أنهم كانوا معصومين قبل البعثة وذهب الروافض إلى امتناع ذلك كله عليهم عقلاً ووافقهم أكثر المعتزلة في امتناع وقوع الكبائر منهم عقلاً قبل البعثة ومعتمد الفريقين التقبيح العقلي لأن صدور المعصية منهم مما يحقرهم في النفوس وينفر الطباع عن اتباعهم وهو خلاف ما اقتضت الحكمة من بعثة الرسل فيكون قبيحاً عقلاً وقد سبق الكلام على فساد أصل التحسين والتقبيح العقليين
The discussion on the infallibility of the prophets, peace and blessings be upon them, is in two places: one: before prophethood, and the second: after it. As for their ruling before prophethood: the view held by most Ash'aris and a large group of the Mu'tazila is that it is not rationally impossible for prophets, peace and blessings be upon them, before the mission, to commit a sin, whether major or minor. And some of our companions held that it is impossible, and this is the choice of Qadi Iyad, who said: 'Imagine the issue is as if impossible; for sins only occur after the establishment of revelation; since an act is not known to be a sin except from revelation.' And some of our companions said: the impossibility is by revelation; for reason has no role, but revelation after the arrival of religious law indicates that they were infallible before the mission. And the Rawafid held that all of that is rationally impossible for them, and most of the Mu'tazila agreed with them regarding the impossibility of committing major sins from them rationally before the mission. And the basis of both groups is rational evil (taqbīḥ ʿaqlī); because the occurrence of sin from them would demean them in souls and repel natures from following them, and that is contrary to what wisdom requires from the sending of messengers, so it would be rationally evil. And the discussion on the corruption of the principle of rational good and evil has preceded.
Source pages p. 572

Commentary
This passage is a classification and enumeration of positions on prophets' infallibility before prophethood. Sanusi first divides the discussion into two aspects: before and after prophethood. For the before aspect, he presents four views: (1) Most Ash'aris and many Mu'tazila hold that sin (major or minor) is rationally possible before the mission. (2) Some Ash'aris (including Qadi Iyad) hold it impossible; Qadi Iyad's rationale is that sin requires revelation, so before revelation the very concept is meaningless. (3) Others say impossibility is known by revelation (sam'), not reason. (4) The Rawafid (Shi'a) hold all sin rationally impossible before prophethood, and most Mu'tazila agree for major sins. The basis for groups (4) and some Mu'tazila is rational evil (taqbīḥ ʿaqlī): sin would demean prophets and repel followers, contradicting the purpose of sending them. Sanusi notes that the corruption of rational good and evil (taḥsīn and taqbīḥ ʿaqliyyayn) has been discussed earlier, hinting at the Ash'ari rejection of that principle.

عصمة الأنبياء
قبل النبوة
بعدها
أكثر الأشاعرة
المعتزلة
الروافض
القاضي عياض
التحسين والتقبيح العقليين
التقبيح العقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Ash'aris agree that prophets could sin before prophethood.
Correction: Sanusi notes that some Ash'aris (including Qadi Iyad) hold that sin is impossible before prophethood, showing internal disagreement.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the Rawafid and the Mu'tazila view that major sins are impossible for prophets before prophethood?
A) Revelation (samʿ)
B) Rational evil (taqbīḥ ʿaqlī)
C) Consensus (ijmāʿ)
D) Miracle evidence

Key Takeaway
The infallibility of prophets before prophethood is a subject of scholarly disagreement; the positions hinge on the role of reason versus revelation and the acceptance of rational good and evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage mentions that some scholars hold that sin before prophethood would 'demean them in souls and repel natures from following them'. Honestly examine your own heart: would learning of a sin committed by Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) before his prophethood affect your trust in him? What does your answer reveal about your true understanding of his humanity and his prophethood? Commit to one action today that strengthens your love for him regardless of such hypotheticals.
?
Qadi Iyad argued that sin is meaningless without revelation. Think of a mistake you made before you had full Islamic knowledge. Do you view that mistake as a sin? How does the Quran's promise of forgiveness for pre-guidance sins (e.g., Surah Al-Furqan 25:70) change your emotional response to your own past? Are you able to treat that mistake as a lesson rather than a burden? If not, what step can you take this week to release that weight?
?
This lesson reveals that even among Ash'aris, the infallibility of prophets before prophethood is debated. Scholars disagreed yet remained within the fold of Ahl al-Sunna. How do you handle theological disagreements with fellow Muslims? Do you dismiss them or seek to understand their reasoning? Challenge yourself to learn one other valid scholarly position on a different issue that you currently reject, and ask Allah for humility in your own beliefs.

The Miracle as Proof of Prophetic Truthfulness
Sanusi states the consensus on infallibility from deliberate lying after prophethood and the logical argument from the miracle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the consensus on prophetic infallibility from deliberate lying after prophethood is grounded in the evidential value of miracles.

Core Idea
The miracle proves truthfulness; if prophets could lie, miracles would lose their meaning.

This lesson explores a key consensus in Islamic theology: the infallibility of prophets from deliberate lying after they receive prophethood. The argument is anchored in the nature of miracles as divine confirmation of truthfulness. As you read the passage, notice how Sanusi uses a logical proof to show that if lying were possible, the miracle's evidential power would collapse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the claim and the proof structure.
Notice the concise style: Sanusi states consensus, gives the reason, then presents the conditional argument.
2
Identify the three key elements
Identify: (1) the consensus claim, (2) the specific scope (deliberate lying in rulings after prophethood), and (3) the miracle-based argument.
The scope is limited; this passage does not cover other sins or errors.
3
Analyze the logical structure
Break down the argument: Premise: Miracle indicates truthfulness. Conditional: If lying possible → miracle's evidence invalidated. Conclusion: Lying impossible. Note the logical necessity.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: allowing lying would destroy the miracle's reliability.
4
Reflect on the broader context
Consider that this is part of a larger discussion on prophetic infallibility (ʿiṣma). Earlier lessons likely cover infallibility before prophethood or from other sins.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question. Ensure you can explain why the argument works without adding information from outside this passage.
Focus on what Sanusi actually says, not general knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بعد النبوة فالإجماع على عصمتهم من تعمد الكذب في الأحكام لأن المعجزة دلت على صدقهم فيما يبلغونه عن الله تعالى فلو جاز تعمد الكذب عليهم لبطلت دلالة المعجزة على الصدق
And as for after prophethood: there is consensus on their infallibility from deliberately lying regarding rulings; because the miracle indicated their truthfulness in what they convey from Allah the Exalted. So if deliberate lying were possible for them, the evidence of the miracle for truthfulness would be invalidated.
Source pages p. 573

Commentary
This passage is a concise statement of the consensus (ijmāʿ) on the infallibility of prophets after prophethood with respect to deliberate lying in religious rulings. The proof is that the miracle establishes their truthfulness in conveying revelation. If lying were possible, the miracle would no longer serve as proof of truthfulness, because a liar could also perform a miracle. Therefore, to maintain the evidential value of the miracle, prophets must be infallible from deliberate falsehood in matters of religion.

Key Terms
إجماع
عصمتهم
تعمد الكذب
المعجزة
دلالة المعجزة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage implies prophets are infallible from all sins and errors after prophethood.
Correction: The passage only addresses deliberate lying in religious rulings. It does not cover other sins, unintentional errors, or statements outside rulings.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why is there consensus that prophets are infallible from deliberate lying after prophethood?
A) Because reason alone proves it.
B) Because the miracle testifies to their truthfulness, and allowing lying would invalidate that evidence.
C) Because it is mentioned in the Qur'an explicitly.
D) Because all scholars agreed on it without proof.

Key Takeaway
The consensus on prophetic infallibility from deliberate lying after prophethood is logically necessary because miracles would otherwise lose their evidential power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to entertain the possibility that the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) could have deliberately lied about a religious ruling, what would that do to your trust in the miracle of the Qur'an? Are you willing to admit any hidden doubt you might have, and what concrete step will you take today to silence it?
?
The lesson states that the miracle would lose its evidential power if lying were possible. In your own life, where do you act as if the Prophet's testimony is not fully reliable—perhaps by delaying obedience to a command or rationalizing a shortcut? What is one ruling you have struggled to accept, and how will you submit to it from this moment on?
?
Imagine standing before Allah and being asked: 'Did you truly believe My messengers were truthful in everything they conveyed?' Based on this lesson, is your heart fully convinced, or are there residual reservations? What will you change starting now to align your inner certainty with the logical necessity of their infallibility?

Views on Prophetic Infallibility Before Prophethood
Sanusi quotes Qadi 'Iyad and others on the impossibility of sins before prophethood, contrasting rational and scriptural proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different Islamic positions on whether prophets can sin before prophethood and the basis of each view.

Core Idea
Prophetic infallibility before prophethood is debated on rational vs. revealed grounds.

This lesson presents a key theological debate: could a prophet sin before receiving revelation? Sanusi quotes several scholars and groups who argue for impossibility on different grounds—some relying on revelation alone, others on rational evil. The passage also hints at a broader critique of rational ethics.

Guided Path
1
Understand Qadi 'Iyad's argument
Read the opening quote from Qadi 'Iyad, who argues that sin is defined only by revelation, so before revelation the concept of sin does not apply.
This makes the issue conceptually impossible.
2
Distinguish revealed vs. rational impossibility
Notice the difference between 'some companions' who rely on revelation, and the Rafidah and Mu'tazila who rely on rational evil. Identify how each group grounds its claim.
3
Recognize the critique of rational ethics
The author concludes with a reminder that rational good and evil (taḥsīn wa taqbīḥ 'aqliyyān) has already been refuted, implying the rationalist position is unsound.
This is a key methodological point in Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عياض، على أنَّهُ قالَ: (تصورُ المسألة كالممتنع؛ فإنَّ المعاصي إنَّما تكون بعد تقريرِ الشرع؛ إذ لا يُعلَمُ كونُ الفعل معصية إلا مِنَ الشرع. وقال بعضُ أصحابنا: الامتناع بالسمع؛ إذ لا مجال للعقل لكن دل السمعُ بعد ورودِ الشرع على أنَّهم كانوا معصومين قبل البعثة. وذهب الروافضُ: إلى امتناع ذلكَ كلِّه عليهم عقلاً، ووافقهم أكثر المعتزلة في امتناع وقوع الكبائر منهم عقلاً قبل البعثة، ومعتمد الفريقين: التقبيحُ العقلي؛ لأنَّ صدور المعصية منهم ممَّا يُحقِّرُهم في النفوس ويُنفِّرُ الطباعَ عنِ اتِّباعهم، وهو خلافُ ما اقتضَتِ الحكمةُ مِنْ بعثة الرُّسُلِ، فيكون قبيحاً عقلاً، وقد سبق الكلام على فسادِ أصل التحسين والتقبيح العقليين)
From Qadi 'Iyad: he said: 'The conceptualization of the issue is impossible, because acts of disobedience only occur after the establishment of the law, since an act is not known to be disobedience except from the law.' And some of our companions said: The impossibility is by revelation, for there is no scope for reason, but revelation after the coming of the law indicated that they were protected before prophethood. The Rafidah held: the impossibility of all that for them rationally, and most of the Mu'tazila agreed with them on the impossibility of committing major sins by them rationally before prophethood. The basis of the two groups is rational evil, because the occurrence of disobedience from them degrades them in souls and alienates natures from following them, and that contradicts the requirement of wisdom in sending messengers, so it would be rationally evil. And the discussion has preceded on the corruption of the principle of rational approval and disapproval.
Source pages p. 573

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the impossibility of prophets committing sins before prophethood. It begins by quoting Qadi 'Iyad, who declares that imagining such a thing is impossible because sin is only defined by divine law, and before the law there is no sin. Then some of the author's companions (Ash'aris) argue that the impossibility is established by revelation, not reason, but revelation after the coming of law shows they were protected before. Then the Rafidah (Shi'a) and most Mu'tazila argue for rational impossibility, based on rational evil: that any sin would degrade the prophet and repel followers, contradicting the wisdom of sending them. The author then adds a parenthetical reminder that the foundation of rational good and evil has already been refuted, indicating his rejection of that basis.

تحسين وتقبيح عقليين
روافض
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Islamic schools agree that prophets are infallible before prophethood on rational grounds.
Correction: The passage shows that Ash'aris (Qadi 'Iyad and some companions) rely on conceptual or revealed grounds, not rational, and the author critiques the rationalist position.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis of the Rafidah and Mu'tazila for holding that prophets cannot sin before prophethood?
A) Revelation (sam')
B) Rational evil (taqbih 'aqli)
C) Consensus (ijma')
D) The miracle of the prophet

Key Takeaway
This passage shows that the basis for prophetic infallibility before prophethood varies among schools, with Ash'aris relying on revelation and rationalists on rational evil, which the author refutes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The passage warns against relying on rational evil to establish prophetic infallibility. In your own life, where do you find yourself trusting your own sense of right and wrong over what Allah has commanded? What step can you take today to submit more fully to revelation even when it contradicts your reasoning?

Consensus on Protection from Deliberate Lying After Prophethood
Sanusi states the consensus on prophetic protection from deliberate lies after prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that scholars unanimously agree that after prophethood, prophets are protected from deliberately lying in religious rulings, and that this protection is proven by the miracle that confirms their truthfulness.

Core Idea
The miracle proves prophets cannot lie deliberately after prophethood.

Having established that prophets are protected from lying before prophethood, Sanusi now turns to the period after their prophethood. Here he presents a consensus on their protection from deliberate lies specifically in the context of conveying rulings. This protection is directly tied to the miracle, which serves as the proof of their truthfulness.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the concise structure: a claim ('there is consensus') followed by a proof ('because the miracle...').
Pay attention to the key term 'عصمة' (protection) and 'إجماع' (consensus).
2
Identify the claim and its scope
The claim is that after prophethood, prophets are protected (ma'sum) from deliberately lying in rulings (al-ahkam). This means they can intentionally lie only about non-ruling matters? No, the text specifies 'in rulings' – the consensus is limited to judgments they convey from God.
3
Understand the proof from the miracle
The proof is that the miracle (mu'jiza) has already proven their truthfulness in what they convey from Allah. If intentional lying were possible, the miracle's indication of truthfulness would be nullified. This is a logical argument: if the miracle confirms truth, any possibility of falsehood would contradict it.
Think of the miracle as a divine stamp of approval on the prophet's message.
4
Reflect on the consensus
The consensus ('ijma') is not about all forms of error – the passage only addresses deliberate lying. This distinction prevents misunderstanding: prophets can still make unintentional mistakes in non-ruling matters? The text does not say, but the focus here is on deliberate falsehood in conveying divine commands.
This is a key nuance often missed by students.
5
Apply the lesson
Consider why this protection is essential: if a prophet could lie after being confirmed by miracles, believers could never trust any ruling. The miracle guarantees that the message remains reliable.
This links to the broader theme of prophethood and trustworthiness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا بعد النبوَّةِ: فالإجماع على عصمتهم مِنْ تعمد الكذب في الأحكام؛ لأنَّ المعجزة دلَّت على صدقهم فيما يُبلِّغونَهُ عن الله تعالى، فلو جازَ تعمُّدُ الكذب عليهم لبطلت دلالة المعجزة على الصدق.
As for after prophethood: there is consensus on their protection from deliberate lying in legal rulings, because the miracle indicated their truthfulness in what they convey from God Almighty; so if deliberate lying were possible for them, the miracle's indication of truthfulness would be invalidated.
Source pages p. 574

Commentary
This passage states the unanimous agreement that after prophethood, prophets are protected from deliberately lying when conveying religious rulings. The proof is the miracle that confirms their truthfulness. If they could lie intentionally, the miracle would lose its evidential force.

Key Terms
عصمة
إجماع
معجزة
كذب
أحكام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The consensus applies to all forms of error (including unintentional mistakes) in all matters.
Correction: The passage only states protection (isma) from deliberate lying (ta'ammud al-kidhb) in rulings (al-ahkam). It does not claim protection from forgetfulness or mistakes in non-ruling matters.

Checkpoint
What is the proof given for the consensus that prophets cannot deliberately lie after prophethood?
A) Reason
B) The miracle

Key Takeaway
The miracle that accompanies prophethood guarantees that prophets cannot deliberately lie when conveying rulings from Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What does that say about your trust in his truthfulness? Today, identify one such teaching and make a concrete plan to act on it, acknowledging that the one who brought it was truthful beyond doubt.
?
The consensus is that prophets are protected from deliberate lying in legal rulings. But what about you? When you speak about Islam to others, do you check your sources and ensure you are not misrepresenting Allah? Think of a time you spoke without certainty. Commit now to only speak with knowledge, and if you are unsure, to say 'I don't know.' Write down one situation where you will exercise this discipline.

Can Prophets Err in Rulings? The Debate on Forgetful Lies
Sanusi presents two Ash'ari views on prophetic lies by mistake, and a clarification on proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate between al-Ustadh and al-Qadi on whether prophets could lie in rulings by mistake or forgetfulness, and the different proofs they used.

Core Idea
The miracle only guarantees intentional truth — but does it cover mistakes?

Sanusi presents a nuanced disagreement among Ash'ari scholars. While all agree a prophet cannot deliberately lie, they differ on whether a prophetic mistake in a ruling could constitute a lie. Al-Ustadh sees the miracle as a guarantee of all prophetic statements, while al-Qadi limits it to intentional ones. This passage clarifies the point of contention and its underlying proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the two positions and the clarifying quote from al-Qadi 'Iyad.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two scholars and their positions
Al-Ustadh forbids lying in rulings by mistake; al-Qadi permits it. Understand their reasoning: al-Ustadh says it contradicts the miracle; al-Qadi says the miracle only covers intentional statements.
3
Understand the role of miracles
The miracle is a decisive proof (dalala qati'a) of prophetic truthfulness. How each scholar defines the scope of that proof is the key difference.
The miracle stands in place of God saying 'My servant has spoken truthfully' according to al-Ustadh.
4
Analyze al-Qadi 'Iyad's clarification
He states there is no disagreement on the impossibility of such lies—only on the evidence. Al-Ustadh relies on the miracle, al-Qadi on other scriptural evidence.
This shows the disagreement is about method, not outcome.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا جواز صدورِ الكذب منهم في الأحكام غلطاً أو نسياناً: فمنعه الأستاذ وطائفةٌ كبيرةٌ مِنْ أصحابنا؛ لما فيهِ مِنْ مناقضة دَلالة المعجزة القاطعة، وجوَّزَهُ القاضي وقال: إنَّ المعجزةَ إِنَّما دَلَّتْ على صدقهم فيما يصدر عنهم قصداً واعتقاداً، قال القاضي عياض: ( لا خلاف في امتناعِهِ سهواً أو غلطاً، لكن عندَ الأستاذ بدليل المعجزة القائمةِ مَقامَ قول الله تعالى: صدق عبدي، وعند القاضي بدليل الشرع ) (انظر « الشفا » ( ص ٦٤٢ ) )
As for the possibility of them committing lies in rulings by mistake or forgetfulness: al-Ustadh and a large group of our companions forbade it, because it contains contradiction of the decisive indication of the miracle. And al-Qadi permitted it and said: The miracle only indicates their truthfulness in what occurs from them intentionally and with belief. Al-Qadi 'Iyad said: 'There is no disagreement on its impossibility by forgetfulness or mistake, but according to al-Ustadh by the evidence of the miracle which stands in the place of God's saying: My servant has spoken truthfully, and according to al-Qadi by the evidence of the law.' (Look at 'Al-Shifa' p. 642).
Source pages p. 574

Commentary
This passage discusses whether prophets could mistakenly or forgetfully utter falsehood in rulings. Two positions: al-Ustadh (al-Isfara'ini) and many Ash'aris forbid it because it would contradict the miracle's decisive proof. Al-Qadi al-Baqillani permits it, arguing that the miracle only guarantees intentional truth. Al-Qadi 'Iyad clarifies that both agree it is impossible, but their proofs differ: al-Ustadh uses the miracle as direct evidence from God saying 'My servant has spoken truthfully', while al-Qadi uses other scriptural evidence.

Key Terms
الأستاذ
القاضي
معجزة
غَلط
نسيان
دلالة قاطعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The disagreement is about whether lying by mistake can actually occur.
Correction: Both al-Ustadh and al-Qadi agree that it is impossible; their disagreement is about the proof that establishes this impossibility.

Checkpoint
According to al-Qadi 'Iyad's clarification, what is the status of the disagreement?
A) Al-Ustadh permits, al-Qadi forbids
B) Both permit
C) Both forbid but differ on the proof
D) Both forbid with the same proof

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari debate on prophetic mistakes in rulings is not about whether they can happen but about what constitutes the proof of their impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the miracle only guarantees the Prophet's truth in what he intends, how does that affect your trust in hadith that seem difficult to reconcile with your own reasoning? Will you reconsider your understanding before doubting the text?
?
Al-Qadi 'Iyad says there is no disagreement that prophets cannot lie even by mistake. If you ever catch yourself thinking 'maybe the Prophet was wrong about this ruling,' what does that reveal about your commitment to revelation? Will you commit to never using 'mistake' as an excuse to disregard a prophetic command?
?
The debate is about the proof, not the impossibility. Which scholar's position makes you more careful in applying the Sunnah? Does your heart lean towards absolute trust (al-Ustadh) or towards relying on other evidence (al-Qadi)? What does that say about your spiritual state?

Consensus on Protection from Major and Vile Minor Sins
Sanusi states the consensus that prophets are protected from intentionally committing major sins and vile minor sins, against the Khawarij.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the consensus that prophets are protected from intentionally committing major sins and vile minor sins, and which sect opposed this.

Core Idea
All prophets are protected from major and vile minor sins, by consensus.

In the previous lesson, Sanusi established that prophets are protected from lying. Now he extends that protection to all other sins, making a clear distinction: they are protected from intentionally committing major sins and vile minor sins. This is a point of consensus among Ahl al-Sunnah, in contrast to the Khawarij who denied such protection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the straightforward meaning.
Notice that the passage begins with 'فالإجماع' (thus consensus), linking to the previous discussion.
2
Identify the two categories of sins
The passage mentions 'major sins' (الكبائر) and 'vile minor sins' (صغائر الخسة). Understand that not all minor sins are included—only those that are vile.
3
Note the opposing sect
The passage explicitly names 'some Khawarij' as the group that opposed this consensus. Reflect on why they held a different view.
The Khawarij believed prophets could commit major sins, which is a serious deviation.
4
Understand the significance of consensus
This is a matter of ijma (consensus) among the scholars of Ahl al-Sunnah. Knowing that this is a settled point helps avoid confusion.
Consensus is a strong evidence in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Thus consensus, and as for other sins not mentioned, verbal and actual: they are protected from deliberately committing major sins and vile minor sins, contrary to some Khawarij.

Commentary

This is against the Khawarij who claimed otherwise. The mention of 'other sins' refers back to the earlier discussion of lying.

Commit this week to avoid one such sin you have been tolerating.
?

Think of a specific command you have been ignoring. Will you accept it today, or will you follow the path of those who deny?

Refuting al-Amidi on Prophetic Forgetful Sins
Sanusi refutes al-Amidi's claim about prophetic forgetful sins and presents two lines of proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi rejects al-Amidi's claim that scholars permit prophets committing sins forgetfully, and the two lines of evidence used to prove the impossibility.

Core Idea
Consensus, not permission, surrounds prophetic forgetful sins.

Al-Amidi, a prominent scholar, asserted that only the Rafidah believe prophets cannot commit sins forgetfully. Sanusi vigorously rejects this, claiming instead that there is consensus on its impossibility. This passage shows how scholars use both revelation and reason to defend prophetic infallibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify al-Amidi's claim and Sanusi's response
Find the exact wording of al-Amidi's claim and Sanusi's rejection. Note how Sanusi introduces his refutation ('This statement is not correct').
Al-Amidi's claim is quoted directly; Sanusi counters with 'rather they agreed on its impossibility'.
3
Distinguish the two groups of scholars
Identify the two groups Sanusi presents: al-Qadi and the investigators (using revelation), and al-Ustadh and a large group (using reason as well). Understand why a group adds reason.
This distinction shows that even among those who accept the impossibility, the evidence can differ.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Ask yourself: why does this matter for Sunni aqeedah? The consensus protects the doctrine of 'isma (prophetic infallibility) from any exception.
This connects to the broader topic of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا إتيانُ ذلك نسياناً أو غلطاً: فقالَ الآمدي: ( اتفق الكلُّ على جوازه سوى الروافض ) !(۲) وهذا الذي ذكرَ لا يصح، بل اتفقوا على امتناعه؛ فقال القاضي والمحققونَ: بدليل السمع (۳)، وقال الأستاذ وطائفةٌ كبيرةٌ منَّا وَمِنَ المعتزلة: وبدليل العقل أيضاً. (۲) انظر « أبكار الأفكار » ( ٤ / ١٤٥ ) . (۳) وهو هنا الإجماع
As for committing that by forgetfulness or mistake: al-Amidi said: 'All agreed on its possibility except the Rafidah!' (2) And this statement is not correct; rather they agreed on its impossibility. So al-Qadi and the investigators said: by evidence of revelation (3), and al-Ustadh and a large group of us and of the Mu'tazila said: also by evidence of reason. (2) Look at 'Abkar al-Afkar' 4/145. (3) This here is consensus.
Source pages p. 574

Commentary
This passage addresses the possibility of prophets committing major or vile sins forgetfully. Al-Amidi claimed that all except the Rafidah (Shi'a) permit it. The author rejects this, asserting that in fact there is consensus on its impossibility. The proof differs: al-Qadi (al-Baqillani) and the investigators use revelation (specifically consensus), while al-Ustadh and a large group (including some Mu'tazila) also use reason. The footnotes provide sources.

Correction: In fact, there is consensus that it is impossible for prophets to commit sins forgetfully, as Sanusi states.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Sanusi asserts that there is consensus among scholars that prophets cannot commit sins forgetfully, supported by revelation and reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What belief about your faith are you holding onto without evidence, and what would it take for you to re-examine it today?

Prophets' Infallibility from Error in Rulings
Sanusi presents the dispute over prophets erring in rulings and quotes Qadi Iyad's resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly dispute regarding whether prophets can err in rulings due to forgetfulness or mistake, and how both sides ultimately affirm infallibility.

Core Idea
Prophetic infallibility is agreed upon; the dispute is only over its proof.

This passage presents a key theological dispute among Ash'ari scholars: can a prophet inadvertently lie in a ruling? While some argued that the miracle prohibits any error, others held that the miracle only guarantees intentional truthfulness. Yet both sides affirm that prophets are protected from error in rulings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage presents two opposing views and then a resolution.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage mentions 'al-Ustadh' and 'al-Qadi' with differing views on whether lying due to mistake or forgetfulness is permissible for prophets. Find each and note their arguments.
Al-Ustadh represents the majority Ash'ari view; al-Qadi is likely al-Baqillani.
3
Understand the resolution by al-Qadi 'Iyad
Al-Qadi 'Iyad states that both sides agree on the impossibility of such error, but differ on the proof: al-Ustadh relies on the miracle, al-Qadi relies on the revealed law.
This shows that the disagreement is not over the doctrine of infallibility but over its evidentiary basis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا جواز صدورِ الكذب منهم في الأحكام غلطاً أو نسياناً : فمنعه الأستاذ وطائفةٌ كبيرةٌ مِنْ أصحابنا ؛ لما فيهِ مِنْ مناقضة دَلالة المعجزة القاطعة ، وجوَّزَهُ القاضي وقال : إنَّ المعجزةَ إِنَّما دَلَّتْ على صدقهم فيما يصدر عنهم قصداً و اعتقاداً ، قال القاضي عياض : ( لا خلاف في امتناعِهِ سهواً أو غلطاً ، لكن عندَ الأستاذ بدليل المعجزة القائمةِ مَقامَ قول الله تعالى : صدق عبدي ، وعند القاضي بدليل الشرع )
As for the permissibility of lying occurring from them in rulings due to mistake or forgetfulness: al-Ustadh and a large group of our companions prevented it, because it contradicts the conclusive indication of the miracle; and al-Qadi permitted it and said: 'The miracle only indicates their truthfulness in what issues from them intentionally and with conviction.' Al-Qadi 'Iyad said: 'There is no disagreement regarding its impossibility due to forgetfulness or mistake, but according to al-Ustadh it is by the evidence of the miracle which stands in the place of Allah's statement: 'My servant has spoken the truth', and according to al-Qadi it is by the evidence of the revealed law.'
Source pages p. 574

Commentary
This passage presents the scholarly dispute regarding whether prophets can lie or err in their rulings (ahkam) due to forgetfulness or mistake. It begins by stating the view of al-Ustadh (Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini) and a large group of Ash'ari scholars: they forbid it because it would contradict the definitive evidence of the miracle (mu'jiza) which confirms their truthfulness. Then it presents the view of al-Qadi (al-Baqillani or al-Qadi 'Iyad?) who permitted it, arguing that the miracle only proves their truthfulness in what they intend and believe, not in unintentional errors. The passage then quotes al-Qadi 'Iyad directly to clarify that both agree on the impossibility of such errors, but disagree on the proof: al-Ustadh relies on the miracle itself acting as Allah's testimony that the prophet is truthful, while al-Qadi relies on the revealed law (shar'). Thus both affirm infallibility from error in rulings, but differ on the evidential basis.

Key Terms
الكذب
الأحكام
غلطاً
نسياناً
الأستاذ
القاضي
المعجزة
صدق
الشرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the permissibility of error in rulings contradicts prophetic infallibility.
Correction: Both sides affirm infallibility; they only differ on whether the proof is the miracle or the revealed law.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, on what basis does al-Qadi permit the possibility of prophets erring in rulings?
A) Because the miracle only indicates truthfulness in what they intend and believe.
B) Because scripture explicitly allows it.
C) Because it is impossible to prevent forgetfulness.
D) Because the Ustadh's argument is weak.

Key Takeaway
Prophets are infallible in their rulings, but scholars debated whether the proof lies in the miracle itself or in the revealed law.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever excused a sin by saying you didn't intend it or it was a mistake? This lesson shows that even prophets are protected from such errors—does that knowledge change how you view your own unintentional wrongs? What step will you take today to be more mindful of even your slip-ups?
?
The scholars differ on the proof of infallibility, but they agree on the fact. In your own life, are you more convinced by miracles (like the Quran) or by the commands of the shari'a? Which one makes you more certain that the Prophet is truthful? How can you strengthen that certainty?

Prophetic Infallibility from Intentional Major and Base Minor Sins
Sanusi asserts prophetic protection from intentional major and base minor sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that prophets are divinely protected from intentionally committing major sins and base minor sins, and that this is the orthodox view contrary to some Khawarij.

Core Idea
Prophets are protected from intentional major and base minor sins by divine infallibility.

Sanusi continues his discussion of prophetic infallibility by addressing sins other than those related to conveying rulings. He asserts that prophets are protected from intentionally committing major sins and base minor sins, a view held by the majority and opposed only by some Khawarij. This lesson establishes the scope of infallibility regarding intentional sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Focus on the claim about intentional sins.
Notice the phrase 'خلافاً لبعض الخوارج' which signals the opposing view.
2
Identify the two categories of sins
The passage mentions 'major sins' (الكبائر) and 'base minor sins' (صغائر الخسَّة). Understand what each refers to.
Base minor sins are those that indicate a low character, such as lying or stealing.
3
Note the sectarian opposition
The Khawarij held that prophets could commit major sins before prophethood. Sanusi rejects this.
4
Reflect on the doctrine's importance
Why is it essential that prophets are protected from such sins? Consider their role as role models.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا غيرُ المذكورِ مِنَ المعاصي القولية والفعلية : على عصمتهم مِنْ تعمُّدِ الكبائرِ وصغائر الخسَّة ، خلافاً لبعض الخوارج . وأمَّا إتيانُ ذلك نسياناً أو غلطاً : فقالَ الآمدي : ( اتفق الكلُّ على جوازه سوى الروافض ) ! وهذا الذي ذكرَ لا يصح ، بل اتفقوا على امتناعه ؛ فقال القاضي والمحققونَ : بدليل السمع ، وقال الأستاذ وطائفةٌ كبيرةٌ منَّا وَمِنَ المعتزلة : وبدليل العقل أيضاً
As for other than what was mentioned of verbal and actual sins: they are protected from intentional commission of major sins and base minor sins, contrary to some of the Khawarij. And as for committing that unintentionally or by mistake: al-Amidi said: 'All have agreed on its permissibility except the Rawafid!' But what he mentioned is not correct; rather they have agreed on its impossibility. So al-Qadi and the verifying scholars said: by the evidence of transmitted revelation; and al-Ustadh and a large group from us and from the Mu'tazila said: and by the evidence of reason as well.
Source pages p. 574

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of prophetic infallibility by addressing sins other than falsehood in rulings. The author first states that prophets are protected from intentionally committing major sins (kaba'ir) and base minor sins (sagha'ir al-khissa), contrary to some Khawarij who claimed otherwise. Then the discussion turns to unintentional commission of such sins. The author reports al-Amidi's claim that everyone except the Rawafid permits it, but then refutes this, asserting that there is actually consensus on its impossibility. Two lines of proof are given: al-Qadi and the verifying scholars rely on transmitted evidence (sam'), while al-Ustadh and a large group from both the Ash'aris and Mu'tazila add rational evidence ('aql). Thus the lesson establishes that prophets are preserved from both intentional and unintentional major and base minor sins, based on consensus.

الكبائر
صغائر الخسَّة
الخوارج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think prophets can commit major sins intentionally, especially before prophethood.
Correction: The passage affirms that prophets are protected from intentionally committing major sins and base minor sins, contrary to the Khawarij.

Checkpoint

A) The Ash'aris
B) Some Khawarij
C) Al-Amidi
D) The Rawafid

Key Takeaway
Prophets are divinely protected from intentionally committing major sins and base minor sins, contrary to the Khawarij.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that prophets are protected from intentionally committing degrading sins. Do you treat the Prophet's ﷺ character with the same reverence? Are there areas of your life where you follow your own desires rather than his example? Today, pick one aspect of his character to emulate consciously.
?
The Khawarij held a contrary view. Are there any modern influences that make you question the Prophet's perfection? How can you actively protect your heart from such doubts? Commit to reciting one dua for steadfastness.

Unintentional Sins: Consensus on Impossibility
Sanusi refutes al-Amidi and asserts consensus on impossibility of unintentional sins for prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that there is consensus that prophets cannot commit sins unintentionally, and that this is supported by both transmitted and rational evidence.

Core Idea
Unintentional commission of sins by prophets is impossible by consensus, proven by revelation and reason.

After establishing protection from intentional sins, Sanusi turns to unintentional commission. He reports al-Amidi's claim that all except the Rawafid allow it, but then refutes it, asserting consensus on impossibility. This lesson highlights the unanimous doctrine and the two lines of evidence supporting it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on unintentional sins

2
Identify al-Amidi's claim and the refutation
Al-Amidi claimed consensus on permissibility except the Rawafid. Sanusi says this is incorrect; the real consensus is on impossibility.
3
Understand the two proofs
Al-Qadi and the verifying scholars rely on transmitted revelation (sam'), while al-Ustadh and others add rational evidence ('aql).
Note that both groups agree on the impossibility; they differ only on the source of evidence.
4
Reflect on the significance
Why is it important that prophets are protected from unintentional sins? How does this affect their credibility?

The Disagreement on Non-Base Minor Sins
Sanusi presents the disagreement and the arguments of the forbidding scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreement among scholars regarding whether prophets can commit minor sins that are not base, and the key arguments of those who forbid it.

Core Idea
Are prophets immune even from non-base minor sins? The verifying scholars say yes.

Sanusi now turns to a subtle category of sins: minor sins that are not base. While the majority permitted them, a group of verifying scholars argued for complete immunity. Their reasoning is rooted in the nature of prophetic obedience and the command to follow them. Let's explore their arguments.

2
Examine the three reasons

The three reasons start with 'قالوا : لاختلافِ الناس...'
3
Reflect on the significance

وأمَّا الصغائر التي لا خسَّة فيها : فجوَّزَها عمداً وسهواً الأكثرونَ ، وبه قال أبو جعفر الطبري مِنْ أصحابنا ، ومنعته طائفةٌ مِنَ المحققينَ مِنَ الفقهاء والمتكلمينَ عمداً أو سهواً ، قالوا : لاختلافِ الناس في الصغائر ، ولأنَّ جماعة ذهبوا إلى أنَّ كلَّ ما عُصِيَ اللهُ بهِ فهو كبيرةٌ ، ولأنَّ الله تعالى أمر باتِّباعهم ، وأفعالهم يجب الاقتداء بها عند أكثر المالكيَّةِ وبعض الشافعيّةِ والحنفيَّةِ ، فلو جازَتْ منهم المعصية لكُنَّا مأمورين باتباعهم فيها . قلتُ : وبهذا تعرفُ عدم جواز وقوع المكروه منهم ؛ فالحق أنَّ أفعالهم دائرة بين الوجوبِ والندبِ والإباحة ، وليس وقوع المباح منهم كوقوعِهِ مِنْ غيرِهم ؛ وهو أنْ يقع بحسَبِ مقتضى الشهوة ، بل لعظيم معرفتهم بالله تعالى وخوفهم منه ، واطلاعهم على ما لم يطلع عليه غيرهم . لا يصدرُ منهم المباحُ إلا على وجه يصيرُ في حقهم طاعةً وقُربةً ؛ كقصدهم تشريعه ، أو التقوي بهِ على طاعة الله تعالى ، ونحوَ ذلكَ ممَّا يليقُ بمقاماتِهم الرفيعة ، وإذا كانَ أهلُ المراقبة مِنْ أولياءِ اللهِ تعالى بلغوا في الخوفِ منهُ تعالى ورسوخ المعرفة ما منعهم أن تصدر منهم حركة أو سكون في غيرِ رضاهُ تعالى .. فكيف بأنبيائِهِ تعالى ؟! ورسلِهِ صلواتُ الله وسلامه على جميعهم
As for minor sins that are not base: the majority permitted them intentionally and unintentionally, and Abu Ja'far al-Tabari from our companions held that view; and a group of verifying scholars among the jurists and theologians forbade them intentionally or unintentionally. They said: because of people's disagreement regarding minor sins, and because a group held that everything by which Allah is disobeyed is a major sin, and because Allah Most High commanded following them, and their actions must be emulated according to most Malikis and some Shafi'is and Hanafis; so if disobedience were permissible from them, we would be commanded to follow them in it. I say: and by this you know the impermissibility of the occurrence of the disliked (makruh) from them. So the truth is that their actions revolve around obligation, recommendation, and permissibility. And the occurrence of the permissible from them is not like its occurrence from others; which is that it occurs according to the dictates of desire, but rather because of their great knowledge of Allah Most High and fear of Him, and their awareness of what others are not aware of. The permissible does not issue from them except in a manner that becomes in their case obedience and closeness, such as intending to legislate it, or strengthening oneself by it for obedience to Allah Most High, and similar things befitting their exalted stations. And if the people of spiritual vigilance among the friends of Allah Most High have reached such a degree of fear of Him and firmness of knowledge that prevents them from any movement or stillness not in His pleasure, then how much more so for His prophets and messengers, may Allah's prayers and peace be upon all of them.
Source pages p. 575

Commentary
This passage addresses minor sins that are not base (sagha'ir la khissa fiha). It presents the disagreement: the majority, including Abu Ja'far al-Tabari, permit them intentionally and unintentionally. However, a group of verifying scholars forbid them altogether. Their reasoning is threefold: (1) People disagree on what constitutes a minor sin, so it is safer to forbid all; (2) some scholars hold that all disobedience is major; (3) since Allah commands following the prophets and their actions are to be emulated, if they could commit sins we would be commanded to emulate them in sin. The author then interjects with 'I say' (qultu) to give his own conclusion: based on this, even the disliked (makruh) is impermissible from them. The true position is that prophets' actions are limited to obligatory, recommended, and permissible. However, even their permissible actions differ from ordinary people's; they are not driven by desire but by deeper knowledge and fear of Allah, so that their permissible acts become acts of worship (ta'a and qurba) such as intending to legislate or using it as a support for obedience. The passage concludes with a rhetorical comparison: if saintly people (awliya') are so cautious that they avoid any movement not in Allah's pleasure, then prophets are certainly even more so. Thus the lesson establishes a very high standard of prophetic infallibility, extending even to minor and permissible actions.

Key Terms
الصغائر
الخسَّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that prophets can commit any minor sin because the majority permitted them.
Correction: The verifying scholars argued against this, providing strong reasons that elevate prophetic infallibility to include non-base minor sins.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the reasons given by the forbidding scholars?
A) People disagree on minor sins.
B) All disobedience is a major sin.
C) Prophets are not subject to desires.
D) Following prophets is obligatory.

Key Takeaway
The verifying scholars argued that prophets must be free even from non-base minor sins, because otherwise we would be commanded to follow them in disobedience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a 'small' sin that you consider harmless, do you still feel the weight of disobeying Allah? The verifying scholars argued that from prophets, even minor disobedience is unthinkable. How does that raise the bar for your own standard of obedience?
?
If you were commanded to follow a prophet in every action, would there be any 'minor' habit in your life that you would have to abandon because it contradicts prophetic character? What would that be, and why is it hard to let go?
?
The majority permitted non-base minor sins from prophets, yet the verifying scholars forbade them. Whose side do you find yourself leaning toward, and what does your choice reveal about how seriously you take sin—even the 'small' ones?

Sanusi's Conclusion: Permissible Acts Become Worship
Sanusi concludes that prophets cannot commit even makruh and that their permissible actions are elevated to acts of worship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's conclusion that prophets' permissible actions are not like ours but become acts of worship due to their profound knowledge and fear of Allah.

Core Idea
For prophets, even the permissible becomes worship.

After presenting the disagreement, Sanusi gives his own decisive view. He argues that not only are prophets free from sin, but even their permissible actions are transformed into acts of closeness to Allah. This is because of their unique awareness and fear. Let's unpack his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read Sanusi's own statement
Start from 'قلتُ : وبهذا تعرفُ...' and read through to the end. Notice the progression from impermissibility of makruh to the nature of mubah.
The phrase 'قلتُ' marks Sanusi's personal intervention.
2
Understand the three categories
Sanusi says prophets' actions are limited to wajib, mandub, and mubah. Why is makruh excluded? Connect it to the previous lesson.
Makruh is impossible because it is a form of disobedience.
3
Grasp the special nature of prophets' mubah
Sanusi explains that prophets' permissible acts are not driven by desire but by deeper motives like legislation or strengthening for worship. This turns mubah into worship.
Consider the analogy with the awliya' (friends of Allah) who are extremely cautious.

?

?

?
