The Logical Proof for Muhammad's Prophethood
Sanusi presents the syllogistic proof for Muhammad's prophethood using miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi constructs a categorical syllogism to prove the prophethood of Muhammad (saw) using the miracle as evidence.

Core Idea
Miracles are the rational proof of prophethood, when no one can challenge them.

Sanusi now presents the formal proof for Muhammad's prophethood. He uses a syllogism that any student of kalam must understand: a minor premise from history, a major premise from theology, and an unassailable conclusion. This passage shows how empiricism and logic work together in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the syllogism carefully
Identify its three parts: minor premise, major premise, and conclusion. Notice how each part builds on the previous.
The syllogism is the core of the proof.
2
Understand the minor premise
The minor premise states that Muhammad claimed prophethood and a miracle appeared. This is proven by tawatur (mass transmission), which yields certain knowledge.
Tawatur is a key concept in Islamic epistemology.
3
Examine the major premise
The major premise states that anyone with a claim and an unimpeachable miracle is a true messenger. This was established earlier in the discussion on miracles.
This premise connects the particular to the universal.
4
Note the mention of Jewish deniers
The author notes that among the rejectors are the Jews, who are divided into two groups. This sets up a potential objection or elaboration.
The passage breaks off before detailing the groups.
5
Reflect on the rational nature of the proof
Consider why this syllogism is considered a valid rational proof. It uses observable facts (miracle) and logical necessity.
This counters the misconception that prophethood cannot be proven logically.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان اثبات الرساله سند نا محمت صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم
ونبينا ومولانا محمد صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ قد عُلِمَ ضرورة ادّعاؤُهُ الرسالة، وتحدى بمعجزاتِ لا يُحاط بها
نظم الدليل في إثباتِ نبوَّة نبينا ومولانا محمد صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ أنْ يُقالَ : نبينا ومولانا محمد صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ ادَّعى الرسالةَ ، وظهر الخارقُ على وَفْقِ دعواهُ معَ العجز عن معارضتِهِ ، وكلُّ مَنِ ادَّعى الرسالة وظهر الخارقُ على وَفْقِ دعواهُ معَ العجز عن معارضته.. فهو رسولُ الله تعالى ؛ ينتج : أنَّ نبينا ومولانا محمداً صلى الله عليهِ وسلَّمَ رسولُ الله جل وعزَّ .
أما الصغرى : فهي معلومة بالتواتر الذي ينقلُـه الـمـوافـقُ والمخالف ، والتواتر يفيد العلم ضرورةً على ما تقرَّرَ في أصول الفقه ، وأمَّا دليلُ الكبرى : فقد تقدَّمَ في وجهِ دلالة المعجزة
واعلم : أنَّ مِنَ المنكرينَ لنبوَّة نبينا ومولانا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلّم : اليهود ، وهم فرقتان :
Chapter on explaining the proof of the message of our master Muhammad, may Allah bless him and grant him peace. And our Prophet and Master Muhammad, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, his claim to prophethood is known necessarily, and he challenged with miracles that cannot be encompassed. The arrangement of the proof for the prophethood of our Prophet and Master Muhammad, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, is that it is said: Our Prophet and Master Muhammad, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, claimed prophethood, and a miraculous event appeared in accordance with his claim with the inability to oppose it. And everyone who claimed prophethood and a miraculous event appeared in accordance with his claim with inability to oppose it, then he is a Messenger of Allah Almighty. This yields: That our Prophet and Master Muhammad, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, is the Messenger of Allah, Mighty and Majestic. As for the minor premise: it is known by recurrent transmission (tawatur) which is reported by both supporter and opponent, and tawatur yields necessary knowledge as established in usul al-fiqh. As for the proof of the major premise: it has already preceded in the indication of the miracle. And know: that among the deniers of the prophethood of our Prophet and Master Muhammad, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, are the Jews, and they are two groups:
Source pages p. 576

Commentary
This passage is a structured proof (nazm al-dalil) for the prophethood of Muhammad (saw). It begins by stating that Muhammad's claim to prophethood is known with certainty (darura) and that he performed miracles beyond count. The formal proof is a categorical syllogism: Minor premise: Muhammad claimed prophethood and a miracle appeared confirming his claim, with no one able to oppose it. Major premise: Anyone who claims prophethood and has a confirming miracle that cannot be opposed is a true messenger of Allah. Conclusion: Therefore, Muhammad is a messenger of Allah. The minor premise is proven by tawatur (mass transmission) which gives certain knowledge. The major premise is taken from an earlier discussion on the implication of miracles. Finally, the author notes that among the rejectors are the Jews, who are divided into two groups (the text breaks off before describing them).

صغرى
كبرى
تواتر
معجزة
رسالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophethood cannot be proven logically; it is purely a matter of faith.
Correction: This syllogism shows that prophethood can be proven rationally using observable miracles and logical deduction.

Checkpoint
What does the major premise of the proof state?
A) Muhammad (saw) claimed prophethood and produced a miracle.
B) Anyone who claims prophethood and has a confirming miracle is a true messenger of Allah.
C) Tawatur is the basis for knowing the minor premise.
D) The Jews are divided into two groups.

Key Takeaway
The prophethood of Muhammad is logically proven by the conjunction of his claim, a miracle, and the inability of anyone to refute it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have now seen the logical proof for prophethood. But have you internalized it to the point that you could defend it under pressure? If not, what specific plan will you make this week to master this proof?
?
The Jews denied Muhammad's prophethood despite clear miracles. Is there any sign from Allah in your own life—a prayer answered, a situation saved—that you have conveniently overlooked or explained away? Will you acknowledge it fully today?
?
If someone you love doubts the prophethood of Muhammad, will you be able to present this syllogism with conviction? More importantly, does your own life reflect the belief that he is truly a messenger, or have you settled for mere intellectual assent?

Jewish Denial of Prophethood and Refutation of the Abrogation Argument
Identification and refutation of Jewish denial of prophethood via abrogation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main Jewish objections to Muhammad's prophethood, especially the argument that abrogation implies badā', and how Sanusi refutes it using a reductio ad absurdum and an analogy.

Core Idea
Abrogation shows God's wisdom, not ignorance, as seen in everyday divine actions.

This passage deals with the Jewish denial of Muhammad's prophethood based on the claim that his law abrogates parts of the Torah. Sanusi first identifies two Jewish sects and their arguments, then provides a powerful refutation of the main objection that abrogation entails badā' (a change in God's mind). By using logical reasoning and a simple analogy, he shows that changing rulings is consistent with perfect wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sects and their arguments
Read the passage to find the two Jewish sects described. Note the first sect's two arguments against abrogation: one based on badā' and one based on a transmitted claim from Moses.
The second sect al-ʿĪsawiyya accepts Muhammad as a prophet only for Arabs.
2
Understand the concept of badā'
Badā' is the idea that God changes His mind due to new information, which is impossible for an all-knowing God. The first sect argued that abrogation implies badā'.
Sanusi will refute this by clarifying what badā' really means.
3
Analyze Sanusi's refutation
Sanusi asks what they mean by badā'. If they mean God discovering hidden wisdom, he argues that abrogation does not entail that. He then uses a reductio: if legal changes implied badā', then God's physical changes (health, wealth, life) would also imply it, which they do not accept.
This is a powerful logical argument.
4
Appreciate the analogy of the wise doctor
Sanusi gives an analogy of a doctor who prescribes then forbids medicine as circumstances change. This shows that changing rulings does not imply ignorance but rather wisdom adapting to different situations.
This makes the abstract argument concrete.
5
Conclude the lesson
Reflect on how Sanusi's refutation defends the possibility of abrogation, thereby supporting the prophethood of Muhammad.

واعلم : أنَّ مِنَ المنكرينَ لنبوَّة نبينا ومولانا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلّم : اليهود ، وهم فرقتان : فرقة امتنعتْ مِنْ تصديقِهِ : لما تضمَّنَتُهُ شريعتُهُ مِنْ نَسْخِ بعضِ أحكام شريعة موسى صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ ، وزعموا أنَّ النسخ محال ، وتمسكوا في إحالته على أنَّ النسْخَ يستلزمُ البَداء ، وبعضُهم تمسَّكَ في إحالته على النقل فقالوا : إنَّ موسى عليه الصلاةُ والسلام نص على أنَّ شريعتَهُ لا تُنسَخُ ، وأَنَّهُ قالَ : تمسكوا بالسبتِ أبداً . الفرقةُ الثانيةُ : تُعرَفُ بالعيسويَّةِ ، قالوا : محمد عليه الصلاة والسلام رسول ، لكن إلى العرب خاصةً . والرد على مَنْ أَحالَ النسخ للبَداءِ أَنْ يُقال : ما تعني بالبَداءِ ؟ إِنْ عنيتَ أنَّ الله تعالى ظهرَ لهُ مِنَ الحكمة ما كان خافياً عليهِ عندَ شرع الحكم الأوَّلِ ولذلك نسخه .. فلا نُسلّمُ لزوم ذلكَ في النسخ ؛ فإِنَّهُ لَوِ استلزم تصرُّفُهُ في أفعالِ عبادِهِ ؛ بمنع ما أطلقه في وقت ، وإطلاقِ ما منعَهُ في وقتِ البَداء .. لاستلزمَهُ تصرُّفَهُ فيهم بأفعالِهِ ؛ مِنْ نقلهم مِنَ الصحة إلى المرضِ ، ومِنَ المرضِ إلى الصحة ، ومِنَ الغنى إلى الفقرِ ، ومِنَ الفقرِ إلى الغنى ، ومِنَ الحياةِ إلى الموتِ ، وإذا لم يدل الثاني فلا يدلُّ الأَوَّلُ ، كيفَ ومِنَ المعلوم أنه لا يمتنع في الحكمة أن يأمر الحكيم مريضاً باستعمال الدواء في وقتٍ ، ثم ينهاه عنه في وقتِ آخر لعلمِهِ بصلاحِهِ في الحالين ؟!
Know that among the deniers of the prophethood of our Prophet and our master Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace, are the Jews, and they are two sects: A sect that refrained from believing in him because his law includes the abrogation of some rulings of the law of Moses, peace be upon him, and they claimed that abrogation is impossible, and they clung in their claim of its impossibility to the fact that abrogation entails badā' (a change of divine purpose), and some of them clung in their claim of its impossibility to transmitted evidence, saying: Moses, peace be upon him, explicitly stated that his law is not abrogated, and that he said: 'Hold fast to the Sabbath forever.' The second sect is known as al-ʿĪsawiyya; they said: Muhammad, peace be upon him, is a messenger, but only to the Arabs specifically. The refutation of the one who claimed abrogation is impossible due to badā' is to say: What do you mean by badā'? If you mean that God Almighty became aware of a wisdom that was hidden from Him at the time of legislating the first ruling and therefore He abrogated it, then we do not concede the necessity of that in abrogation; because if His disposal in the actions of His servants (by preventing what He had permitted at a time, and permitting what He had prevented at another time) entailed badā', then His disposal in them through His actions (by transferring them from health to sickness, from sickness to health, from wealth to poverty, from poverty to wealth, from life to death) would also entail it. And if the second does not imply badā', then the first does not imply it either. How could it be otherwise, when it is known that it is not impossible in wisdom for a wise man to order a sick person to use medicine at one time, and then forbid him from it at another time, due to his knowledge of its benefit in both situations?
Source pages p. 576

Commentary
This passage identifies the two Jewish sects that deny the prophethood of Muhammad (صلى الله عليه وسلم). The first sect denies him because his law abrogates some parts of the Mosaic law, which they consider impossible. They give two arguments: (1) abrogation implies badā' (a change in God's mind, which is impossible), and (2) Moses explicitly stated that his law is eternal, citing the Sabbath commandment. The second sect (al-ʿĪsawiyya) accepts Muhammad's prophethood but restricts it to Arabs. The refutation focuses on the first sect's badā' argument. The author asks what they mean by badā'. If they mean that God learned something He previously did not know, then we do not accept that this follows from abrogation. The author uses a reductio ad absurdum: if abrogation of legal rulings entailed badā', then God's daily actions in the physical world (sickness, death, etc.) would also entail badā', which they would not accept. He then gives an analogy of a wise doctor who prescribes and then forbids medicine based on changing circumstances. This shows that changing a ruling does not imply ignorance; rather, it reflects wisdom adapted to different situations. The refutation is effective because it appeals to common sense and the consistent actions of a wise agent.

Key Terms

بداء
اليهود
العيسوية
تمسك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abrogation means God changed His mind because He was ignorant of the best ruling initially.
Correction: Abrogation reflects God's changing wisdom according to different circumstances, not ignorance, as seen in the analogy of the wise doctor.

Checkpoint
What is the first Jewish sect's main argument against Muhammad's prophethood?
A) He did not perform miracles.
B) His law abrogates some of Moses' laws, and abrogation is impossible.
C) He was sent only to Arabs.
D) He claimed divinity.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes the claim that abrogation implies divine ignorance by showing that God's actions in the physical world are similar to abrogation in law, yet they do not imply ignorance, thus abrogation is consistent with divine wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a change in your life that you don't understand—a sudden illness, loss, or blessing—do you react with trust in God's wisdom, or do you question His plan? How can this lesson's analogy of the doctor help you submit to His changing decrees?
?
This lesson shows that abrogation reflects God's wisdom, not ignorance. Is there an area of your life where you resist God's command because it seems inconsistent with what He once required? What would it take for you to accept His wisdom over your own understanding?
?
Sanusi's refutation demands that we examine the assumptions behind our objections. What objection to God's guidance do you hold that, once examined, might actually reflect a lack of trust in His perfect knowledge? How will you actively replace that doubt with reliance on His wisdom starting today?

The Universal Testimony of Miracles
Sanusi presents a logical dilemma to the Jews on the evidential force of miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's logical argument that if miracles prove the truth of one prophet, they must prove the truth of all prophets who performed miracles.

Core Idea
Miracles are universal proofs, not exclusive to any single prophet.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses Jewish interlocutors who accept Moses' miracles but reject Muhammad and Jesus. He presents a logical dilemma: either miracles indicate truthfulness or they do not. If they do, then the same standard applies to all prophets with miracles. This argument showcases the universal rational principle underlying prophetic proof.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Dilemma
Read the passage carefully and identify the two options Sanusi presents: either a miracle indicates the truth of the claimant or it does not.
Notice the logical structure: لا يخلو (is free from) introduces a dichotomy.
2
Apply to Moses
Consider the first option: if miracles do not indicate truth, then Moses' miracles would not prove his prophethood. This contradicts Jewish belief.
Sanusi uses this to show the absurdity of rejecting the indication.
3
Apply to Muhammad and Jesus
Now consider the second option: if miracles do indicate truth, then the same logic applies to Muhammad and Jesus, who also performed miracles.
The conclusion is necessary: وجب تصديق (must affirm).
4
Reflect on the Universal Principle
Ask yourself why this argument is powerful: it forces consistency. The evidential value of a miracle is not tied to a specific prophet but to the nature of the miracle itself.
This connects to broader debates about prophethood and rational proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول لليهودِ : وقوع الخارقِ على وَفْقِ دعوى المتحدّي معَ العجز عن معارضتهِ مَنْ تُحُدِّيَ عليهِ لا يخلو : إِمَّا أَنْ يدل على صدْقِ مُدَّعي الرسالة أو لا ، فإن لم يدلُّ لزم ألا تقوم دلالة على صدق موسى عليه السلامُ على زعمِكم ، وإِنْ دلَّ وجب تصديق محمد وعيسى عليهما السلام .
Then we say to the Jews: The occurrence of the miraculous (al-khariq) in accordance with the claim of the challenger, along with the inability to oppose it on the part of the one challenged, is free from [one of two possibilities]: either it indicates the truthfulness of the claimant of prophethood, or it does not. If it does not indicate, then it follows that no indication of the truthfulness of Moses (peace be upon him) exists according to your claim; and if it indicates, then the truthfulness of Muhammad and Jesus (peace be upon both of them) must be affirmed.
Source pages p. 578

Commentary
This passage is a direct argument addressed to the Jews. Sanusi uses a logical disjunction (لا يخلو) to force the Jewish interlocutor into a dilemma. The premise is that a miracle (al-khariq) — defined as a supernatural event that happens in accordance with the claim of someone challenging others to produce something similar, and that those challenged cannot match — has only two possible implications: either it proves the truthfulness of the one claiming prophethood, or it does not. If it does not prove truthfulness, then according to the Jews' own reasoning, the miracles of Moses would not prove his truthfulness, which they would deny. If it does prove truthfulness (as they must affirm for Moses), then the same logic applies to Muhammad and Jesus, who also performed miracles. Therefore, they must accept Muhammad and Jesus as true prophets.

Key Terms
خارق
معجزة
تحدي
تصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles are specific to Moses and cannot be used as evidence for other prophets.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the logical force of miracles is universal; if they prove Moses, they prove any prophet with miracles.

Checkpoint
What is the central premise of Sanusi's argument against the Jews regarding miracles?
A) Miracles are only for Moses
B) If a miracle indicates truth for one, it must for all
C) Jews reject all miracles
D) Miracles cannot be proven

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's dilemma shows that the evidential value of miracles is universal, forcing consistency in accepting all prophets who performed them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter clear evidence of truth—whether in religion or life—do you submit to it, or do you find ways to rationalize your resistance? What does that say about your heart's posture toward truth?
?
The Jews rejected Muhammad despite the logical force of Sanusi's argument. Where in your own life do you reject a truth because accepting it would require changing your habits, relationships, or worldview? Will you examine that area today and take one step toward surrender?
?
Sanusi's argument demands consistency. In what area of your faith or daily life are you inconsistent—believing one thing but living another? What concrete change can you make this week to align your actions with what you profess?

Evidence of Abrogation in Jewish Law
Sanusi lists five Torah examples proving abrogation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses examples from the Torah to demonstrate that abrogation (naskh) is a necessary feature of divine law, countering the claim that the Torah is immutable.

Core Idea
Abrogation is not a contradiction but gradual legislation, as even the Torah shows.

Sanusi now addresses a key objection from those who deny abrogation: that earlier scriptures are immutable. He turns to the Torah itself, the scripture of the Jews, and points to clear examples where laws changed over time. By citing these instances, he shows that abrogation is not an innovation but a consistent feature of prophetic teachings.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Notice how Sanusi begins with a claim that abrogation is necessary in Jewish law, then provides evidence from the Torah.
The passage is a proof by example.
2
Identify each example
List the five examples Sanusi gives. For each, note the original ruling and the later change. This will help you see the pattern of abrogation.
Examples include food, marriage, Sabbath, and circumcision.
3
Reflect on the refutation
Consider how these examples refute the claim that the Torah is immutable. Why does this strengthen the case for abrogation in Islam?
Abrogation is shown as a consistent divine practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النسخ : فهو لازمٌ في شرعهم أيضاً ؛ إذْ قد ثبتَ مِنْ نص التوراة أنَّ الله تعالى قد قالَ لنوحٍ حينَ خرجَ مِنَ السفينة : ( إِنِّي جاعل كل دابَّةٍ مأكلاً لكَ ولذريَّتِكَ ، وأطلقتُ ذلك لكم كسائر العشب ما خلا الدم ) ، وقد حُرِّمَ بعد ذلك في التوراة أشياء كثيرة ، وفي التوراة : أنَّ مِنْ شريعةِ آدمَ عليه السلام جواز نكاح الأختِ ، وقد حُرَّموا ذلكَ ، وقد كانَ مِنْ شرع يعقوبَ عليه السلام الجمع بين الأختينِ ، وقد حُرموه . وقد كانَ العمل في السبتِ قبلَ شريعة موسى عليه السلام مباحاً ثم حرَّمه موسى عليه السلام ، ولم يكن الختان واجباً لذوي الولادة ، وقد أوجبوه .
As for abrogation (al-naskh): it is necessary in their law as well; for it is established from the text of the Torah that Allah Most High said to Noah when he left the ark: 'I have made every creature a food for you and your descendants, and I have permitted that to you like the rest of the plants, except for blood.' And after that, many things were forbidden in the Torah. And in the Torah: that from the law of Adam (peace be upon him) was the permissibility of marrying a sister, and they forbade that. And from the law of Jacob (peace be upon him) was the combining of two sisters [in marriage], and they forbade it. And work on the Sabbath was permissible before the law of Moses (peace be upon him), then Moses forbade it. And circumcision was not obligatory for those born, and they made it obligatory.
Source pages p. 578

Commentary
This passage is a proof by example. Sanusi addresses the Jewish denial of abrogation (naskh) by showing that their own scriptures contain clear instances of changes in divine law. He lists five examples from the Torah: (1) After Noah, all animals were permitted as food except blood; later many foods were forbidden. (2) In Adam's law, marrying one's sister was permissible; later it was forbidden. (3) In Jacob's law, marrying two sisters simultaneously was permissible; later it was forbidden. (4) Work on the Sabbath was allowed before Moses, then Moses forbade it. (5) Circumcision was not obligatory at birth, but later became obligatory. These examples demonstrate that abrogation is not an innovation but a recognized feature of prophetic law, thus refuting the Jewish claim that the law of Moses is immutable.

Key Terms
نسخ
توراة
شرع
ختان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abrogation means God contradicts Himself or changes His mind arbitrarily.
Correction: Sanusi shows abrogation is gradual legislation suited to different times and contexts, not a contradiction.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Abrogation is affirmed even in the Torah, countering the claim that earlier laws are immutable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that Allah changed His laws for past nations, are you truly grateful that He has given you a complete and final law through Muhammad (ﷺ)? Or do you sometimes wish you could pick and choose from different laws to suit your desires? How can you actively cultivate trust in the Shari‘ah as perfect for your time and place?
?
The Torah records that work on the Sabbath was once allowed, then forbidden. Think of one command from Allah that you know is true but find difficult to follow. Will you commit today to obey it fully, trusting that His abrogation of previous laws was for wisdom, and His command to you now is also wise—even when it is hard?

Refuting the Claim That Moses' Law Is Unchangeable
Sanusi refutes the claim that Moses' law is unchangeable with two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that Moses explicitly stated his law cannot be abrogated is false, based on the appearance of miracles after Moses and the historical silence of the Jews.

Core Idea
Miracles after Moses prove his law could be abrogated; Jewish silence confirms the claim is fabricated.

Sanusi now tackles a specific objection raised by those who deny abrogation of previous laws: that Moses himself declared his law eternal. He traces this claim to a known heretic and then delivers two devastating arguments—from the appearance of miracles after Moses and from the historical record of Jewish opposition to Islam. This lesson unpacks those arguments and shows how they decisively refute the objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source of the claim
Sanusi names Ibn al-Rawandi as the origin of the claim. Note that he accuses him of teaching sophistry for worldly gain—this immediately discredits the transmission.
Ibn al-Rawandi was a controversial figure; his reliability is zero.
2
Follow the miracle argument
Read the argument: if Moses’ law were truly unchangeable, no genuine miracle would appear after him to confirm a new prophet. Since Jesus and Muhammad performed miracles, the claim must be false.
Sanusi also notes that no miracle appears after Muhammad, as he is the final prophet.
3
Examine the historical silence argument
If the Jews possessed such a statement, they would have used it when Muhammad called them to Islam. Instead, they resorted to altering scriptures and hiding his description—proving the statement never existed.
This is an argument from silence: the absence of evidence is evidence of absence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما دعوى أنَّ موسى عليهِ السلامُ نصَّ على أنَّ شريعتهُ لا تُنسَخُ .. فهذا ممَّا لقنه لهمُ ابنُ الرَّاوَندي (١) ، وقد كانَ يُعلَّمَ الفِرَقَ الشَّبَة طلباً للدنيا ، ولا يخفى كذبُ هذا النقل ؛ إذْ لو كان حقاً لَمَا ظَهرَتِ المعجزة على يد عيسى عليه السلام ، ولا على يد نبينا ومولانا محمد صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ ، كما لم تظهر ولا تظهر على يد أحدٍ بعد نبينا صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ ؛ إِذْ قالَ : « لَا نَبِيَّ بَعْدِي . وأيضاً : لو كانَ ذلكَ النقل حقاً لكان أولى الأزمنة بذكرِهِ والاحتجاج به الزمانُ الذي دعاهم به نبينا صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ إلى الإسلام ، وقد بالغوا حينئذ في إخفاء نورِهِ جهدَهم حتى غيَّرُوا صفتَهُ في كتبهم وفي غيرها ، ولم يحتج أحدٌ منهم بذلكَ معَ شَدَّةِ حرصهم عليه وتوفر الدواعي على نقله لو كان موجوداً حقاً
As for the claim that Moses (peace be upon him) explicitly stated that his law cannot be abrogated... this is something that Ibn al-Rawandi taught them, and he used to teach the sects sophistry (ash-shubah) seeking worldly gain. The falsity of this transmission is not hidden; for if it were true, no miracle would have appeared at the hand of Jesus (peace be upon him), nor at the hand of our Prophet and Master Muhammad (peace and blessings of Allah be upon him), just as no miracle appeared nor will appear at the hand of anyone after our Prophet (peace and blessings of Allah be upon him), for he said: 'There is no prophet after me.' Also: if that transmission were true, the most appropriate time to mention and argue with it would have been the time when our Prophet (peace and blessings of Allah be upon him) called them to Islam. And they exerted their utmost at that time to hide his light, even changing his description in their books and elsewhere, and not one of them argued with it despite their intense keenness for it and the abundant motives to transmit it if it had truly existed.
Source pages p. 579

Commentary
This passage refutes a specific claim: that Moses explicitly stated his law is unchangeable. Sanusi identifies the source of this claim as Ibn al-Rawandi, a known heretic who taught sophistry for worldly gain. Then he gives two arguments to prove the claim false. First, the argument from miracles: if Moses had declared his law unchangeable, no true miracle would appear after him, because a miracle would confirm a new prophet whose law would abrogate the previous one. But Jesus and Muhammad performed miracles, so the claim is false. Sanusi adds that after Muhammad no miracles appear because he is the final prophet. Second, the argument from historical silence: if such a statement existed in the Torah, the Jews would have used it as their strongest argument when Muhammad called them to Islam. Instead, they resorted to other arguments and even altered the descriptions of Muhammad in their scriptures. Their failure to use this alleged statement, despite their strong desire to refute him, proves it is a fabrication.

Key Terms
نسخ
ابن الراوندي
معجزة
شبهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Jewish scholars have a rational argument that their law cannot be abrogated based on Moses' own words.
Correction: The alleged statement is a fabrication by Ibn al-Rawandi; the miracle and historical evidence prove it false.

Checkpoint
What is Sanusi's first argument against the claim that Moses said his law cannot be abrogated?
A) The Torah does not mention it

C) The Jews never read the Torah
D) Ibn al-Rawandi was a Jew

Key Takeaway
The claim that Moses declared his law unchangeable is a fabrication; the appearance of miracles after Moses and the Jews' silence prove it false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Ibn al-Rawandi taught falsehood for worldly gain. Is there any area of your faith where you have accepted a comfortable lie because it benefits you—in your reputation, relationships, or desires? Are you willing to surrender that benefit for the truth, no matter the cost?

Refuting the Claim That Moses' Law Cannot Be Abrogated
Sanusi refutes the claim that Moses' law is abrogation-proof using miracles and finality of prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses examples from earlier prophets and historical reasoning to refute the claim that Moses' law cannot be abrogated.

Core Idea
Abrogation is a recurring pattern in divine laws; later prophets confirm its validity.

Sanusi confronts a challenge from those who claim that Moses' law cannot be abrogated, a claim he attributes to the heretic Ibn al-Rawandi. Using concrete examples from earlier prophets, he demonstrates that abrogation occurred even within the Mosaic tradition itself. Then he presents two powerful arguments: the appearance of miracles by later prophets and the historical silence of the Jews when confronted by Prophet Muhammad. This lesson unpacks these arguments and shows why the claim is baseless.

Guided Path
1
Identify examples of abrogation
Read the passage and note the three examples given: Jacob's allowance of marrying two sisters, Sabbath work before Moses, and circumcision not mandatory at birth. Each was later abrogated.
These examples show that abrogation is not new.
2
Understand the claim and its source
The claim that Moses explicitly stated his law cannot be abrogated is attributed to Ibn al-Rawandi, a known heretic. Sanusi dismisses it as false.
Ibn al-Rawandi is a controversial figure in Islamic heresiography.
3
Analyze the first proof: miracles of later prophets
If the claim were true, miracles would not have appeared on later prophets like Jesus and Muhammad, as they would indicate abrogation. The Prophet's statement 'There is no prophet after me' confirms finality.
Miracles are signs of prophethood, and later prophets' miracles indicate abrogation of previous laws.
4
Analyze the second proof: historical silence of the Jews
When Prophet Muhammad invited Jews to Islam, they had the strongest motive to use any such statement from Moses to reject him. Yet they never did, showing the claim is baseless.
This argument uses absence of evidence under strong motive as proof of non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد كانَ مِنْ شرع يعقوبَ عليه السلام الجمع بين الأختينِ ، وقد حُرموه . وقد كانَ العمل في السبتِ قبلَ شريعة موسى عليه السلام مباحاً ثم حرَّمه موسى عليه السلام ، ولم يكن الختان واجباً لذوي الولادة ، وقد أوجبوه . وأما دعوى أنَّ موسى عليهِ السلامُ نصَّ على أنَّ شريعتهُ لا تُنسَخُ .. فهذا ممَّا لقنه لهمُ ابنُ الرَّاوَندي (١) ، وقد كانَ يُعلَّمَ الفِرَقَ الشَّبَه طلباً للدنيا ، ولا يخفى كذبُ هذا النقل ؛ إذْ لو كان حقاً لَمَا ظَهرَتِ المعجزة على يد عيسى عليه السلام ، ولا على يد نبينا ومولانا محمد صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ ، كما لم تظهر ولا تظهر على يد أحدٍ بعد نبينا صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ ؛ إِذْ قالَ : « لَا نَبِيَّ بَعْدِي (۲) . وأيضاً : لو كانَ ذلكَ النقل حقاً لكان أولى الأزمنة بذكرِهِ والاحتجاج به الزمانُ الذي دعاهم به نبينا صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ إلى الإسلام ، وقد بالغوا حينئذ في إخفاء نورِهِ جهدَهم حتى غيَّرُوا صفتَهُ في كتبهم وفي غيرها ، ولم يحتج أحدٌ منهم بذلكَ معَ شَدَّةِ حرصهم عليه وتوفر الدواعي على نقله لو كان موجوداً حقاً
And it was from the law of Jacob, peace be upon him, the marriage of two sisters together, and they have forbidden it. And work on the Sabbath was permissible before the law of Moses, peace be upon him, then Moses, peace be upon him, forbade it. And circumcision was not obligatory for those born, and they made it obligatory. As for the claim that Moses, peace be upon him, explicitly stated that his law cannot be abrogated... this is something that Ibn al-Rawandi taught them, and he used to teach the sects falsehood seeking worldly gain. The falsity of this transmission is not hidden; for if it were true, the miracle would not have appeared at the hand of Jesus, peace be upon him, nor at the hand of our Prophet and Master Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace, just as no miracle has appeared nor will appear at the hand of anyone after our Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, when he said: 'There is no prophet after me.' Also: if that transmission were true, the most appropriate time to mention it and argue by it would have been the time when our Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, called them to Islam. And they then exerted their utmost to conceal his light, even changing his description in their books and elsewhere, and none of them argued with that, despite their strong desire for it and the abundant motivations to transmit it if it had truly existed.
Source pages p. 579

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim, attributed to Ibn al-Rawandi, that Moses explicitly stated his law cannot be abrogated. The author begins by noting that previous prophets' laws included practices later abrogated: Jacob allowed marrying two sisters, Moses later forbade it; Sabbath work was permissible before Moses, then forbidden; circumcision was not obligatory at birth, then made obligatory. These examples show that abrogation is a known phenomenon in divine laws. Then the author addresses the specific claim: Ibn al-Rawandi taught this falsehood to the sects for worldly gain. The refutation proceeds with two arguments. First, if the claim were true, miracles would not have appeared at the hands of Jesus and Muhammad, since later prophets confirm abrogation of previous laws, and the Prophet Muhammad declared finality with 'There is no prophet after me.' Second, historically, when Prophet Muhammad invited the Jews to Islam, they were most desperate to reject him, yet they never used this alleged statement of Moses. Instead, they changed his description in their scriptures, indicating that such a statement never existed. This second argument uses absence of evidence under strong motive to disprove the claim.

Key Terms
نسخ (abrogation)
ابن الراوندي (Ibn al-Rawandi)
معجزة (miracle)
لا نبي بعدي (finality of prophethood)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Moses' law cannot be abrogated because it is final and unchangeable.
Correction: Sanusi shows that abrogation occurred even within the Mosaic tradition, and later prophets' miracles confirm that abrogation is possible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which practice was permissible before Moses but later forbidden in his law?
A) Marriage of two sisters
B) Work on the Sabbath
C) Circumcision at birth
D) Prayer in congregation

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes the claim that Moses' law cannot be abrogated by showing prior abrogations and using the evidence of later prophets' miracles and the Jews' historical silence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that previous prophets' laws were abrogated for a greater divine wisdom, how does that challenge your attachment to any religious practice that you might hold as unchangeable? Are you willing to submit to what Allah has commanded today, even if it contradicts your previous assumptions?
?
The Jews hid the truth and changed their scriptures to avoid accepting Muhammad. In what ways do you sometimes suppress or distort the truth in your own life to avoid acknowledging a call to change? What would it take for you to honestly confront such a claim?
?
Sanusi says the Jews were silent when challenged because they had no evidence. Have you ever been silent when you knew the truth was inconvenient? How can you cultivate the courage to speak truth even when it goes against your desires?

The Contradiction of the 'Isawiyya Sect
Sanusi points out the logical inconsistency of the 'Isawiyya who affirm prophethood but deny universal mission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why affirming a prophet's truthfulness logically requires accepting his entire message, using the 'Isawiyya as a case study.

Core Idea
Affirming prophethood requires affirming the full message.

In this lesson, we examine a logical refutation of the 'Isawiyya sect, who accepted the prophethood of Muhammad but denied his universal mission. Sanusi demonstrates that such a position is self-contradictory, as acceptance of a prophet's truthfulness compels acceptance of everything he conveys. This short passage illustrates a key principle in Islamic theology: consistency in belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation slowly. Notice the logical structure: premise, implication, conclusion.
The ellipsis in the Arabic indicates a pause for effect.
2
Identify the 'Isawiyya's position
What do they affirm (prophethood, truthfulness) and what do they deny (universal mission)? Write it down.
3
Trace the logical argument
Follow the reasoning: if they accept his truthfulness, they must accept all his reports; he reported universal mission; therefore they must accept it. Their denial creates a contradiction.
This is a classic conditional proof.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Why does Sanusi say the contradiction is obvious to any rational person? Think about the principle of consistency.
Belief systems must be internally coherent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العيسوية : فإذا سلَّموا صدقه عليه الصلاة والسلام .. لزمهم تصديقه في جميع ما أخبر بهِ ، وقد أخبر أَنَّهُ رسولٌ إلى الكافَّةِ ، وأَنَّهُ مبعوث إلى الأحمر والأسود ؛ فإقرارهم بنبوتِهِ ، ثم تكذيبهُ في أَنَّهُ رسول لجميع أهل الأرضِ . . لا يخفى تناقضه لكل عاقل .
As for the 'Isawiyya: if they acknowledge his truthfulness, peace and blessings be upon him, then they must affirm him in everything he has informed. And he has informed that he is a messenger to all, and that he is sent to the red and the black. So their affirmation of his prophethood, then their denial that he is a messenger to all the people of the earth... its contradiction is not hidden to any rational person.
Source pages p. 579

Commentary
This passage presents a concise logical refutation of the 'Isawiyya sect. The 'Isawiyya are a group that accepts Jesus but also acknowledges the prophethood of Muhammad while rejecting his universal mission. The author argues that if they accept the truthfulness of the Prophet (i.e., that he is truthful in his message), then they must accept all that he has informed. And he has informed that he is a messenger to all of mankind—'to the red and the black' meaning all races and nations. Therefore, their position is contradictory: they affirm his prophethood yet deny part of his message, namely his universal mission. This inconsistency is obvious to any rational person. The passage thus demonstrates that acceptance of a prophet's truthfulness entails acceptance of his entire message; selective acceptance is inherently contradictory.

Key Terms
عيسوية (Isawiyya)
تصديق (affirmation of truthfulness)
رسالة عامة (universal mission)
تناقض (contradiction)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can accept a prophet's prophethood while rejecting part of his message.
Correction: Accepting a prophet's truthfulness logically requires accepting everything he conveys, including his universal mission.

Checkpoint
What logical flaw does the author identify in the 'Isawiyya's belief?
A) They accept Jesus but deny Muhammad
B) They affirm Muhammad's truthfulness but deny his universal mission
C) They believe in abrogation
D) They reject the miracles of the Qur'an

Key Takeaway
If a person accepts a prophet's truthfulness, they must accept his entire message; denying part of it reveals a logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Prophet's command to submit fully to Allah, is there any area of your life where you resist, believing it's 'too much' or 'unnecessary'? How is that any different from the 'Isawiyya's selective acceptance?
?
The 'Isawiyya's contradiction was obvious 'to any rational person.' What contradictions might be obvious to others in your own practice of faith? Will you sincerely ask Allah to show you one such blind spot today and act on it?
?
If you truly believe that Muhammad is Allah's messenger, what would it mean for you to accept every single thing he informed—even those that challenge your comfort, culture, or desires? What will you change starting now?

The Arabs' Silencing and the Two Explanations
Sanusi explains the silencing of the Arab eloquence masters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main explanations for why the Arabs were silenced before the Quran's eloquence and why the capacity explanation is preferred.

Core Idea
The Arabs knew the Quran was divine, not just eloquent.

Sanusi begins by addressing an imagined critic who questions the Arabs' failure to contest the Quran. He argues that the Arabs, masters of eloquence, sensed that the Quran was beyond human power. This lesson unpacks the two theories—divine intervention vs. human incapacity—and the example of Musaylima's failed imitation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and translation carefully. Notice the rhetorical question at the start and the shift to explanation.
The passage uses a question-answer format.
2
Identify the two opinions
Find the two reasons given for the Arabs' inability to imitate: beyond their capacity (al-aṣaḥḥ) or al-ṣarfa (divine turning away).
Sanusi explicitly says 'which is the more correct' for the first.
3
Reflect on the Musaylima example
Consider why Sanusi brings up Musaylima: even the most brazen imitator produced only a laughable farce.
This confirms the objective nature of the miracle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عارض يقدح في مناصبهم وإنْ كانَ في ذلك حتفُ أنفسهم فكيف بما هو مِنْ نوع البلاغةِ التي هي كلامهم وتَدِبُّ فيهم دبيباً ، حتى إنَّهم بها في كلِّ وادٍ يهيمونَ ؟! لكنَّ القومَ أخرسَهم أنَّهم أحسُّوا أنَّ الأمر إلهي لا تمكن مقاومتُهُ ؛ إمَّا لأنَّهُ ليس في طوقهم وهو الأصح ، أو للصَّرْفة ، وهما قولان ومَنْ لم يستحْيِ منهم ، وانْتَدَبَ لمقاومة هذا الأمر الإلهي ؛ كمُسَيْلِمَةَ افْتَضَحَ ، وأتى بمَخرَقةٍ يُتضاحَكُ منها إلى قيام الساعة ولو أنَّهم نُقِلَ إليهمُ القرآنُ نقل غيرِهِ مِنَ الكلام نقل آحاد .. لأمكن الاعتذارُ عنهم بعدم الوصول ، كلا بل امتلاتْ بحَمَلَتِهِ وصُحُفِهِ وإشادة أمرِهِ الأَرضُ كلُّها ؛ سهلها وجبلها، بدوها وحضرها ، برُّها وبحرها، مؤمنُها وكافرها ، جِنُّها وإنسُها ، وتطاولَتْ أزمنته على تلك الصفة قريباً مِنْ تسع مئة سنةٍ ، أفيستريب عاقل بعد هذا في كونه مِنْ  ِعندِ اللهِ جَلَّ وعلا صدَّقَ بهِ نبيَّه صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ ؟! هذا معَ ما فيهِ مِنَ الإخبار قبل الوقوع بالغيوب المطابقة ، ومحاسنِ علوم الشريعة المشتملة على ما لا يَقدِرُ البشر على ضبطِهِ ؛ مِنَ المصالح الدنيوية والأخروية ، وتحرير الأدلَّةِ ، والرد على المخالفين بالبراهين القطعية وسردِ قصص الماضين ، وتزكية النفس بمواعظ يغرقُ في أدنى بحارها جميع وعظ الواعظين . هذا كله على يد نبي أُمّى لم يلحظ قط كتاباً ، ولا حصلت قطُّ له مخالطة لذي علم ما يمكنُ بها تحصيل أدنى شيءٍ مِنْ ذلك عُلِمَ ذلكَ كلُّهُ بالضرورة ، ﴿ وَمَا كُنتَ تَتْلُوا مِن قَبْلِهِ مِن كِتَبٍ وَلَا تَخُطُّهُ بِيَمِينِكَ إِذًا لَّارْتَابَ الْمُبْطِلُونَ ﴾ [العنكبوت : ٤٨]
An objector who disparages their positions, even if in that is their own destruction, then how about what is of the type of eloquence which is their speech and penetrates among them insidiously, so that through it they wander in every valley?! But the people were silenced by the fact that they sensed that the matter is divine, impossible to resist; either because it is beyond their capacity—which is the more correct—or because of al-ṣarfa (the turning away), and these are two opinions. And whoever among them had no shame and embarked on resisting this divine command—like Musaylima—was disgraced and produced a farce to be laughed at until the Day of Resurrection. And if the Qur'an had been transmitted to them with the transmission of other speech, i.e., isolated transmission (naql āḥād), then an excuse for them on account of non-receipt would have been possible—no, rather the earth in its entirety was filled with its bearers, its manuscripts, and the proclamation of its affair: its plains and its mountains, its nomads and its settled peoples, its land and its sea, its believer and its disbeliever, its jinn and its humans—and its times extended in that manner for nearly nine hundred years. Is any rational person dubious after this about it being from Allah, mighty and exalted, and that His prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, testified to it? This is along with what it contains of informing of unseen matters before their occurrence, corresponding to reality, and the beauties of the sciences of the Sharia, which include what human beings cannot encompass: of worldly and otherworldly benefits, refinement of proofs, refutation of opponents with definitive evidences, narration of stories of the ancients, and purification of the soul with admonitions in the least of whose seas all the admonition of admonishers drowns. All of this was at the hand of an unlettered prophet who never once looked at a book, nor did he ever have any association with a possessor of knowledge by which even the least of that could be acquired. All of that is known necessarily, {And you did not recite before it any book, nor did you inscribe it with your right hand; otherwise the falsifiers would have doubted} [al-ʿAnkabūt: 48].
Source pages p. 581

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument for the miraculous nature of the Qur'an and its divine origin. Sanusi begins by addressing an imagined objector who would disparage the Arabs' inability to match the Qur'an. He notes that the Arabs were silenced because they sensed that the matter was divine and beyond their capacity (the more correct view) or because of the 'ṣarfa' (a theory that God turned them away from imitating it). He then cites the example of Musaylima, who tried to imitate it and became a laughingstock. Next, he argues that even if the Qur'an had been transmitted via isolated chains (āḥād), the Arabs could have claimed it hadn't reached them, but in fact it was transmitted massively (mutawātir), filling the entire earth for nearly nine hundred years. He then asks rhetorically whether any rational person could doubt its divine origin. He further points to its content: prophecies of the unseen, the comprehensive benefits of its laws, definitive proofs, refutation of opponents, and profound moral admonitions that surpass all human preaching. Finally, he highlights that all this came through an unlettered prophet who never studied under any scholar—yet everyone knows this necessarily (bi l-ḍarūra). He concludes with the Quranic verse asserting that the prophet never read or wrote any book, removing any suspicion.

Key Terms
إعجاز
صَرْفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Quran's inimitability is merely a matter of subjective aesthetic taste.
Correction: Sanusi shows it is an objective, rational miracle because the Arabs themselves, masters of eloquence, acknowledged their inability.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the more correct explanation for the Arabs' inability to imitate the Quran?
A) They were forbidden by God (al-ṣarfa).
B) It was beyond their capacity.
C) They feared the prophet's authority.
D) They lacked eloquence.

Key Takeaway
The Arabs were silenced not by lack of skill but by the clear divine origin of the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Arabs, masters of eloquence, were silenced by the Quran. When you read the Quran, are you silenced by its divine weight, or do you approach it as just another book? What would change in your life if you truly sensed that every word is from God, impossible to resist? Will you make a habit of pausing after each verse to acknowledge its divine origin?
?
Musaylima’s attempt to produce a rival was a farce. Are you, in any way, treating the Quran as if you can add to it or replace its guidance with your own preferences? Examine your daily choices: where are you subtly resisting submission to its commands? Will you repent and surrender to the One whose word cannot be matched?
?
The most eloquent Arabs were incapable of producing speech like the Quran. Do you ever rely on your own wisdom or skills instead of humbly submitting to the Quran’s guidance? In what area of your life are you still trying to 'compete' with Allah’s word instead of simply obeying? What step will you take today to silence your pride before the Quran?

Mass Transmission and Enduring Presence
Sanusi proves the Quran's divine origin via tawātur and longevity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran's widespread transmission and long duration serve as a rational proof of its divine origin.

Core Idea
The Quran's universal and continuous transmission leaves no room for doubt.

Sanusi now turns to the transmission of the Quran. He argues that even if it had come through isolated chains, the Arabs could have claimed ignorance, but in fact it was transmitted en masse, filling the earth for nearly 900 years. This lesson examines how the manner and duration of transmission preclude any rational doubt.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the contrast between hypothetical (āḥād) and reality (tawātur).
The passage shifts with 'kalla' (no).
2
Analyze the argument structure
Identify the two parts: a hypothetical scenario (if transmitted via āḥād, an excuse would be possible) and the actual scenario (tawātur, no excuse).
This is a conditional argument.
3
Consider the 'nine hundred years'
Note that Sanusi mentions nearly nine centuries—a striking duration that strengthens the transmission's credibility.
This appeals to the test of time.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical question
The passage ends with a rhetorical question: 'Is any rational person dubious?' This is a clinching point.
Sanusi uses this to assert certainty.

Is any rational person dubious after this about it being from Allah, mighty and exalted, and that His prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, testified to it? This is along with what it contains of informing of unseen matters before their occurrence, corresponding to reality, and the beauties of the sciences of the Sharia, which include what human beings cannot encompass: of worldly and otherworldly benefits, refinement of proofs, refutation of opponents with definitive evidences, narration of stories of the ancients, and purification of the soul with admonitions in the least of whose seas all the admonition of admonishers drowns. All of this was at the hand of an unlettered prophet who never once looked at a book, nor did he ever have any association with a possessor of knowledge by which even the least of that could be acquired. All of that is known necessarily, {And you did not recite before it any book, nor did you inscribe it with your right hand; otherwise the falsifiers would have doubted} [al-ʿAnkabūt: 48].
Source pages p. 581

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument for the miraculous nature of the Qur'an and its divine origin. Sanusi begins by addressing an imagined objector who would disparage the Arabs' inability to match the Qur'an. He notes that the Arabs were silenced because they sensed that the matter was divine and beyond their capacity (the more correct view) or because of the 'ṣarfa' (a theory that God turned them away from imitating it). He then cites the example of Musaylima, who tried to imitate it and became a laughingstock. Next, he argues that even if the Qur'an had been transmitted via isolated chains (āḥād), the Arabs could have claimed it hadn't reached them, but in fact it was transmitted massively (mutawātir), filling the entire earth for nearly nine hundred years. He then asks rhetorically whether any rational person could doubt its divine origin. He further points to its content: prophecies of the unseen, the comprehensive benefits of its laws, definitive proofs, refutation of opponents, and profound moral admonitions that surpass all human preaching. Finally, he highlights that all this came through an unlettered prophet who never studied under any scholar—yet everyone knows this necessarily (bi l-ḍarūra). He concludes with the Quranic verse asserting that the prophet never read or wrote any book, removing any suspicion.

Key Terms
نقل آحاد
ضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What would have been a possible excuse for the disbelievers if the Quran had been transmitted via isolated chains?
A) They could have claimed it was not eloquent.
B) They could have claimed it never reached them.
C) They could have said it was poetry.
D) They could have said it was written by a human.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's universal and continuous transmission over centuries is a rational proof of its divine origin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the Quran recited by someone who does not believe in it, do you sense the weight of this passage's claim that it fills the earth – and does that move you to honour it more deeply?
?
If the Quran's transmission is so undeniable that even jinn and disbelievers spread it, what is really stopping you from letting it transform your life right now?
?
The Quran has been preserved for over 1400 years. Will you be among those who merely pass it on, or will you be among those who live by it? What step will you take today to ensure the latter?

Internal Evidence and the Unlettered Prophet
Sanusi presents the Quran's content as internal proof and the prophet's illiteracy as final seal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran's content—prophecies, comprehensive teachings, and the prophet's illiteracy—combined form a decisive proof of its divine origin.

Core Idea
No human, especially an unlettered one, could produce the Quran's content without divine aid.

Sanusi now turns to the internal evidence of the Quran itself: accurate prophecies, all-encompassing laws, definitive arguments, and profound moral lessons. Then he highlights the stunning fact that this came through an unlettered prophet who never studied with scholars. This lesson shows how these factors together make a compelling case for the Quran being from Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the list of internal features and the climactic conclusion about the unlettered prophet.
The passage builds up to a verse from Surah al-Ankabut.
2
Identify the internal evidences
List the elements: prophecies of the unseen, comprehensive sharia sciences, worldly and otherworldly benefits, refined proofs, refutation of opponents, stories of the past, and soul-purifying admonitions.
Each is introduced by a waw (and).
3
Consider the unlettered prophet
Reflect on why Sanusi emphasizes that the prophet never read a book or sat with a scholar. This removes any human authorship possibility.
This is a final, decisive point.
4
Connect to the Qur'anic verse
Read the verse from al-Ankabut: it confirms the prophet's illiteracy, making the argument even stronger.
Sanusi says 'all of that is known necessarily' (ḍarūra).

An objector who disparages their positions, even if in that is their own destruction, then how about what is of the type of eloquence which is their speech and penetrates among them insidiously, so that through it they wander in every valley?! All of that is known necessarily, {And you did not recite before it any book, nor did you inscribe it with your right hand; otherwise the falsifiers would have doubted} [al-ʿAnkabūt: 48].

The Best Opinion on I'jaz: Eloquence and Special Style
Sanusi presents the chosen view on i'jaz combining eloquence and special style.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi argues that the Quran's inimitability lies in the inseparable combination of grand eloquence and a unique stylistic form.

Core Idea
The Quran's inimitability is the union of eloquence and a unique style, not either alone.

Sanusi has surveyed several opinions on the miraculous nature of the Quran. Now he presents the view he considers strongest—the one championed by two great scholars, al-Qadi al-Baqillani and Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni. He argues that the Quran’s challenge to produce a surah like it involves two equally essential elements: lofty eloquence and a unique stylistic form. This lesson unpacks that argument through a vivid analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get its flow, then read the translation. Notice the argument structure: statement, reason, analogy, example.
The passage is from Sanusi's commentary on i'jaz.
2
Identify the two components of i'jaz
The passage says the Qur'an comprises 'al-jazala' (grandeur/eloquence) and 'al-uslub al-khas' (special style). These are the two inseparable elements.
Jazala refers to high eloquence; uslub khas is the unique structural and stylistic pattern.
3
Understand the poet/orator analogy
Sanusi gives an analogy: if a brilliant poet challenges others to match his poem, neither an eloquent sermon nor a metered poem lacking eloquence would count as a proper contestation. Both elements must be present together.
This illustrates that the Qur'an's challenge requires both aspects simultaneously.
4
Connect to the example of Musaylimah
Musaylimah's attempts to imitate the Qur'an were laughable because they lacked both eloquence and the special style. This reinforces that mere mimicry fails.
Musaylimah's 'revelations' are classic examples of failed i'jaz.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this opinion is considered the best: it explains why the Qur'an cannot be imitated by any human effort, as it combines perfect eloquence with a unique divine style.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأحسنُ هذه الأقوالِ : القولُ الذي اختاره القاضي وإمامُ الحرمين ؛ فإنَّهُ عليهِ الصلاةُ والسلام تحدَّى بسورة مِنَ القرآن ، وهي مشتملة على الأمرين معاً ؛ الجزالة والأسلوب الخاص ، وإِنَّما يتحقَّقُ الإتيان بمثلها عندَ الإتيان بالمشتمل على الوجهين معاً ، فإنَّ الشاعر المُفْلِقَ إذا سرد قصيدةً بليغةً ، ودعا إلى المعارضة بمثلها ، فعُورِضَ بخُطبة بليغةٍ أو نثر مرسل بالغ أقصى الفصاحة.. لم يكن الآتي بذلك معارضاً لها ، ولو أتى شاعر بمثل وزنِ شعره عارياً عن فصاحتِهِ وجزالته . . لم يكن معارضاً له (۲) ، وهو نظير معارضةِ مُسَيْلِمَةَ الكَذَّابِ له بتُرَّهَاتِهِ التي يُتضاحَكُ منها
And the best of these sayings is the saying chosen by al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn; for indeed he (peace and blessings be upon him) challenged with a surah of the Qur'an, and it comprises both matters together: grandeur and the special style. And indeed the bringing of something like it is only realized when one brings something comprising both aspects together. For indeed the brilliant poet, when he recites an eloquent poem and calls for contestation with its like, and then a person contests with an eloquent sermon or a flowing prose reaching the utmost eloquence, that person is not considered a contestant for it. And if a poet brought something like its meter, bare of its eloquence and grandeur, he would not be considered a contestant for it. And it is analogous to the contestation of Musaylimah the Liar with his absurdities that are laughed at.
Source pages p. 584

Commentary
This passage presents and defends the chosen opinion on the miraculous nature (i'jaz) of the Qur'an. The author begins by stating that the best opinion is that of al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani and Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni. The key claim is that the Qur'an's challenge (tahaddi) was with a surah that combines two inseparable elements: al-jazala (grandeur/eloquence) and al-uslub al-khas (special style). The author illustrates with an analogy: if a poet challenges others to match his poem, an eloquent sermon or prose cannot count as a contestation because it lacks the poetic style. Conversely, matching only the meter without the eloquence also fails. The point is that both aspects must be present together. He then cites the example of Musaylimah the Liar, whose attempted imitations were laughable nonsense, showing that neither element alone suffices. The author uses the analogy to reinforce that the Qur'an's unique combination is what makes it inimitable.

Key Terms
الجزالة
الأسلوب الخاص
المعارضة
المُفْلِق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that i'jaz is solely based on eloquence or solely on a unique style.
Correction: Sanusi argues that it is the combination of grand eloquence and a special style that makes the Qur'an inimitable.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the best opinion on the miraculous nature (i'jaz) of the Qur'an?
A) It is the oldness of the Qur'an
B) It is the absence of contradiction
C) It is the combination of eloquence and special style
D) It is the prophecies about the unseen

Key Takeaway
The Quran's inimitability cannot be reduced to a single feature; it is the unique fusion of perfect eloquence with a distinctive style that makes it impossible to imitate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Refutation of the Sarfa View of I'jaz
Sanusi refutes the sarfa view of i'jaz with two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the sarfa view of the Qur'an's miraculousness is refuted by historical and logical arguments.

Core Idea
The sarfa view is self-defeating: it contradicts history and the Qur'an's actual eloquence.

Sanusi presents a crisp refutation of the sarfa theory, which claims the Qur'an's inimitability comes from God preventing the Arabs from matching it. This lesson unpacks two arguments that show why this theory fails. The first is historical: if God had prevented them, why are there no reports? The second is logical: the theory would require the Qur'an to be low in eloquence, yet it is unanimously acknowledged as the highest.

Guided Path
1
Understand the sarfa claim
Read the passage and identify what the sarfa view asserts. Note the key term الصَّرْفِة.
Sarfa means divine prevention.
2
Examine the historical argument
Notice how Sanusi argues that if sarfa had occurred, there would be transmitted reports of the Arabs' inability. The absence of such reports proves it didn't happen.
The motives (الدواعي) to transmit such a notable event would be abundant.
3
Examine the logical argument
See how the sarfa theory implies the Qur'an should be low in eloquence for the prevention to be evident. But the Qur'an is at the highest rank of eloquence.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical question
Consider why Sanusi ends with 'how so?' to emphasize the consensus on high eloquence and the inconsistency of the sarfa view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا مَنْ ذهب إلى أنَّ إعجازَهُ بالصَّرْفِةِ : فقد سبق التنبيه على ضَعفِهِ ؛ فإنَّهُ لو كانَ لَنُقِلَ شيء عنهم مِنْ ذلك فيما مضى ، ولو نُقِلَ لوُجِدَ ؛ فإِنَّهُ ممَّا تتوفَّرُ الدواعي على نقلِهِ ، وأيضاً : فلو كانَ إعجازه بالصَّرْفِةِ لكانَ كونه في أدنى مراتب الفصاحة أنسب ؛ لظهور إعجازه ، كيف ولا خلاف أنَّهُ في أعلى مراتبِ البلاغةِ ؟!
As for the one who holds that its miraculousness is by way of al-sarfa (divine prevention), then indeed the warning has preceded about its weakness. For if it were so, something would have been transmitted from them regarding that in the past, and if it were transmitted it would be found, for the motives to transmit it would be abundant. And also: if its miraculousness were by al-sarfa, then its being at the lowest ranks of eloquence would be more appropriate, for the manifestation of its miraculousness. How so, while there is no disagreement that it is at the highest ranks of eloquence?!
Source pages p. 584

Commentary
This passage refutes the view that the Qur'an's miraculousness is due to al-sarfa – the doctrine that God prevented the Arabs from matching it, even though they were capable. The author gives two arguments. First, historical: if God had prevented them, there would be reports (naql) from the Arabs describing their inability and frustration, but no such reports exist. Given the widespread motivation to transmit such a notable event, its absence proves it did not happen. Second, logical: if i'jaz were by sarfa, then the Qur'an being at the lowest level of eloquence would make the prevention more evident. But the Qur'an is unanimously recognized as being at the highest level of eloquence. Thus, the sarfa view is inconsistent with the actual high eloquence. The author concludes with a rhetorical question emphasizing the consensus on the Qur'an's supreme eloquence.

Key Terms
الصَّرْفِة
التنبيه
الدواعي
الفصاحة
البلاغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sarfa view is a plausible explanation for the Qur'an's inimitability.
Correction: Sanusi shows that if sarfa had occurred, there would be historical reports and the Qur'an would need to be low in eloquence, neither of which holds.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the sarfa (divine prevention) view of i'jaz weak?
A) Because it contradicts the consensus on the Qur'an's high eloquence and lacks historical reports of prevention
B) Because it was rejected by the majority of scholars
C) Because the Qur'an is not that eloquent
D) Because the Arabs were not challenged

Key Takeaway
The sarfa view fails because it lacks historical evidence and contradicts the Qur'an's known high eloquence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi argues that if sarfa were true, historical reports of the Arabs' inability would have been transmitted. Think of a time you believed something because it was convenient or comfortable, but lacked evidence. How does this remind you to ground your faith in what is transmitted and verified, rather than personal speculation?
?
The sarfa view implies that the Qur'an's eloquence is irrelevant to its miraculousness. When you recite or hear the Qur'an, do you ever rush through its words without pondering its unmatched beauty? How can you cultivate a deeper awe for its intrinsic eloquence as the true miracle?
?
Sanusi's second argument shows that the Qur'an's high eloquence contradicts the sarfa hypothesis. If someone suggested that the Qur'an is only miraculous because God made it so, not because of its own quality, how would you respond? Does this lesson equip you to defend the Qur'an's excellence with confidence?

Internal Coherence: Evidence, Not the Challenge
Sanusi acknowledges coherence as evidence but denies it was the challenge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why internal coherence of the Qur'an is considered a proof of its divine origin, yet it was not the basis of the challenge to produce a single surah.

Core Idea
Coherence proves divinity, but the challenge was about a single surah.

Some argued that the Qur'an's miraculousness lies in its internal harmony—the absence of contradiction across its length. Imam Sanusi agrees this is a powerful sign, but he distinguishes it from the specific challenge issued to the Arabs. In this passage, he clarifies that the challenge was not based on a property that only appears in the whole book.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Note the structure: Sanusi first presents the opposing view, then gives his concession, and finally his qualification.
2
Identify the concession and the qualification
Notice that Sanusi affirms the opponent's point as a great evidence, but then restricts it by saying the challenge was not made with that. Understand the difference between an evidence of truth and the specific challenge.
3
Reflect on why the distinction matters
Consider why the challenge had to be something that can be demonstrated in a single surah. This shows the precision of the Qur'anic challenge.
This connects to the broader discussion of i'jaz.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأَمَّا مَنْ قالَ : إعجازه في جملتِهِ ؛ بعدم التناقض فيه على طولِهِ وتصديق بعضه بعضاً : فلا ننكرُ أنَّ ما ذكرَهُ مِنْ أعظمِ دليل على أَنَّهُ مِنْ لدن حكيم عليم ، ولذا وصفَهُ تعالى بأنَّهُ : لَا يَأْتِيهِ الْبَاطِلُ مِنْ بَيْنِ يَدَيْهِ وَلَا مِنْ خَلْفِهِ ﴾ [فصلت : ٤٢]، إلا أنَّ التحدي لم يقع بذلكَ
As for the one who says: its miraculousness lies in its entirety, by the absence of contradiction in it despite its length and its parts confirming each other: we do not deny that what he mentioned is among the greatest evidences that it is from a Wise, All-Knowing. That is why the Exalted described it as: 'Falsehood cannot come to it from before it or from behind it' [Fussilat 42]. But the challenge was not made with that.
Source pages p. 585

Commentary
This passage addresses the view that the Qur'an's miraculousness consists in its internal coherence – the absence of contradiction across its length and the mutual confirmation of its parts. The author agrees that this is a powerful proof that the Qur'an is from God (a Wise, All-Knowing), citing the verse from Surah Fussilat: 'Falsehood cannot come to it from before it or from behind it.' However, he points out that this attribute cannot be what the challenge (tahaddi) was based on. Why? Because the challenge was to produce a single surah (or even ten surahs), and coherence across the entire Qur'an is not something that can be demonstrated in just one surah. So while coherence is a sign of divine origin, it is not the specific feature with which the Arabs were challenged.

Key Terms
التناقض
تصديق بعضه بعضاً
الجملة
التحدي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the Qur'an's freedom from contradiction is itself the miracle of the challenge.
Correction: The author clarifies that the challenge was with a single surah, whereas internal coherence is a property of the entire book and cannot be demonstrated in a single surah.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that internal coherence (absence of contradiction) cannot be the basis of the Qur'anic challenge?
A) Because the Qur'an does have contradictions
B) Because the challenge was with a single surah, and coherence is only apparent in the whole
C) Because the Arabs did not understand coherence
D) Because coherence is not a valid proof

Key Takeaway
Internal coherence is a powerful proof of the Qur'an's divine origin, but the challenge (tahaddi) was to produce a single surah, which cannot demonstrate coherence across the entire book.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Qur'an, do you actively look for its internal coherence and consistency, or do you take it for granted? What would change in your relationship with the Book if you made a habit of reflecting on how one verse confirms another?
?
The challenge of producing a single surah was directed at the disbelievers who doubted the Qur'an's origin. Have you ever truly considered the weight of that challenge? If you were asked to produce something like a surah, what would that demand of your own sincerity and humility before Allah?
?
Sanusi affirms that the Qur'an's freedom from contradiction is a powerful proof, yet the challenge was on a different basis. Does this distinction make you more or less convinced of the Qur'an's divine origin? What would it take for you to feel the same awe at the coherence of the whole Book as you might feel at the eloquence of a single surah?

Why Prophecies Are Not the Basis of the Qur'anic Challenge
Sanusi admits prophecies are signs but notes the challenge is with any surah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the miraculousness of the Qur'an cannot be reduced to its prophecies about the unseen, and why the challenge (tahaddi) requires a feature present in every surah.

Core Idea
The Qur'an's miracle must be in every surah, not just those with prophecies.

Sanusi now addresses another proposed theory: that the Qur'an's miraculousness lies in its prophecies about the unseen. He acknowledges that the Qur'an indeed contains true prophecies, but he argues that this cannot be the basis of the challenge because not every surah includes such prophecies. The challenge was to produce any surah, even one without predictions. This forces us to look for a feature that is universal across all surahs.

Guided Path
1
Understand the alternative view
The passage begins with 'As for the one who says...'—identify the view that i'jaz is prophecies about the unseen. Recognize that this is a proposed theory for the Qur'an's miraculousness.
This view is based on the many accurate predictions in the Qur'an.
2
Acknowledge the validity of prophecies
Sanusi does not deny that the Qur'an contains true prophecies. He concedes that they are among the truest signs. Note his concession: 'we also do not deny its inclusion of that, and that it is among the truest of signs.'
He is not dismissing the prophecies, only their role as the basis of the challenge.
3
See the logical flaw
The key point: the challenge (tahaddi) was made with any surah (mutlaq surah), but prophecies are not found in every surah. Therefore, the miraculous quality must be something common to all surahs, such as eloquence and style. This is why prophecies cannot be the basis.
Ask yourself: What quality is present in every surah? Sanusi suggests it is something like eloquence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأَمَّا مَنْ قالَ : إعجازه إنباؤُهُ عن المُغيَّبَاتِ : فلا يُنكَرُ أَيضاً اشتماله على ذلكَ ، وأَنَّهُ مِنْ أصدقِ الآياتِ ، إلا أنَّه لم يقع التحدّي بهِ ؛ إذْ لا تحقق له في كلِّ سورة ، والتحدي وقع بمطلق سورة وإن لم تشتمل على المغيّبات
As for the one who says: its miraculousness is its informing of the unseen: we also do not deny its inclusion of that, and that it is among the truest of signs. But the challenge was not made with it, because it is not realized in every surah, and the challenge was with an absolute surah even if it does not include the unseen matters.
Source pages p. 585

Commentary
This passage examines the view that the Qur'an's miraculousness lies in its prophecies about the unseen (al-mughayyabat). The author acknowledges that the Qur'an indeed contains such prophecies and that they are among the most truthful signs. However, he again argues that the challenge (tahaddi) was not based on this feature. The reason is that not every surah contains prophecies about the unseen. The challenge was to produce any surah, even one without such prophecies. Therefore, the miraculous nature must be something present in every surah, such as eloquence and style, not something limited to certain passages.

Key Terms
إنباؤه
المُغيَّبَات
أصدق الآيات
مطلق سورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qur'an's miracle is primarily in its prophecies about the future.
Correction: While prophecies are true signs, the challenge (tahaddi) applies to every surah, and many surahs contain no prophecies. Therefore, the miraculous quality must be something present in every surah, such as its eloquence and inimitability.

Checkpoint
Why does the author rule out prophecies (informing of the unseen) as the basis of the Qur'anic challenge?
A) Because the prophecies are not true
B) Because not every surah contains prophecies
C) Because the Arabs rejected prophecies
D) Because prophecies are not miraculous

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an's miraculous challenge is tied to every surah, so the basis of i'jaz must be a quality found in all surahs, not just those containing prophecies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite a surah that contains no prophecy, do you still feel its miraculous power? If not, what does that reveal about the basis of your faith? Commit to pondering the linguistic and structural miracle in every surah you read this week.
?
Are there aspects of your Islamic practice that you rely on only because they are 'impressive' (like miracles or prophecies) while neglecting the consistent, everyday miracle of the Qur'an's guidance? Will you shift your focus to appreciate the universal signs in every verse?
?
The challenge of the Qur'an is for any surah. In your life, do you only accept challenges that match your strengths, or are you willing to be tested by the ordinary and the universal? Reflect on how you respond to Allah's commands in all areas, not just the spectacular.
